As she absorbed its contents, she felt a mix of fatigue and intrigue, leading her to ponder Rastignac's bold approach.This moment highlights her complexities—caught between societal expectations and personal desires, the letter challenges her perceptions of propriety and ignites curiosity about Rastignac's intentions.Madame de Listomere decided to forbid her porter from admitting Rastignac, resolving to show disdain for his insolence. Although she initially considered keeping the love letter, she ultimately burned it, surprising her housemaid, Caroline.At a party hosted by the Marquis de Beauseant, Madame de Listomere anticipated Rastignac's arrival. By 2 AM, realizing he would not come, her thoughts underwent a process Stendhal termed "crystallization."Four days later, Rastignac scolded his valet, Joseph, for misplacing letters. Joseph revealed one was for Madame de Listomere, prompting Rastignac to realize he had mistakenly sent her a love letter not meant for her. This blunder led him to reflect on his mistakes, particularly not visiting her sooner, allowing her to dwell on the incident.When Rastignac finally visited, the porter informed him that Madame de Listomere was out, but the marquis invited him in. Upon entering, Rastignac noticed Madame de Listomere blush at his presence, flattering his vanity. The marquis read the newspaper while Madame de Listomere maintained her composure despite the awkwardness.Rastignac attempted to explain the misunderstanding about the letter, but Madame de Listomere, feigning offense, seemed amused. She insisted on forgetting the incident, but Rastignac clarified that no offense had occurred. Their conversation revealed underlying tensions, with Madame de Listomere expressing skepticism about his intentions.As the discussion progressed, Rastignac inadvertently suggested that Madame de Nucingen was the intended recipient of the letter, causing a visible reaction in Madame de Listomere. She dismissed him coldly, pointing out the absurdity of the situation. Feeling foolish, Rastignac left after a few awkward exchanges.Days later, Madame de Listomere withdrew from society, claiming illness, while the marquis attributed her absence to stomach inflammation. In reality, she was experiencing a nervous attack, using it as an excuse to avoid social interactions. The narrative highlights the complexities of social dynamics and misunderstandings in romantic pursuits among the young and inexperienced.

Augustine Guillaume's cousin's tale is worth mentioning. Élodie-Rose, the widow of Maelien, lived a very miserable life. She was born in the same city, Paris, in the same year as Augustine, and grew up happily alongside her, until she got married. She married very early at 16, to a skinny man exactly twice her age with the same hair color and height,  and moved to Albi with him immediately afterward. The last time Augustine and Élodie-Rose saw each other was at Élodie-Rose’s wedding. Élodie-Rose became single again very shortly after and had to continue her life in a town far from her family. She endured a life full of hardship and pain. The six feet tall lady aged very quickly—her dark black hair turned gray, she developed stage 4 glioblastoma, the worst type of brain cancer, and her beautiful hazel eyes grew sad. She lived in Albi for two decades, most of it alone,  and died there a lonely death.


######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In Paris, social gatherings typically fall into two categories: the official party, marked by superficial pleasantries, and a more intimate gathering that follows, characterized by genuine conversation. This second type, often hosted by women like Madame d’Espard and Mademoiselle des Touches, provides a refuge for authentic French wit, contrasting with the cold luxury of the first.Mademoiselle des Touches’ salon is renowned for its grace and freedom of expression, where ideas flow freely. One memorable evening, a group of distinguished individuals, including the charismatic de Marsay, gathered to discuss the evolution of women in France since the July Revolution.The lively conversation featured clever repartee and engaging anecdotes, showcasing the unique blend of wit and intellect that defines Parisian social life. As the night progressed, the atmosphere shifted from formal to relaxed, with guests sharing personal stories and confessions.By around two in the morning, the intimate circle remained at the table, enjoying camaraderie from shared experiences. Mademoiselle des Touches insisted on keeping her guests at the table, believing that moving to the drawing-room would disrupt the enchanting spirit of the gathering.De Marsay, now a prominent politician, recounted a pivotal moment from his youth that shaped his understanding of love and ambition. He described his first love for a captivating widow six years his senior, marked by secrecy and passion, with both parties skillfully concealing their feelings.He reminisced about the intensity of his emotions and the joy of their clandestine meetings, while also acknowledging the jealousy and insecurity that accompanied such a deep attachment. His narrative took a turn when he revealed a moment of betrayal that shattered his idealistic view of love.After a brief illness, he sent her a note to cancel a meeting, fearing she would doubt his sincerity. In a twist of fate, he witnessed her with another man, leading to a profound realization about love and trust. This experience transformed him, instilling disillusionment and prompting reflection on the complexities of relationships.De Marsay articulated a philosophy regarding love and revenge, suggesting that true passion is rare and that the desire for vengeance often stems from believing in the uniqueness of one’s love. His reflections resonated with the group, sparking deeper discussion about love, fidelity, and societal expectations, ultimately serving as a poignant reminder of the fragility of human emotions and the intricate dance between love and power.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Cured of my cold and unrequited love, I embarked on a new adventure with Charlotte, a charming woman whose beauty starkly contrasted with my former love. I was careful not to quarrel with her, as her skilled deception provided pleasures that true love could not match. Seeking an artist in Rue Boucher to create a keepsake from my hair, I discovered he used false hair, leaving me skeptical about love, though I still believed in pleasure.Two months later, I found myself in Charlotte's boudoir, where we shared tender moments. In a passionate moment, I asked if she loved me, and she responded with sweet lies of devotion. However, I interrupted by asking when she would marry the Duke, startling her. I suggested she should marry him, as he was wealthy and virtuous, believing she had been faithful to me due to his scruples. Charlotte feigned innocence, insisting she loved only me, but I pressed her on her relationship with the Duke, revealing my suspicions.As our conversation unfolded, Charlotte's demeanor shifted from playful to desperate. She claimed that if I had spoken sooner, we could have been happy together. I maintained it was too late, and she had gone too far with the Duke. I advised her to reconcile with God, as the Duke awaited proof of her fidelity. In a moment of emotional turmoil, she expressed gratitude for my loyalty, but I sensed her manipulation.Despite lingering feelings of betrayal, I found humor in her belief that I would be left heartbroken. I revealed I had made advances to another woman, sending Charlotte into a panic. She lamented the plight of women, claiming they could never be truly loved. I calmly countered her accusations of infidelity, asserting that love was merely a game for men.Charlotte's anger escalated as she accused me of shattering her illusions about love. I acknowledged her grievances but maintained a submissive attitude, recognizing her need for revenge. My retreat from the situation was calculated, and I felt a sense of control over my emotions.The women present seemed offended by my portrayal of their experiences. Lady Dudley defended Charlotte, arguing that she did not see her marriage as an act of infidelity. De Marsay lamented the decline of social distinctions, noting that duchesses and marquises were disappearing, leaving a diluted version of aristocracy. The conversation turned to the changing nature of women in society, with many lamenting the loss of the great lady archetype.The discussion highlighted how modern women, regardless of social standing, were often reduced to mere shadows of their predecessors. The perfect lady, a product of contemporary society, was characterized by elegance and grace but lacked the depth of the great ladies of the past. The group reflected on how societal changes had transformed women into figures more concerned with appearances than substance.As the conversation continued, it became clear that the ideal of the perfect lady was now a blend of taste and cleverness, ultimately superficial. The essence of femininity had shifted, and the women recognized the challenges they faced in a society that no longer valued the qualities that once defined them. The laughter and camaraderie among the group masked a deeper sense of loss for the elegance and refinement of the past.In discussing women's roles, Blondet noted that bourgeois women often reference their husbands, while upper-class women tend to conceal theirs, highlighting the isolation women experience in social circles. The "perfect lady" captivates with charm but embodies hesitancy and moral ambiguity, reflecting turbulent times.Blondet critiques modern women's intelligence, which often manifests as doubt. He contrasts the virtuous townswoman with the conflicted lady, who struggles with morality and societal expectations. The modern lady's relationship with religion is superficial; she engages in discussions for social cohesion rather than genuine belief.The "perfect lady" emerges from a society caught between hypocrisy and the openness of the past. Her independence is often an illusion, constrained by the fear of losing status or facing peer judgment, making her a figure of compromise.Blondet's analysis extends to female authors, suggesting that without genius, they cannot be true "ladies." This leads to reflections on genius and women's roles, shifting to historical figures like Napoleon, whose character embodies both greatness and contradictions.As the dialogue progresses, characters debate whether women have diminished or retained their greatness. The Princesse de Cadignan argues that while society has changed, the essence of passion and devotion remains constant. The Comte de Vandenesse supports this, asserting that contemporary women possess talents comparable to those of the past.The narrative shifts to a poignant story recounted by General de Montriveau about a disastrous event during the 1812 campaign, illustrating the brutal realities of war. His account starkly contrasts earlier discussions about societal roles, serving as a reminder of the chaos and moral dilemmas faced during conflict. The characters' reflections on love, loss, and human relationships resonate deeply, revealing the complexities of being a woman in their era.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################The story begins with Doctor Monsieur Bianchon sharing a chilling tale about La Grande Bretèche, a dilapidated house near Vendôme, surrounded by a wild garden that hints at its former splendor. The house's decay evokes mystery and melancholy, suggesting a tragic history.Bianchon imagines the house as a refuge for a country gentleman passionate about horticulture. The overgrown garden symbolizes lost joy, while the house's ruin seems cursed, raising questions about its abandonment.During his stay in Vendôme, Bianchon becomes fascinated by the house and often sneaks into the garden, allowing his imagination to create stories of its past glory. One evening, he meets Monsieur Regnault, a local notary, who reveals that the house belongs to the estate of the late Comtesse de Merret. Regnault explains that her will forbids anyone from entering or repairing the house for fifty years after her death, adding to its mystery.Regnault recounts the Comtesse's tragic story. After her husband, the Count de Merret, died, she became reclusive and eccentric, leaving La Grande Bretèche in disarray. Rumors about her mental state circulated. On her deathbed, she summoned Regnault to witness her will, intending to leave her estate to the hospital in Vendôme, with strict conditions regarding the house.The will's peculiar stipulations intrigue Bianchon. Regnault, a former clerk turned notary, describes the Comtesse's decline marked by isolation and illness. Her last moments were filled with urgency to secure her wishes, culminating in her death shortly after finalizing her will.As Bianchon reflects on the Comtesse's life and the fate of La Grande Bretèche, he becomes engrossed in local gossip. His landlady, Madame Lepas, reveals more about the Comtesse and her husband, depicting a once-happy couple whose relationship soured. The Count was known for his temper, while the Comtesse was admired for her beauty.Madame Lepas also shares a secret involving a young Spanish prisoner who stayed at her inn during the war, hinting at possible connections to the Comtesse's tragic fate.The narrative intertwines themes of love, loss, and the passage of time, as Bianchon grapples with the haunting legacy of La Grande Bretèche, prompting readers to ponder the nature of memory and the shadows of the past that shape the present.In a parallel story, a woman recounts the mysterious disappearance of Count Férédia, a Spanish Grandee living in her house. After he fails to return one night, she finds a letter and a significant sum of money and diamonds he left behind, intended for masses of gratitude for his escape. Her husband searches for him and discovers the Count's clothes near the river, suggesting he escaped rather than drowned.The story darkens as the woman suspects the Count's involvement in the death of Madame de Merret, whose crucifix resembled one owned by the Count. She grapples with the moral dilemma of keeping the Count's money, which she feels is rightfully hers.Intrigued by Rosalie, the maid who seems to know secrets about Madame de Merret's death, the narrator seeks the truth. Rosalie reveals that Madame de Merret had been ill, and her husband, Monsieur de Merret, was unaware of her condition until he returned home late one night.That evening, suspecting something was wrong, Monsieur de Merret decided to check on his wife instead of trusting Rosalie. He confronted Madame de Merret, who denied anyone being in the cupboard despite his suspicions. In a tense moment, she swore on a crucifix that no one was there, leading to a tragic outcome.Believing in his wife's purity, Monsieur de Merret chose not to open the cupboard but planned to wall it up with mason Gorenflot. As the wall was built, Madame de Merret attempted to signal to the man inside, indicating hope for his escape.After the wall was completed, Monsieur de Merret confined his wife to her room for twenty days, reminding her of her oath, leaving her in despair. The story concludes with the women at the table reflecting on the moral complexities and emotional turmoil that unfolded within La Grande Bretèche.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In Baronne de Watteville's drawing-room, a central figure in Besançon society, the Archbishop frequently visited due to her devout nature. Monsieur de Watteville, a gentle man from a notorious lineage, married wealthy heiress Mademoiselle de Rupt in 1815, merging their fortunes and leading Madame de Watteville to become increasingly pious.Monsieur de Watteville, a turner by trade, also collected geological specimens, a hobby some found eccentric. Their home, reflecting noble heritage, was filled with fine furnishings and exquisite food, showcasing their social status. Madame de Watteville’s connections with the Archbishop attracted notable clergy to their gatherings.During a ceremonial dinner in September 1834, guests welcomed Monsieur l’Abbe de Grancey, who announced a surprising legal victory in a lawsuit, crediting young lawyer Monsieur Savaron for his success against a formidable Parisian opponent. This revelation intrigued the guests, particularly Madame de Watteville, who inquired about Savaron’s background. The Vicar-General noted that Savaron lived nearby, but his origins were unknown, raising further curiosity and setting the stage for a deeper exploration of his character and events in Besançon.Historically, France and England have influenced each other culturally, especially in language and fashion, often bypassing customs regulations. Styles deemed "English" in Paris are seen as "French" in London, showcasing a mutual fascination. For example, the English national anthem, "God Save the King," was composed by Lulli, while hoop skirts, popular in Paris, were introduced by an Englishwoman but originated from a French invention.Recently, England has added new terms to the French language, such as "dandy" and "lion." The latter, popularized by Alfred de Musset's poetry, describes a flamboyant man and influenced provincial towns like Besançon, where Amedee-Sylvain de Soulas emerged as a local "lion." Amedee, a young man of Spanish descent, sought to elevate his status in a conservative town known for its military culture.Living modestly in a sparsely furnished apartment, Amedee maintained a lavish lifestyle with the help of his stylish servant, Babylas. Despite his extravagant appearance, he struggled financially, relying on a limited income from his father's estate. Perceived as a spendthrift, Amedee cleverly prioritized appearances over substance.His social life revolved around impressing the local aristocracy, particularly Rosalie de Watteville, a sheltered young woman from a wealthy family. Raised in a strict household, Rosalie received limited education focused on religious and domestic skills. At eighteen, she embodied modesty and virtue but harbored a strong spirit beneath her reserved exterior.The dynamic between Amedee and Rosalie was complicated by their social standings and familial expectations. Amedee aspired to marry Rosalie, motivated by her substantial dowry, which he believed would secure his financial future. Meanwhile, Rosalie's mother, Madame de Watteville, was protective, scrutinizing Amedee's intentions and controlling Rosalie's interactions.Despite Amedee's efforts to charm Rosalie and her mother, he faced challenges in gaining their approval. His attempts to present himself as a cultured gentleman were met with skepticism, as Rosalie remained indifferent. The tension was exacerbated by Madame de Watteville's rigid expectations and Rosalie's internal struggles.As Amedee navigated the complexities of social life in Besançon, he became an intriguing figure among the local elite. His fashionable attire and social maneuvers earned him a reputation as the most charming young man in town, yet his true motivations remained obscured. Ultimately, Amedee's pursuit of Rosalie was not just a quest for love but a strategic move to elevate his social standing and secure a prosperous future.In summary, the interplay of fashion, social status, and personal ambition shaped Amedee de Soulas and Rosalie de Watteville's lives in Besançon, reflecting broader themes of aspiration, societal expectations, and the complexities of human relationships in a changing cultural landscape.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur de Soulas recounted the story of Albert Savaron, who moved from Paris to Besançon, rented an apartment, and registered as a barrister. Mademoiselle de Watteville noted Savaron's prestigious lineage, but Amedee de Soulas clarified that he was a common Frenchman who had added a bar-sinister to his name to imply nobility.Initially overlooked by the local legal community, Savaron worked diligently, rising early and keeping to himself, which led to his being forgotten. He attended early mass but remained largely a mystery. Monsieur de Soulas learned about him through his servant and local gossip.Savaron's reputation changed when he successfully defended a mentally challenged man accused of forgery, impressing the court and attracting clients. The Vicar-General, Abbe de Grancey, noted Savaron's striking appearance and eloquence, highlighting his calm demeanor despite a passionate nature. His modest living conditions contrasted with his impressive intellect.Madame de Chavoncourt expressed curiosity about Savaron's motivations for coming to Besançon and his lack of community integration. The Abbe speculated that Savaron would eventually make a significant impact.Rosalie de Watteville, intrigued by Savaron, found him more appealing than her superficial suitors. Determined to meet him, she planned to encounter him at church. After a brief confession with the Abbe, she glimpsed Savaron during mass and felt an intense connection.As Rosalie's fascination grew, she encouraged her father to build a Belvedere in their garden to observe Savaron's private life. Meanwhile, Savaron launched a fortnightly publication, the _Eastern Review_, aimed at promoting Besançon's interests. Rosalie's father subscribed, allowing her to read its contents, including a tale attributed to Savaron, deepening her infatuation with him.In summary, the narrative follows Albert Savaron's arrival in Besançon, his rise through legal and literary achievements, and Rosalie de Watteville's growing affection for him.In July 1823, friends Rodolphe and Leopold traveled to Switzerland to enjoy the beauty of the Four Cantons. While on a boat, Rodolphe became captivated by Fanny Lovelace, who was looking out from a house in Gersau. He decided to stay in Gersau, while Leopold continued their journey.Rodolphe and Leopold had contrasting backgrounds: Leopold was set to become a notary in Paris, while Rodolphe, the illegitimate son of a nobleman, was raised by a devoted mother who sacrificed for his education. Rodolphe was passionate and sensitive, driven by his desires.After breakfast at the Swan Inn, Rodolphe found lodging with the Stopfer couple, who provided him a room with a lake view. He learned that Fanny had moved to Gersau with her elderly father for his health, living modestly as a translator and writer. Rodolphe was enchanted by her beauty and musical talent.Eager to meet Fanny, Rodolphe sought permission to visit the Bergmanns’ gardens, where she lived with her mute servant, Gina. Initially met with suspicion, he eventually gained access. While exploring, he noticed Gina's unusual appearance, suspecting a hidden background.One evening, while listening to Fanny sing, Rodolphe overheard her speaking Italian with Gina, leading him to suspect they were Italian refugees. When he approached them, Gina attacked him, mistaking him for a threat. Fanny and Gina helped him recover, deepening Rodolphe's attraction to Fanny's beauty and kindness.As Rodolphe healed, he learned of his mother’s death, which plunged him into despair. Leopold returned to France, leaving Rodolphe in Gersau, where he found solace in Fanny's company. Their bond deepened as they shared sorrows and dreams. Fanny revealed she was married to an older man, Emilio, but expressed hope for a future together.Rodolphe's feelings for Fanny intensified, admiring her strength and independence. Fanny encouraged him to pursue his ambitions, believing in his potential. Their conversations revealed a shared passion for freedom and liberal ideals.When Fanny received news that her family was safe and would return to Italy, she felt joy, but Rodolphe struggled with jealousy regarding her past and her relationship with Emilio. Despite these challenges, Rodolphe remained devoted to Fanny, vowing to wait for her. Their love blossomed against the stunning Swiss landscape, symbolizing their hopes and the obstacles they faced amid political turmoil and personal sacrifice.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################For twenty days, Rodolphe stayed with the Bergmanns, captivated by Francesca, who embodied youthful imprudence and the complexities of womanhood. He observed her relationship with her husband, Emilio, marked by gratitude and fatherly affection, which intrigued him and drew him closer to her.Their interactions were filled with secret joys and playful quarrels. One evening, Francesca expressed her longing for luxury, likening it to her love for art and beauty. She declined to accompany Rodolphe to Geneva, fearing gossip that could tarnish her husband’s reputation. While professing her love for Rodolphe, she emphasized the need for discretion to protect their bond.The next day, upon learning the Bergmanns had left, Rodolphe departed Gersau for Geneva. He settled in Eaux-Vives and inquired about Italian refugees, particularly a bookseller named Lamporani. His curiosity led him to Villa Diodati, where he encountered Francesca in an elegant carriage, revealing her connection to the aristocratic Colonna family.Rodolphe was struck by the realization of Francesca’s true identity, grappling with feelings of inadequacy for loving a princess disguised as a commoner. He sought an audience with Prince Gandolphini, Francesca’s husband, who welcomed him warmly. Despite his desire to see Francesca, Rodolphe returned to his lodgings, consumed by thoughts of their future.When Rodolphe finally saw Francesca at a gathering, her beauty captivated him. Their eyes met, igniting a profound connection that transcended their surroundings. Francesca’s subtle acknowledgment of their bond amidst the social elite deepened Rodolphe’s feelings, solidifying his love for her.As they spent time together, Francesca encouraged Rodolphe to pursue his ambitions while remaining youthful. Their conversations reflected a shared commitment to love faithfully, despite challenges.Francesca’s background as a well-educated heiress, initially destined for a cloistered life, added depth to her character. Her strategic marriage to Prince Gandolphini contrasted with her revolutionary spirit, aligning with Rodolphe’s aspirations.As spring approached, Rodolphe struggled with longing and anxiety. Francesca’s calm demeanor contrasted with his emotional turmoil, leading him to question her feelings. Tension arose when Rodolphe compared Francesca to a French noblewoman, inadvertently hurting her. Sensing her distress, he sought to comfort her, but she maintained her dignity, instructing him to leave until they could meet again in Naples. This separation marked a pivotal moment in their relationship, highlighting the challenges they faced as lovers from different worlds. Francesca’s strength left a lasting impression on Rodolphe, who was determined to prove himself worthy of her love.Upon returning to Paris, Rodolphe discovered a portrait of Princess Gandolphini by Schinner, who had unexpectedly agreed to paint it. Inspired by Francesca's charm, the artist created an original for Rodolphe and a duplicate for Emilio, leading to regular correspondence. Driven by ambition and love, Rodolphe invested all his resources in a venture that failed, costing him three years. After the Villele ministry fell, he sought solace at the Villa Gandolphini in Naples. Just as he was poised for success, the July 1830 revolution struck, but Rodolphe persevered, supported by love.Meanwhile, Mademoiselle de Watteville was consumed by rage and jealousy over Francesca Colonna, the woman she believed Albert loved. This ignited a fierce desire to win Albert's affection, yet she doubted her worthiness, thinking, “He will never love me!”That night, Rosalie's imagination ran wild with schemes to connect with Albert, from sneaking into his garden to writing him a letter. While gazing at Albert's window, she noticed a suspicious scene involving Jerome, Albert's servant, and Mariette, the maid. This discovery sparked a dangerous curiosity in Rosalie, who realized that a single moment could unravel her mother's strict upbringing.The next day, Rosalie confronted Mariette about her relationship with Jerome, offering her silence in exchange for access to letters Albert sent through Jerome. Mariette agreed, deepening Rosalie's obsession with Albert's life.Rosalie learned from her father that a copy of the _Eastern Review_ was sent to the Duchesse d’Argaiolo in Italy, further fueling her jealousy. She later intercepted a letter from Albert to his friend Leopold, revealing his noble lineage and ambitions, intensifying her infatuation.As Albert's legal career flourished, Rosalie's feelings morphed into a dangerous obsession. She felt compelled to read another letter from Albert to the Duchesse, detailing his hopes and affections for her. The letters revealed Albert's deep love for the Duchesse, leaving Rosalie heartbroken yet determined to respect their bond.Despite her turmoil, Rosalie resolved to step back, believing she should sacrifice her desires for Albert and the Duchesse's happiness, grappling with her feelings of unrequited love and questioning when she would find someone who truly loved her. Ultimately, Rosalie's internal conflict highlighted the complexities of love, jealousy, and the sacrifices made in the name of affection.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In the spring of 1835, nineteen-year-old Rosalie fantasized about overcoming the Duchesse d’Argaiolo, driven by her solitude and secret desires. Meanwhile, Albert de Savarus made political strides in Besançon, gaining support from influential figures, including a wealthy contractor, as he aimed to restore an aqueduct from Arcier, benefiting both his ambitions and the contractor's interests.Savarus, who had successfully represented local priests in lawsuits, concealed his true intentions behind a facade of disinterest. In October 1834, he arranged financing for a merchant, positioning himself for election. The Abbe de Grancey recognized Savarus's potential but noted his reluctance to align with the Wattevilles, fearing it would compromise his neutrality.Determined to introduce Savarus to her social circle, Rosalie planned a trip to les Rouxey, her family estate. There, she learned of her father's legal troubles over land disputes with Riceys and suggested hiring Savarus as their lawyer, believing it would protect her inheritance and strengthen their connection.While trying to keep Savarus in Besançon, Rosalie sought to undermine his political ambitions by informing the Prefect of his Royalist leanings. Despite her efforts, Savarus was nominated as a candidate, and his eloquence during a meeting won the townsfolk's support.However, the political landscape shifted as the Prefect and his allies worked to weaken Savarus's backing. The election process revealed the complexities of local politics, with various factions vying for influence. As the election approached, Savarus faced increasing challenges, creating a tense atmosphere filled with uncertainty.In despair, Savarus reflected on his unrequited love for Francesca, feeling the weight of his ambitions. Just when he felt defeated, the Abbe de Grancey arrived with hopeful news, urging him to connect with the Wattevilles, particularly Madame de Chavoncourt, whose daughter Sidonie could provide crucial support.The Abbe's advice led Savarus to consider a marriage alliance with Sidonie, which would secure the votes he needed to win the election. As he prepared to navigate this new political landscape, he recognized the sacrifices he would need to make for his ambitions, including a potential marriage that could alter his future. Ultimately, the intertwining of personal desires and political aspirations set the stage for a dramatic confrontation between love and ambition.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The following evening, Madame la Baronne de Watteville hosted a gathering of Besançon’s aristocracy to discuss the election, particularly regarding the Rupts' daughter. The former Master of Appeals was to be introduced, with attendees including Madame de Chavoncourt and her daughters. The Abbe de Grancey mingled among the guests, offering sharp political insights.By nine o’clock, Albert de Savarus had not arrived, raising concern. While Madame de Chavoncourt dismissed his delay as trivial, Rosalie, the Baroness's daughter, suspected otherwise. Frustration grew, and the Abbe de Grancey speculated that Albert might be dead. They learned from a servant that he had left in a post-chaise, prompting mixed reactions of shock and relief.As the evening continued, only a few guests remained, including Madame de Chavoncourt and the Abbe de Godenars. The Abbe de Grancey received a letter that visibly affected Rosalie, revealing her feelings for Albert. He deduced she was in love with him and discovered that Albert had withdrawn from the election, leaving Rosalie desperate to keep this news secret to protect her sister Sidonie's prospects.The next morning, Mademoiselle de Watteville learned from Mariette about Albert Savaron's sudden disappearance after a visit from an old gentleman from Paris left him pale and agitated. Following their meeting, Savaron quickly arranged for a journey, leaving behind his keys and instructing his servant to find another job. His departure stirred Besançon, leading to the election of Monsieur de Chavoncourt.As time passed, most forgot Albert, except for a few affected by his absence. Rosalie, aware of the truth about the Duc d’Argaiolo's death, grew troubled. The Baroness de Watteville suggested Rosalie marry Monsieur de Soulas, but she adamantly refused, insisting she was content.Tension escalated during a summer at les Rouxey, where Rosalie's refusal to marry became public. After Mariette and Jerome's marriage, the Baron improved les Rouxey, which Rosalie took pride in, further aggravating her mother.Tragedy struck when the Baron drowned while retrieving a shell from the lake, deepening the rift between mother and daughter. Grief-stricken, Rosalie felt responsible and contemplated confessing her role in Albert's fate, having intercepted letters between him and the Duchess to manipulate their correspondence.The Vicar-General, horrified by Rosalie's actions, urged her to seek redemption. She resolved to write to Albert's friend, Monsieur Leopold Hannequin, to uncover his fate.Hannequin revealed that Albert had renounced the world and entered a monastery, seeking peace after the heartbreak caused by the Duchess's marriage. The Vicar-General wrote to Albert, who confirmed his decision to remain in the monastery, forgiving Rosalie.Years later, Rosalie's mother married Monsieur de Soulas, and Rosalie, now of age, was encouraged to marry. Despite her mother's attempts to control her, Rosalie remained resolute in her feelings for Albert. She confronted the Duchess at a ball, revealing the truth about Albert's letters and their tragic separation, leaving the Duchess shaken. Rosalie returned to Besançon, feeling a sense of closure, marked by loss, regret, and a quest for redemption.Mademoiselle de Watteville, an eccentric figure living alone at les Rouxey, is known for her solitary pursuits like riding and land improvement. Madame de Soulas, a mother of two, reflects on her husband’s aging and the difficulties of marrying a bigot. Mademoiselle de Watteville's quirks include annual visits to the Grande Chartreuse, possibly inspired by her grand-uncle's legacy. After leaving Besançon in 1841 to marry, she returned severely injured from a steamboat accident, losing her right arm and left leg, and now leads a life devoted to religious practices at les Rouxey.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**LETTERS OF TWO BRIDES**  **By Honoré de Balzac**  **Translated by R. S. Scott**  **DEDICATION**  To George Sand, whose friendship has been a source of joy amidst my literary challenges. May this work reflect the esteemed company of great names and noble hearts that accompany my writings.---**LETTERS OF TWO BRIDES****FIRST PART****I. LOUISE DE CHAULIEU TO RENEE DE MAUCOMBE. PARIS, September.**  My dearest Renee, I am free! I hope you have escaped from the convent as well. My release was ordinary; my aunt, fearing for my health, allowed me to leave after your departure left me despondent. Now in Paris, I feel our souls are intertwined after our shared dreams. Without you, I felt trapped in monotony. My aunt cannot understand my longing for life outside the convent.I remember the day I left; my aunt's parting words were both ominous and affectionate. She believes I possess a noble spirit that will guide my passions. Arriving in Paris, I found no one waiting for me, and my heart sank at the emptiness of my new home, which belonged to my late grandmother. The memories overwhelmed me, and I felt a deep connection to her presence in the rooms.Philippe, my grandmother's servant, informed me that my inheritance was intact, but my father had not yet restored the family home. This realization filled me with despair, as I feared I would be left to languish without a marriage arrangement. Yet, as I settled into my rooms, I found comfort in my grandmother's memories and resolved to make the space my own.I began to write to you, longing for our conversations and the details of our lives. I hope you are well and will share everything about your surroundings with me.**October 19th.**  I met my mother for the first time since my return. She greeted me warmly, treating me as an equal rather than a daughter. Her beauty is striking, and I was charmed by her demeanor. She expressed her desire to be a friend rather than a strict mother. My father was equally affectionate, and I felt a sense of belonging for the first time.I am now preparing for my debut into society, and my father has provided me with a generous allowance. I will have a governess, Miss Griffith, to help me navigate this new world. I am excited yet anxious about the changes ahead.**II. THE SAME TO THE SAME November 25th.**  I am beginning to feel at home in my new life. My father has given me a substantial allowance, and I am acquiring new clothes for my social debut. I am eager to shed my convent attire and embrace the elegance of society.Miss Griffith is proving to be a good companion, and I appreciate her guidance. My father has declined a ministerial position, preferring the quieter life of an ambassador. I sense that my family is busy with their own lives, leaving me to discover my own path.I have not yet ventured into the streets of Paris, but I am eager to experience the city. My mother is often out, and I find myself alone with Miss Griffith, which allows me time to reflect on my new circumstances.**III. THE SAME TO THE SAME December.**  I am ready to enter society! After much preparation, I feel confident in my appearance and demeanor. I have acquired a beautiful wardrobe and am excited for my debut. I plan to present myself as charming and innocent, hoping to win the favor of those around me.Despite my excitement, I feel a twinge of regret for my past life in the convent. The transition is overwhelming, and I am aware of the separations that come with social life. My family remains distant, absorbed in their own affairs, while I navigate this new world.I am curious about the allure of society and how it captivates people, keeping them from truly connecting with one another. I hope to uncover the mysteries of my family and the society I am about to enter.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################On December 15th, I drove through the Champs-Elysees and Bois de Boulogne on a beautiful autumn day. Despite my carefully curated appearance, I received little attention from passersby, except for my approving father and my mother, who greeted me with a kiss. Griffith scanned the crowd indifferently while I grew frustrated that one man inspected my carriage without noticing me, realizing that beauty in Paris is more common than I expected.The men I observed were generally unattractive, lacking elegance in their fashion, particularly their hats, which resembled truncated columns. I commented to Miss Griffith about their looks, but she defended them as cultivated.At an evening ball, I stayed close to my mother, who attracted all the attention while I was merely a pretext for compliments. My dress, which I thought stunning, paled in comparison to the married women’s finery. The men at the ball did not impress me; they appeared worn and lacked the proud bearing of their ancestors. One man stood out for his beauty and intellect but seemed self-absorbed, suggesting that intellectuals often prioritize ambition over genuine connections.Determined not to let society's indifference affect me, I focused on enjoying the dance. Upon returning home, I shared my experiences with my mother, who advised me to be discreet about my feelings, highlighting the complexities of feminine duplicity and leaving me uncertain about expressing my joy in dancing.Your letter resonated with me as I reflect on our contrasting lives. You are stepping into a vibrant world, while I seek peace at the Castle of Maucombe, where my room remains unchanged, now with a lovely view of the Gemenos valley.A fortnight after my arrival, my family dined with M. de l'Estorade, a wealthy neighbor whose son was lost during Bonaparte's reign. After evading conscription, he joined the Emperor's bodyguard in 1813 but vanished after the Battle of Leipzig. His father, still hopeful, dreamed of his return. Eventually, the son returned from travels through Russia, Poland, and Germany, aided by the French Minister in Berlin. M. de l'Estorade, a small landowner in Provence, has amassed a fortune of about 250,000 livres, excluding his estate.My parents support a match between me and Louis de l'Estorade, especially since I would be acceptable without a dowry. They devised a plan to navigate the Civil Code, which complicates marriages for noble families. The dinner marked my first meeting with Louis, who, despite his humble background, is now my intended. His modest home reflects years of hardship.The dinner was simple, served on old silverware, and Louis appeared worn and silent, bearing the marks of his struggles. Despite this, I have agreed to marry him, contingent upon my ability to improve his estate and create a park. I have requested water from Maucombe to support my plans. In a month, I will be Mrs. de l'Estorade, and I believe I made a good impression on him.While you prepare for the excitement of Parisian life, I embrace a simpler existence. I am determined to beautify my new home, planting trees and creating a garden for my future children. I envision a life filled with nature and family, free from past turmoil.My future seems straightforward, with no more Bonaparte to disrupt it. If I have children, they will be mine to raise, and I will find joy in their growth. You, dear Louise, will continue to live the romance I once dreamed of, and I ask you to share your adventures with me. Describe your balls, attire, and the men you encounter, as I will be there in spirit.I jest about my new life, but I must embrace this role. My lips remain untouched, and our propriety is amusing. I must conclude my thoughts here.P.S. I have about 1,000 livres to spend; please buy me lovely things unavailable here or in Marseilles. While you pursue your interests, remember the recluse at La Crampade. I will respond to your letter soon.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################**Summary of Correspondence Between Don Felipe Henarez and Don Fernand Paris, and Letters from Louise de Chaulieu to Renee de Maucombe****I. Don Felipe Henarez to Don Fernand Paris (September)**In this letter, Don Felipe reassures his brother, Don Fernand, of his safety amid Spain's political turmoil. He reflects on the massacre of their ancestors that forced them into a life of cunning survival. Despite Ferdinand's assurances of protection, Felipe remains skeptical, believing true Spaniards do not need to repeat promises.Felipe recounts his escape from Spain, aided by brigands who once feared him and now protect him from the King’s vendetta. He laments the disrepair of his estates, which require significant investment to be profitable. The sight of Spain’s decline brings him sorrow, and he expresses a sense of martyrdom, feeling trapped between life and death, unable to fight for his country or pursue love.He outlines his last wishes, urging Fernand to claim his titles and properties from the King, using his love for Marie Heredia as leverage. Felipe acknowledges the mutual affection between Fernand and Marie, encouraging their union while resigning himself to a life of obscurity. He plans to keep his mother’s diamonds for independence and offers Fernand a wedding gift from his meager savings. Felipe concludes with a sense of finality, preparing to embrace a life as the Baron de Macumer, away from Spain's chaos.**II. Don Felipe Henarez to Don Fernand Paris (October)**Felipe reflects on his current state, having arrived in France with little money. He describes his modest life, teaching Spanish and living simply. Despite his resignation, he finds solace in his new routine, enjoying nature and solitude. He expresses no regrets about his abdication, feeling detached from his former status and wealth.Felipe opens up about his emotional state, revealing unexpressed tenderness and devotion, which he channels into his love for Spain. He questions his existence and laments the loss of his heritage. He finds joy in Fernand and Marie’s love, hoping they will cherish each other and fulfill the dreams he could not.He reflects on their mother’s hopes for Fernand, believing she would be pleased with the union. Felipe urges Fernand to comply with his wishes and keep his identity as Henarez a secret from Marie, emphasizing the importance of preserving their family’s legacy.**III. Louise de Chaulieu to Renee de Maucombe (First Letter)**Louise expresses disbelief at Renee’s quick engagement to a stranger, warning her against the constraints of marriage and urging her to embrace life in Paris instead. She describes her own experiences in society, filled with excitement but also disillusionment with the men she encounters, finding them lacking in depth.Louise shares her father’s plans to represent the King in Madrid, hinting at potential marriage prospects for herself. She critiques the superficiality of social interactions, noting the brutality underlying polite behavior. Despite her observations, she remains hopeful for love, longing for a connection that transcends the mundane.**IV. Louise de Chaulieu to Renee de Maucombe (Second Letter, January)**Louise writes about a refugee, Don Henarez, who has captured her interest. She describes his dignified yet humble demeanor, contrasting it with the superficiality of her social circle. Despite his taciturn nature, she senses a depth of character in him. Their interactions reveal a complex dynamic as she navigates her feelings and societal expectations.Louise reflects on her father’s plans to return to Spain and considers the implications for her future. She grapples with her emotions, recognizing the rarity of genuine affection and the challenges of finding true love. Her correspondence reveals her internal struggle between societal norms and her desire for a meaningful connection. This summary condenses the original text while retaining the essential themes of political turmoil, personal sacrifice, and the complexities of love and societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Mlle. de Chaulieu expresses concern for her friend Renee's upcoming marriage, fearing it will stifle her spirit and lead to disillusionment. She hopes Renee will seek true passion instead of conforming to a conventional life.In a Monday update, Mlle. de Chaulieu shares her fascination with M. Henarez, a melancholic Spanish teacher whose mysterious nature intrigues her. Despite their social differences, she is drawn to him, contrasting his coldness with her boldness. She reflects on love, suggesting that true passion should exist outside of marriage.Mme. de L'Estorade warns Mlle. de Chaulieu against pursuing M. Henarez, labeling him a dangerous adventurer. She expresses concern for her friend's well-being and hints at her own marital struggles, promising to share more when she feels less anxious.In February, Mlle. de Chaulieu recounts a serious discussion with her father about his political ambitions and France's instability. He seeks her support in using her inheritance to secure a future for her brother, highlighting the tension between personal desires and family obligations.In a Friday update, Mlle. de Chaulieu reflects on her growing feelings for M. Henarez, despite initial hesitations. She describes his charm and intellect, noting how he has transformed during their lessons, deepening her attraction.Mme. de L'Estorade shares her marriage experiences, emphasizing the differences between being single and married. She discusses her decision to marry Louis de L'Estorade, focusing on the importance of friendship and independence while navigating the complexities of marriage.The Duc de Soria urges his brother to accept financial support from their family, expressing hope for his happiness and emphasizing the importance of family bonds despite current struggles.Mlle. de Chaulieu critiques Mme. de L'Estorade's pragmatic views on marriage, arguing that love should be the guiding principle. She expresses disdain for calculated love, insisting on the importance of passion over reason. Mlle. de Chaulieu reflects on her feelings for M. Henarez, acknowledging the joy and turmoil of love.In a Monday update, Mlle. de Chaulieu describes a romantic encounter with M. Henarez, who has been watching her from afar. She feels excitement and apprehension, ultimately deciding to burn a letter he sent her, symbolizing her struggle with her feelings.Mlle. de Chaulieu prepares for a significant evening, wearing white camellias as a symbol of her feelings for M. Henarez. She reflects on love's power and the simplicity of expressing emotions through flowers. The evening culminates in a moment of connection with M. Henarez, deepening her resolve to embrace her feelings.Mlle. de Chaulieu shares her excitement about a new carriage and her growing affection for M. Henarez. She observes his efforts to impress her and reflects on their relationship, feeling empowered by her love. She acknowledges the beauty of love in Paris and the joy it brings to her life.Mme. de L'Estorade responds to Mlle. de Chaulieu's romantic ideals, emphasizing the seriousness of marriage and the need for practicality. She argues that love must be balanced with responsibility, noting that women often navigate complex social laws. Mme. de L'Estorade reflects on her marriage, asserting that happiness can be achieved through careful management of emotions and relationships.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Dear Renee, you are remarkable, and I see that we can only be virtuous through deception. The man who loves us is ours to shape, though we often make fools of them. You aim to inspire ambition in yours while keeping him in love, but isn't love alone enough?Without an afterlife, your self-sacrifice would be tragic. You want to maintain his ambition and love, but where is the line between virtue and calculation? Despite my deceit, I confess my love for Felipe, which I hide behind a mask of hypocrisy. I long for him to enter my life, yet I would scorn him if he did.What prevents me from revealing my happiness to Felipe? His love is pure and overwhelming, yet I feel a strange force pulling me toward him, despite my better judgment. I find myself more attuned to your feelings as I navigate this emotional turmoil.Felipe's happiness seems tied to his devotion to me, but his respectful distance frustrates me. I want to shout that if he loves me as an image, he will be even more captivated by the real me.Oh, Renee, please burn my letters, as I will yours. If anyone else were to read our intimate thoughts, I would have Felipe silence them permanently.On Monday, I ponder the complexities of the heart. I wish my father would introduce me to M. Bonald for answers. Does Felipe still worship me? If I sense any change in his respect, I would have the strength to walk away.My father had my portrait painted by Mme. de Mirbel, and I sent the original to Felipe with a note suggesting both promise and challenge. If he addresses me affectionately in his reply, he is lost.On Tuesday, I received his response, and it was perfect. He described how every moment away from me was filled with thoughts of my image, expressing his deep happiness. He placed me on a pedestal, equating my presence with divine grace. His words revealed his vulnerability and longing, filling me with joy.The weather has been dreadful, preventing me from going out without raising suspicions.On Wednesday evening, I saw Felipe at the Opera, and he seemed transformed. He approached our box, his nervousness palpable. His eyes sparkled with newfound light, and I felt a rush of joy. Others noticed his change, commenting on his love, which elated me.I yearn for him to overcome obstacles and boldly ask for my hand, so I can gauge the depth of his love. As I wrote, I saw him climbing along the wall outside my window. When he leaped down and ran to show me he was unharmed, I was overwhelmed with emotion.I am left wondering what he wanted to say. I will go to bed joyful, dreaming of our conversations. Farewell, dear friend. I have not scolded you for your silence, but it has been over a month since I heard from you. Are you finally happy? Have you given up your cherished independence?############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In her letter, Renee expresses dissatisfaction with marriage, questioning why love is often overlooked by society and philosophers. She feels that marriage imposes a discordant obligation on women, forcing them into subservience for family. Renee reflects on her transformation from an individual to a possession, lamenting the emotional sacrifices she has made. She longs for a love that transcends societal expectations and hopes for fulfillment through motherhood.Renee observes that her husband, Louis, remains blissfully unaware of her struggles, enjoying the benefits of her sacrifices without recognizing the effort behind them. She yearns for a child to channel her energy and emotions, believing motherhood could bring her joy.In response, Louise shares her turmoil as she contemplates a secret meeting with Felipe, a man she desires. Torn between societal norms and her instincts, she wants to test Felipe's character and commitment. Driven by a longing for adventure, Louise plans a clandestine encounter under the lime trees in her garden, hoping to explore their feelings away from prying eyes.As she prepares for the meeting, Louise reflects on the thrill and anxiety of the situation, wishing for Renee's company to share in the excitement. When the moment arrives, she meets Felipe, and they engage in a charged conversation filled with unspoken emotions. Louise expresses concerns about the risks of their relationship, while Felipe, overwhelmed with joy, vows to prove his worthiness.Their exchange reveals a deepening connection, with Louise acknowledging the complexities of love and the balance of power within relationships. Ultimately, both women navigate the intricate landscape of love, duty, and personal fulfillment, seeking to reconcile their aspirations with societal realities.In her correspondence with Felipe, Louise expresses disappointment, stating that if he does not find Racine's _Berenice_ deeply moving, they lack a true connection and should part ways. She criticizes his self-satisfied demeanor at Mme. d'Espard's, contrasting it with the humble lover he once was. Louise feels that if Felipe does not see her as unique and noble, she must be less than a woman in his eyes, breeding mistrust.She reflects on love's nature, noting that betrayals often go unpunished. Louise declares she will not forgive serious transgressions in love, asserting that any sign of diminished passion from Felipe would lead her to despair. She warns that her suffering will be evident, ensuring he feels the weight of her loss.Louise implores Felipe not to make her envy her past self, who was blissfully loved. Doubt has pierced her heart, and if he cannot meet her emotional needs, she prefers he leave. She values his intellect and character, believing they are treasures meant for her, desiring him to embody the noble qualities of a bygone Spanish grandee while hoping for a deeper connection.Felipe expresses deep remorse for upsetting Louise, interpreting her jealousy as love. He reflects on his vulnerability compared to his past confidence and longs for a closer connection. The emotional distance causes him pain, and he fears she no longer loves him. However, her letter brings him joy and hope. He likens himself to a child seeking forgiveness, emphasizing his humility and commitment, and implores her to keep him bound to her, promising to be her devoted servant.Louise shares her experiences with Felipe, revealing her desire to test his love. During a garden walk, she observes his unwavering loyalty, describing him as a true Spaniard willing to risk everything for her. She hints at societal pressures regarding her father's marriage plans. At the opera, she appreciates Felipe's calm demeanor amidst rumors about her potential marriage, signaling him to her window for an intimate moment. Louise declares her love for Felipe and suggests marriage, emphasizing her commitment despite societal constraints.Later, she recounts a conversation with her parents about her impending marriage, where her mother offers advice on the realities of married life. Louise acknowledges her mother's wisdom and the importance of perseverance in marriage, recognizing the challenges ahead.Renee responds to Louise's romantic experiences with a contrasting account of her mundane life with her husband, Louis. She reflects on the monotony compared to Louise's vibrant love life and expresses concern over the romantic ideals Louise holds, cautioning her about potential disillusionment after marriage. Renee emphasizes self-sacrifice and resignation in married life, warning that love's passion may not endure. Despite her worries, she expresses a desire to meet Felipe and share in Louise's happiness.In a later letter, Louise shares the joy of her engagement to Felipe, who has graciously accepted her family's wishes regarding her fortune. She reflects on her mother's supportive role and the love she feels for Felipe, despite his physical appearance. Louise acknowledges the complexities of love and marriage, recognizing the need for patience and understanding in their relationship. As the wedding approaches, she feels a mix of excitement and anxiety, describing preparations and gifts from Felipe's family. Louise expresses her commitment to Felipe and her anticipation of their future together, affirming her identity as Louise de Macumer while cherishing her past as Louise de Chaulieu.Through these letters, themes of love, loyalty, societal expectations, and the transition from maidenhood to womanhood are explored, highlighting the emotional depth and complexity of relationships during this period.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Dear friend, it has been nearly eight months since our marriage, and your silence is inexcusable.We traveled to the Castle of Chantepleurs, purchased by Macumer in Nivernais, sixty leagues from Paris. Our servants arrived ahead, and we reached the castle the following evening. I slept during the journey, comforted by Felipe's tender presence. His affectionate gestures deeply touched me, and I awoke to find his passionate gaze unchanged. Despite his calm demeanor, he later revealed intense emotions he had kept hidden.As we settled into our new life, I found that marriage was not as daunting as I expected. Felipe reassured me of his fidelity, and I felt a mix of joy and complexity in love. I began to understand the depth of my feelings and our bond.I realized that true happiness in marriage is often unspoken, a sacred experience that cannot be easily shared. Duty seems to overshadow the natural expression of love, which flourishes freely. I admire your strength, Renee, and see that those who marry for love, like me, experience a different reality than those who marry for convenience, like you.In my reflections, I discerned that only truly great men know how to love deeply. Felipe embodies this greatness; he is my other half, and our connection grows stronger with time. My joy is profound, and I feel as if I am living in a dream, far removed from the fears my mother instilled in me about marriage.We spent seven and a half blissful months at Chantepleurs, akin to lovers escaping the world. Yet, I often thought of you and your more pragmatic marriage. Our experiences feel worlds apart; I am elevated by love, while you navigate the earthly realm of duty.Now, we are in Paris, enjoying a lovely home in the Rue du Bac. I feel more beautiful and content than ever, relishing the music of Rossini that once soothed my restless spirit.Renee, my happiness compels me to reach out. I see you as noble and admirable, and I wish to understand your heart. I suspect you hide your own wounds. I once thought your marriage was merely an experiment, but I now recognize that true happiness transcends societal expectations.I long for you to find joy with Louis, who adores you. I hope love has illuminated your life as it has mine. Remember our youthful vows to share burdens? I now pray for your happiness as fervently as you once prayed for mine.I have an idea: encourage Louis to pursue a political career. If he becomes a deputy, we can be together in Paris, and perhaps he will gain a title. This could be a new beginning for both of us.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################My dear Louise, your letter brought me joy and a hint of envy. Sitting on my rock overlooking the Mediterranean, where I’ve nurtured climbing plants, I envisioned a future filled with life and laughter. In this moment, I felt the stirrings of motherhood within me, a sensation that brought joy and healing to my soul. This realization has transformed my understanding of sacrifice into a source of strength.While you experience love through Felipe, my love manifests in nurturing my future children. Although I feel disconnected from the excitement of my pregnancy, I recognize that motherhood is a unique journey for each woman. My husband, Louis, shares in the anticipation, yet I remain emotionally detached, viewing this phase more as an imaginative concept than a heartfelt reality.I grapple with strange cravings, particularly for rotten fruit, which I consume despite their unpleasantness. This peculiar desire alleviates my discomfort, suggesting a natural process at work. I wonder when the true sense of motherhood will awaken within me.As I conclude this letter, I urge you to stay humble amidst your happiness. Your love for Felipe seems to challenge fate, and I worry that such joy may attract misfortune. Remember to keep faith and practice kindness, as I have come to value spirituality more since my marriage. I cherish our friendship and the connection we share. God bless you, my dear friend!Madame,We are thrilled to announce that Renee has given birth to a healthy boy, Armand-Louis de l'Estorade, whom we plan to baptize upon your return to Chantepleurs. We hope you will be his godmother.Renee showed remarkable patience during childbirth and is now safe, with the physician praising her as an excellent nurse. Little Armand resembles her, which brings us great joy.Renee has prepared rooms for your visit, and we look forward to welcoming you. My father, now reconciled to his sacrifices, has purchased two estates, enhancing La Crampade's value. Your assistance in obtaining a title for him would greatly benefit our son.I am diligently specializing in my field, and your interest in Armand would encourage me immensely. We eagerly await your visit.Yours sincerely,  LOUIS DE L'ESTORADE.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In January 1826, Louise de Macumer writes to her friend Renee de l'Estorade, eagerly anticipating her visit to Chantepleurs in April. She is excited to be the godmother of Renee's first child and insists that her husband, Felipe, must be the godfather. Louise reflects on their differing lives—Renee as a mother and herself as childless—but both find joy in their roles. While she desires motherhood, she fears jealousy towards Renee's child.Louise describes her vibrant life with Felipe, noting that society views her as more beautiful since their marriage. She longs for intense love, even contemplating dying young to preserve her happiness. Their first quarrel arises from her wish for Felipe to end her life if she reaches thirty, which he refuses, revealing his youthful innocence.Louise and Felipe want to send Renee a meaningful gift for daily use, considering a special luncheon service adorned with baby figures to remind her of them.In response, Renee writes about her experiences as a new mother nearly five months after giving birth to her son, Armand. She apologizes for her delayed reply due to motherhood's demands. Renee recounts her emotional journey during childbirth, expressing immense gratitude and joy at becoming a mother. She contrasts her overwhelming love for her child with her initial fears during pregnancy.Renee shares the transformative experience of nursing, highlighting the deep bond it creates. She finds joy in simple moments with Armand, noting that her feelings for her husband, Louis, have shifted from romantic love to profound gratitude. The support from her family has increased, giving her renewed purpose and happiness.Renee reflects on motherhood, asserting it brings fulfillment and a sense of duty that transcends romantic love. She encourages Louise to embrace her future as a mother, believing it will enhance her happiness. As she concludes her letter, she expresses joy and urges Louise to write back, sharing in her happiness and love. The letters capture the contrasting yet complementary experiences of two friends navigating love, motherhood, and evolving relationships.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################**Summary of Correspondence Between Mme. de Macumer and Mme. de L'Estorade (March - July 1826)****Mme. de Macumer to Mme. de L'Estorade (March 1826)**  It has been over three months since my last letter, and I feel guilty for the delay. Felipe and I interpreted your silence as consent regarding the baby-wreathed luncheon service, which is now on its way to Marseilles after six months of planning. I miss our exchanges deeply. Life in Paris is overwhelming; society consumes all sentiment and time, leaving little room for genuine connection. Molière's character Celimene reflects this societal pressure, and I often feel that my love for Felipe is my only protection against it.My social life is busy; I host gatherings and have become fashionable, which pleases Felipe. My parents have gone to Madrid, and my brother is set to marry an heiress, securing our family's legacy. Society's initial criticism of my marriage has turned into admiration, and I now enjoy a position of influence. However, the constant attention and flattery can be intoxicating, leading me to understand why women may become selfish and frivolous in such an environment.I long for a true friend and cherish Felipe's love. We are preparing to move to Chantepleurs for a brief respite from Parisian life. I look forward to seeing you again in May after two years apart, both of us now in our roles as matrons.**Mme. de L'Estorade to Mme. de Macumer**  Dear friend, motherhood has consumed my time, making it difficult to write. I am fully dedicated to caring for my son, Armand-Louis, and have taken on all responsibilities myself. My life is rich and fulfilling, and I eagerly await your visit to share this joy. I hope you will find my home welcoming, despite the challenges of a teething baby.**Mme. de Macumer to Mme. de L'Estorade (April 1826)**  I am pleased to inform you that your father-in-law is now Comte de l'Estorade, as I made sure to secure this before leaving Paris.**Mme. de Macumer to Mme. de L'Estorade (July 1826)**  I must confess that I left abruptly due to jealousy. Felipe's attention to you during our visit was difficult for me to bear. Your beauty and joy of motherhood reminded me of my insecurities, and I found myself envious of the lively atmosphere in your home.Despite my jealousy, I recognize your happiness with L'Estorade, who seems to be a respectable man. I am heading back to Chantepleurs, hoping to find fulfillment in my own life. I know I am being foolish, but I needed to escape the discomfort I felt in your presence.**Mme. de L'Estorade to Mme. de Macumer**  Your sudden departure surprised us all, and I was taken aback by your letter. You had no reason to feel jealous; our conversations were innocent and centered on your happiness. I urge you to return, as your husband respects you deeply.I have observed that you do not truly love Felipe as a husband; your relationship lacks the depth of true partnership. You may find that your adoration will fade, leading to resentment over the imbalance in your relationship. A woman’s happiness often depends on her partner's respect and authority, and I fear you risk diminishing Felipe's sense of self.Remember that genuine love requires a balance of power. If you continue to dominate the relationship, you may end up feeling unfulfilled. I encourage you to embrace your role as a supportive wife, allowing Felipe to maintain his dignity.Please consider my words carefully. You have the potential for true happiness; do not squander it. Return to Paris when Felipe does, as the demands of society can help you both grow.Farewell, my dear friend. I hope you find peace and clarity in your heart.My beloved, I’m thrilled to be in Italy with Macumer, whose Sardinia plans are on hold. This enchanting country, especially its churches, could convert any Protestant. Macumer is well-received here, and I’m favored at court. We’ll soon be in Florence, then spend a month in Tuscany and Naples before reaching Rome in November. Our journey feels like a honeymoon, revitalizing our passion. Apologies for not writing more; I’m fully immersed in this experience.The Vicomtesse de L'Estorade writes to the Baronne de Macumer about a reply waiting for her at Chantepleurs. She expresses concern over the government's impending dissolution, which may hinder the Crown's power consolidation. Her father will stand for election before resigning and shares joy over her godson's upcoming weaning in December, concluding with affectionate remarks about the Baronne's influence in Italy.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################I received your distressing letter, forwarded by my steward. Renee, I won’t dwell on my hurt feelings but will share the impact of your words.We had just returned from a lovely reception hosted by the ambassador, where I felt radiant, and Macumer was swept away in love. Upon reading your harsh response, I sobbed, risking my composure. Felipe, my dear Arab, declared you were mistaken. He took me to the balcony, professing his love in Italian, insisting that even if your predictions were true, he would not trade our moments together for a mundane existence with children.While he whispered sweet nothings, a bat's cry startled me, and I fainted. But I am well now. This morning, I knelt before Felipe, confessing my fears and asking him to guide me if I ever strayed from his desires. Your letter frightened me, for you are a true friend.Thank you, Renee. Your words revealed the depth of Macumer's love. Rome is the city of love, where passion thrives alongside art and faith.We will soon meet the Duc and Duchesse de Soria in Venice. If you write, please address it to Paris, as we leave Rome shortly.P.S. Your letter shows you know little of love beyond theory. Love produces diverse outcomes, defying any single explanation.---My father has been elected to the Chamber, my father-in-law has passed away, and I am nearing my second confinement. I mention these events to avoid alarming you with the black seal.Your letter from Rome unsettled me. You and Felipe seem like children. His love for you may resemble a man’s affection for a courtesan—lavish yet knowing she is unfaithful. I draw a harsh lesson from our differing fates: do not love if you wish to be loved.Louis received the cross of the Legion of Honor upon his election to the provincial Council nearly three years ago. My father has requested the rank of Officer of the Legion for him, and I hope you can assist in this matter without entangling yourself in my father's ambitions.---Ah, Louise, I have endured hell. If I recount my anguish, it is because you are like a sister to me. Five days of torment felt like centuries. A mother who has not experienced this suffering cannot comprehend it.The night before the crisis, the weather was close, and my son Armand was unusually irritable. I attributed it to teething. In the morning, he cried for water, but his voice was strange, and he became stiff. I called for Louis, fearing he was dying. The doctors arrived, but their opinions varied. They suggested leeches and ice, which felt like death to me.For an hour, I held my lifeless child, watching in horror as he convulsed. I felt utterly helpless, and my mother was far away. After four days of fear, the doctors recommended a painful ointment. I refused, trusting nature instead. In a moment of desperation, I held Armand close, praying for his life. Miraculously, he began to recover, but I was left utterly drained.Now, as I write, Armand is playing, but I remain troubled by the cause of his illness. Is it teething or a nervous issue? I fear for my unborn child. The agony of seeing a child in convulsions is indescribable.---Dear Renee, Macumer and I forgave your earlier naughtiness upon hearing of your distress. Your double agony resonated with me, and I see some compensation in being childless.I write to inform you that Louis has been promoted to Officer of the Legion. You are fortunate, and perhaps you will have a daughter, as you wished.My brother married Mlle. de Mortsauf upon our return. The King has granted him the reversion of a prestigious post, contingent on a family emblem arrangement.My parents visited for the wedding and will return to Madrid after my reception tomorrow. The carnival will be lively, especially with the Duc and Duchesse de Soria in Paris, which makes me uneasy. I am lavish with affection towards Felipe to keep his attention away from Marie Heredia, who is stunning but lacks charm.I plan to retreat to Chantepleurs after March to economize, as our Italian tour was costly. Felipe and I long for the tranquility of the countryside after the extravagance of Italy. We look forward to your visit, and I assure you, I will not be jealous. You are free to explore Macumer's heart. Since our time in Rome, his love for me has deepened.With love, dear friend.My daughter, Jeanne-Athenais, is two months old, with my mother and Louis' grand-uncle as her godparents. I plan to visit Chantepleurs soon, as you’re not worried about a nursing mother. Your godson can now say your name, calling it _Matoumer_. He’s growing quickly, but I worry about him while I recover from childbirth, as the doctors have advised special precautions. My father noticed Felipe looks thin, and I sense something is troubling you, as your letter lacks warmth. My nurse is upset with me for writing, and Mlle. Athenais is hungry. Please write me a long letter.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################**Summary of Correspondence Between Mme. de Macumer and the Comtesse de L'Estorade****Letter XLIII: Mme. de Macumer to the Comtesse de L'Estorade**For the first time, I find myself alone and crying under a willow by the Chantepleurs marsh. Married for nearly three years without a child, I yearn for motherhood, imagining the joy of a child like your Athenais. Each disappointment deepens my melancholy, prompting me to compose elegies and dream of motherhood—embroidering caps, choosing baby clothes, and hearing "Mamma" from my own child. My home feels desolate without children, and I feel unnatural as a childless woman. I long to express my sorrow, and even my Moor was moved to tears by my plight. I sometimes feel the urge to go on a pilgrimage for solace. Next winter, I plan to consult a doctor about my situation.**Letter XLIV: The Same to the Same, Paris, 1829**A year has passed without a letter from you, which pains me. Your husband Louis visits often, but I miss your thoughts and feelings. Your seclusion in the country during a time when you could enjoy Paris and your husband's political success worries me. I wonder if you are too vain to show yourself in Paris while pregnant. I hope you will come soon, as I want to know more about your life and family.**Letter XLV: Renee to Louise**You complain of my silence, but my children keep me busy. I didn’t want to bring them to Paris while expecting another child. I plan to buy a house in Paris with money from Louis' uncle and my mother.
