I appreciate your help with Louis' career, but I prefer he takes a stable position rather than a prominent one. My days revolve around caring for my children. Their health dictates my time, and I find joy in their sleep, planning their futures. Nights are filled with worries about their safety, and mornings bring the chaos of getting them ready. I cherish moments of play and laughter, despite the challenges of motherhood.Motherhood requires self-denial and devotion. I cannot trust anyone else to care for my children as I do. My life revolves around their needs, often at the expense of my own.**Letter XLVI: Mme. de Macumer to the Comtesse de L'Estorade, 1829**My dear Renee, I am overwhelmed with grief after losing Felipe. I stayed by his side until the end, and now I am left alone, haunted by memories. I feel guilty for his suffering, and the pain of his absence is unbearable. I long to see him again, even for a moment.I am in the country, surrounded by reminders of him, struggling to find joy in my surroundings. My family is away, but the Duc and Duchesse de Soria have been supportive. Felipe's last moments were filled with love for me, and I regret the jealousy and demands I placed on him. Now, I live in a haze of sorrow, finding solace in nature but feeling the weight of my loss. I mourn the absence of a child from our love, questioning if such passion is destined to be barren. I invite you to visit me, as your presence would bring comfort. I need you now more than ever to help me bear this heavy burden.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Dear Renee,By the time you read this, I will be near you. Louis must stay in Provence for the elections, as the Liberals are plotting against him. I come not to comfort but to share my heart. Weep, for tears bring joy in reuniting with him. Each duty done brings you closer. In my arms, you will rise, pure and noble, ready to do good in his name. I write amidst preparations, often interrupted by the children, especially Armand, who longs to see you.I have sold my house and properties, but I assure you, I am neither mad nor ruined. After settling everything, I have about twelve hundred thousand francs left. I invested a million in the Three per Cents at fifty, yielding an income of sixty thousand francs, which is far better than the thirty thousand from my properties. Managing farms was overwhelming for a widow of twenty-seven. Now, I receive my income directly from the state, which is simpler and more secure.You may wonder about the risk of national bankruptcy, but I believe it’s unlikely, and even if it happened, I would still be financially stable. My friend, the Comte de l'Estorade, reassures me of my decision.The real reason for my actions is love. I am to be married soon in a secret ceremony near Paris. I love deeply and am loved in return. My beloved, Marie Gaston, has been courting me for nearly two years. He is twenty-three and has debts of thirty thousand francs, which I have paid off. He is a poet struggling to make a name for himself, and I admire his passion and talent.Despite our age difference and his financial struggles, I am ready to end our long period of restraint. This is not a naive surrender; I am confident in my feelings and his. I have never met a man with such noble feelings, and I am excited about our future together.Gaston is the illegitimate son of Lady Brandon, who died tragically. He was raised by his brother Louis, who has since disappeared. Gaston has faced many hardships, believing that genius would lead him to success, but he has only accumulated more debt. I felt an immediate connection when we met at a gathering, though he is unaware of the depth of my love for him.I am certain of my happiness in marriage, but I also fear the potential for loss. I worry about aging and not meeting his expectations. If our love were to fade, I would take the blame entirely upon myself. I ask you, should that happen, to care for my children as my legacy.I plan to keep our marriage private, away from prying eyes. I have purchased a secluded property near Ville d'Avray, complete with a lake and gardens, designed for our solitude. The chalet is beautifully constructed and hidden from view, ensuring our privacy.I have arranged for a small, trustworthy staff to help maintain our home, and I have moved my cherished belongings there. The tranquility of the valley is perfect for our life together, and I hope it will nurture our love.As we prepare for our life together, I intend to invest in my appearance to keep the romance alive. I will spend significantly on my wardrobe, ensuring I remain beautiful for Gaston. I want to be a devoted partner, learning from past mistakes.In two days, I will become Madame Gaston. I am filled with both joy and sorrow, knowing that my love for Gaston is immense, yet I will miss you dearly. Please address future correspondence to me as Madame Gaston, Poste Restante, Versailles, as I wish to keep our lives a secret from the outside world.Farewell, my dear friend. The pain of losing you is as profound as my love for Gaston.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################**Summary of Correspondence Between Marie Gaston and Daniel D'Arthez, Mme. de L'Estorade and Mme. de Macumer, and the Comtesse de L'Estorade to Mme. Marie Gaston****Marie Gaston to Daniel D'Arthez (October 1833)**Dear Daniel,I need you and Joseph Bridau as witnesses for my wedding tomorrow. My future wife prefers seclusion, which is why we’ve seen little of each other. After the wedding, we will be apart for a while, but I trust our friendship can endure this absence. She has sacrificed her closeness with a childhood friend for me, and I must do the same.Our love is profound and genuine, built on mutual understanding. However, I feel a burden of shame; she is wealthy, while I am not. Your support has been crucial during my struggles, and I feel humiliated by our circumstances. This shame reflects my love, as I willingly accept it.Despite this, my dreams are fulfilled. She is noble, intelligent, and beautiful, embodying the ideal woman. At twenty-seven, she is a widow without children, possessing both energy and moments of melancholy. Our love is pure and has flourished without succumbing to physical desire, reflecting our mutual respect. We have both faced disappointments, and she acknowledges her previous marriage as a mistake.You, my friend, are a giant among men, and I hope you can appreciate the happiness that awaits me.**Mme. de L'Estorade to Mme. de Macumer**Louise, are you truly planning a life of solitude after your first marriage? You seem to be inviting misery upon yourself. It appears you have found someone who has changed your mind about remarrying, but have you forgotten your previous criticisms of men?I cannot blame you for seeking happiness, though I fear for you. Absolute happiness often invites disaster, and I sense your joy may be too heavy to bear. I hope you will share more about this man you love.I have not yet told Louis about your marriage plans, as it would only invite his ridicule. You are brave to face winter alone, but I will support you as your discreet friend. Your journey into this emotional wilderness is fraught with danger, yet I wish you a safe return.**Comtesse de L'Estorade to Mme. Marie Gaston (1835)**What has happened to you, dear? After two years of silence, I worry about Louise. Is this what love does? It seems to overshadow our friendship. I miss your letters and your presence.Regarding our situation, I want to address your comments on our political circumstances. We have chosen to remain in the Audit Department, which is necessary for our children's future. With careful management, we hope to secure their prospects.Louis has risen in the new order, becoming a peer and a respected figure in politics. He was even offered an embassy, but I refused it, wanting to stay in Paris for our children's education. In time, he will retire comfortably, and our son Rene will likely pursue a naval career.I am a happy mother, finding joy in my children. Armand is thriving at school, and I am proud of his achievements. I have arranged for him to have a private tutor while still attending school, allowing me to be present in his life.However, I am aware of the challenges of home education. Children raised at home may struggle to navigate societal norms. I strive to protect my children from the world's harsh realities while nurturing their talents.Now that I am thirty, I feel a sense of accomplishment. My bond with my children is profound, and I cherish every moment with them. Armand shows great promise, and I believe he will excel in public service. Rene is clever and determined, while Nais is a delightful presence in our lives.Yet, I worry about Nais's future and the uncertainties of marriage. The thought of her being handed over to someone unworthy fills me with dread. Louise, your silence has reminded me of these fears. Please write back soon; I miss you dearly.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After two years of silence, I feel compelled to share my happiness with you, dear Renee. Our love has flourished, bringing us daily joy. While we consider a trip to Paris, our leisurely mornings and intimate moments keep us content in our secluded life.Gaston, my beloved, is of average height with a well-proportioned figure and natural grace. His dark hair contrasts with his fair complexion, giving him a tragic beauty reminiscent of Louis XIII. He is free from the vices that often plague young men, possessing a steady and kind temperament. His smile is a constant source of joy for me, full of unspoken affection.Our life is rich with simple pleasures. I take pride in maintaining our home, reflecting my care for Gaston. I rise early to greet him, and our days are filled with tending to our garden and enjoying nature's beauty.Gaston is working on plays he began during earlier struggles, and I assist him, keeping our evenings lively. Our servants enjoy a relaxed life, and we have cultivated a harmonious relationship, allowing them to sell surplus produce while ensuring we receive the best.Despite my happiness, I struggle with jealousy and the fear of losing Gaston’s love. The absence of children weighs heavily on me; I long for motherhood. As I approach thirty, I feel the pressure of time, while Gaston remains youthful, which fills me with anxiety.I hope we won’t let years pass without communication again. While my happiness may seem monotonous, I cherish our friendship and look forward to hearing about your life and children.With all my love,  Your friend.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Dear Louise,Your recent letter reveals a naive perspective on love that worries me. You fail to see that true love has evolved into the enduring commitment of family life, which requires sacrifices from both partners. Marriage demands devotion and energy, often at the expense of personal desires.You seek adoration, a fleeting dream of youth, but as a mature woman, your focus should shift to the responsibilities of wifehood and motherhood. I urge you to return to Paris; it is not misfortune you should fear, but the pursuit of happiness that may lead you astray.Life's simple pleasures—bread, air, silence—sustain us. Passionate love can be intoxicating but ultimately exhausting. I chose a life of self-sacrifice, and despite its challenges, I would make the same choice again. Your romantic notions seem rooted in selfishness, and I hope you reconsider your path.Life is about balance; excess leads to suffering. You are unwell because you cling to a passion that marriage should temper. The true glory of home lies in calmness and shared experiences, which you seem to overlook. You risk degrading the sanctity of marriage by pursuing your impulses without restraint.If only you could experience the joys of family life, you might understand the lasting happiness it brings. I despair that my attempts to share my happiness with you have only highlighted your selfishness. Your love for Gaston seems more about your needs than his true self.Renee, calamity has struck! My heart is heavy with doubt, and certainty would be a relief. I discovered Gaston’s mare, Fedelta, covered in mud from Paris, leading me to suspect he had gone there without telling me. Jealousy consumed me, and I struggled to regain my composure.When Gaston found me distressed, he carried me to the parlor, but I shut myself away to weep. After some time, I agreed to see him when I felt better. I confronted him about his trip to Paris, and though he reassured me of his love, I remained unconvinced.Our day together was tense, and I felt my heartstrings tighten around him. I foolishly considered selling Fedelta out of jealousy. When Gaston left for Paris, I was consumed by anxiety and suspicion, feeling trapped in a hell of possibilities.He returned late, bringing me a whip as a gift, but I sensed it was a cover for something deeper. I later discovered he had bought it from Verdier, confirming my suspicions. My jealousy grew, and I resolved to remain vigilant for any signs of betrayal.Days passed, and Gaston worked diligently on his plays, but I noticed he was increasingly secretive. I followed him to the post office, where he received a letter from Nathan, revealing a play of his had been accepted. Yet, I couldn't shake the feeling that he was hiding something from me.I resolved to remain silent but vigilant, fearing he might be involved with another woman. My heart was torn between love and suspicion, and I felt trapped in a cycle of jealousy.Gaston’s work continued, and he seemed to thrive, but I feared he was hiding something significant. I discovered his drawer was empty, and I suspected he needed money for a woman. My heart sank as I realized I might have a rival.I confirmed my worst fears: Gaston was involved with a woman, a large Englishwoman who was the mother of his children. I watched him from a distance, and my heart shattered as I recognized the truth. He had been keeping this secret from me, and I felt utterly betrayed.Despite my heartbreak, my love for Gaston remained strong. He seemed to love me deeply, yet I hesitated to confront him about the other woman. I felt trapped in my emotions, unable to express my pain or jealousy.Dear Louise,I write urgently, having shared your distressing letter with Louis. We took measures to protect you, including discreetly involving the police.Gaston is not the man you fear. His brother, Louis, died in India, leaving behind a widow and two children. Gaston has been secretly supporting them, which explains his trips to Paris. The woman you suspect is not a rival but his sister-in-law, and he has been providing for her and the children out of love for his brother.You should find comfort in this revelation and consider embracing this family. Gaston’s actions stem from kindness and responsibility, not betrayal.Plan a surprise for him, and let this new understanding bring you peace.Oh, dear friend, it is too late for me. My own actions have led to my despair. I am tormented by my feelings and cannot bear the thought of losing Gaston. I feel as if I am fading away, and I long for relief from this pain. Come to me, for I am in need of your support as I face this darkness alone.In a letter dated August 7th, the Comtesse de L'Estorade explains her decision to stay with her dying friend, Louise, instead of traveling to Provence with their children. Louise, suffering from advanced consumption, is aware her time is limited. Although she appears cheerful around her husband, Gaston, she confides in the Comtesse about her impending death.Upon arriving at Ville d'Avray, the Comtesse sees Louise's health deteriorating. It becomes clear that Louise wants to keep her illness a secret from Gaston, believing it will help her maintain dignity. She reflects on her life, acknowledging that her passionate nature has contributed to her demise and recognizing the futility of jealousy in her marriage.Louise shares her views on love and marriage, lamenting that true happiness cannot rely solely on passion. While she feels fortunate to have loved deeply, she also understands societal expectations for women. As her family visits, the tension from her second marriage fades, allowing them to unite in her final days.Despite her illness, Louise retains her charm, even joking about wanting to look good in her coffin. The Comtesse notes how Louise's spirit captivates those around her, even as she experiences delirium and sings beautiful melodies, leaving her loved ones in tears.On August 26th, the Comtesse witnesses Louise's final moments. After a brief resurgence of strength, Louise passes away peacefully, creating a profound sense of loss. The Comtesse reflects on the void left by Louise's death and the deep connections formed through love and loss.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**A Daughter of Eve**  *By Honoré De Balzac*  *Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley*  **Dedication**  To Madame la Comtesse Bolognini, nee Vimercati. This work expresses my gratitude for the joy your conversation brought to a traveler reminiscing about Paris. Your daughter Eugenie embodies the gifts of womanhood, unlike the Eugenie in these pages, who faced a rigid upbringing. I often reflect on our cherished moments in Milan and hope this book serves as a sign of my friendship.---**Chapter I: The Two Maries**  In a luxurious Parisian boudoir, sisters Madame du Tillet and Madame Felix de Vandenesse are distressed. Their father, Comte de Granville, and mother, Comtesse de Granville, raised them under strict religious and social constraints, leading to marriages devoid of true understanding of love or life. The sisters share a deep bond, having grown up in an environment that stifled their emotions and desires.Madame du Tillet, married to wealthy banker Ferdinand du Tillet, feels trapped in a loveless marriage, while Madame de Vandenesse enjoys a more affectionate relationship with her husband, Comte Felix de Vandenesse. However, both women are ultimately dissatisfied, longing for the freedom and passion they have been denied.Their childhood was marked by strict religious education, leaving them innocent yet naive. They were married off without truly knowing themselves or the world, resulting in a sense of loss and yearning for something more fulfilling.---**Chapter II: A Confidence Between Sisters**  Madame de Vandenesse confides her unhappiness to her sister, revealing the emptiness of her luxurious life. She feels trapped by societal expectations and her husband's political ambitions. Madame du Tillet, despite her own struggles, tries to comfort her sister, but both feel the weight of their circumstances.The sisters reflect on their past and the constraints imposed by their upbringing. They lament their innocence and the lack of genuine love in their marriages. Madame du Tillet expresses a desire to help her sister but feels powerless against societal norms.---**Chapter III: The History of a Fortunate Woman**  Comte Felix de Vandenesse marries Madame de Vandenesse, hoping to provide her with a loving home. He is aware of her oppressive upbringing and seeks to nurture her spirit. Their early marriage is filled with happiness, but as time passes, Madame de Vandenesse begins to feel monotony in her life.She becomes increasingly aware of her desires and the limitations of her marriage. The count, while kind, fails to recognize the changes in his wife's heart. As she seeks fulfillment, she finds herself drawn to the idea of romantic love, complicating her feelings for her husband.---**Chapter IV: A Celebrated Man**  Raoul Nathan, a rising literary figure, enters the social scene, capturing Madame de Vandenesse's attention. Their initial interactions are filled with tension and unspoken attraction. Nathan, aware of his growing feelings for the countess, struggles with his ambitions and career demands.As he navigates his life, Nathan's relationship with Florine, an actress, becomes strained. He is torn between his love for Madame de Vandenesse and his obligations to Florine, who has supported him throughout his struggles.---**Chapter V: Florine**  Florine, a talented actress, has built her career with Nathan's support. Their relationship is complicated; she relies on him for success while feeling possessive. Despite their bond, Nathan's ambitions pull him away, leading to tension and jealousy.Florine's life is chaotic, and she grapples with her feelings for Nathan as he becomes increasingly involved with Madame de Vandenesse. Their relationship is tested as Nathan's ambitions grow, and Florine fears losing him to the countess.---**Chapter VI: Romantic Love**  Madame de Vandenesse and Nathan's relationship deepens, filled with passion and longing. The countess is captivated by Nathan's artistic spirit, while he is drawn to her innocence and beauty. Their love blossoms in secret, but societal pressures loom large.As they navigate their feelings, both experience joy and anxiety. The countess is torn between her duty to her husband and her desire for Nathan, while he grapples with the implications of their love on his career and reputation.---**Chapter VII: Suicide**  Nathan's struggles with debt and societal expectations lead him to contemplate suicide, believing it to be his only escape. The countess, unaware of his despair, continues to nurture her feelings for him.When she learns of his plight, she rushes to save him, demonstrating her love and commitment. Their reunion is fraught with emotion as they confront the reality of their situation and the sacrifices they must make for each other.---**Chapter VIII: A Lover Saved and Lost**  The countess's determination to save Nathan leads her to seek financial assistance from her family. As she navigates her love for Nathan, she faces societal judgment and personal turmoil.Despite her efforts, Nathan's situation remains precarious, and the pressures of their lives threaten to pull them apart. The countess's love for Nathan is tested as she grapples with the consequences of their relationship.---**Chapter IX: The Husband’s Triumph**  In the aftermath of their struggles, the countess and Nathan's relationship faces new challenges. The count, aware of the bond between his wife and Nathan, takes steps to protect his family's honor while seeking to understand their connection.As the countess confronts her feelings for Nathan, she must navigate her responsibilities to her husband and societal expectations. The tension between love and duty reaches a climax, forcing the characters to make difficult choices that will shape their futures.---**Addendum**  The characters in "A Daughter of Eve" appear in various other works by Balzac, illustrating the interconnectedness of his literary universe. Each character's journey reflects the complexities of love, ambition, and societal expectations in 19th-century France.######################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**Summary****I. Early Mistakes**On a bright April morning in 1813, a cabriolet arrives at the Tuileries, where an anxious father helps his beautiful daughter, Julie, out of the carriage. As they walk through the gardens, the father notes the curious glances from young men, jokingly suggesting they think she is his wife. However, Julie is preoccupied with the upcoming military review commanded by Napoleon, which she eagerly anticipates.The father reflects on the impending campaign that will lead to Napoleon's downfall, while Julie grows impatient as they are delayed from entering the review. When they finally reach the gates, they are turned away, and Julie expresses her disappointment, fearing she may never see the Emperor again. An officer, recognizing her, allows them entry, and Julie is captivated by the spectacle of the troops.As the review begins, Julie's attention shifts to Colonel Victor d’Aiglemont, a handsome officer who captures her heart. The father observes his daughter's infatuation with concern, fearing for her future happiness.**II. A Year Later**In March 1814, a year later, Julie and Victor are married, but their relationship is strained. They travel to Tours, where Julie's health declines. She feels trapped in a loveless marriage, haunted by her father’s warnings about Victor’s character. Despite her initial joy, she realizes that Victor is selfish and lacks the depth she desires.Julie finds solace in her daughter, Helene, born shortly after their marriage. However, her health continues to deteriorate, and she becomes increasingly isolated. Victor, focused on his ambitions, neglects her emotional needs, leading to a growing rift between them.**III. The Englishman**During this time, Julie encounters Lord Grenville, an Englishman who becomes infatuated with her. Their connection deepens, and Julie feels torn between her duty as a wife and her feelings for Grenville. She struggles with guilt over her attraction while still bound to Victor.As their relationship develops, Julie grapples with her identity and the constraints of her marriage. She seeks to maintain her dignity while navigating the complexities of love and loyalty. The tension between her desires and responsibilities intensifies, leading to moments of despair.**IV. The Crisis**One evening, after a confrontation with Victor, Julie confesses her feelings to Grenville. They share a passionate moment, but the reality of their situation looms over them. Julie is determined to protect her daughter from the pain of a broken home, yet she longs for the love and connection she lacks with Victor.As the story unfolds, Julie's internal conflict escalates. She must confront the consequences of her choices and their impact on her family, exploring themes of love, sacrifice, and the struggle for personal fulfillment within societal expectations.**V. The Resolution**Ultimately, Julie's journey leads her to a painful realization about her marriage and desires. She must decide whether to pursue her happiness at the cost of her family or remain in a loveless union for her daughter's sake. The story concludes with Julie facing the complexities of her life, embodying the struggles of many women of her time.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Nestled between the Seine and the Loing, the desolate landscape around the old chateau of Saint-Lange, bordered by the Forest of Fontainebleau, is steeped in melancholy.In late 1820, the Marquise d’Aiglemont, known for her beauty and wit in Paris, arrived at Saint-Lange, shocking local villagers who had not seen gentry in years. Her frail demeanor suggested she was retreating from despair, possibly due to financial ruin linked to her husband, the Marquis, who was away in Spain.As the Marquise isolated herself in the chateau, she rarely left her room, dining briefly and avoiding contact with her servants. Her daughter, Helene, was often kept at a distance as the Marquise struggled with overwhelming grief that stifled her maternal instincts. The villagers, initially intrigued, soon lost interest as her eccentricities became apparent.At twenty-six, the Marquise grappled with profound sorrow from an unfulfilled love; she mourned a man who died to preserve her honor. Bound by societal expectations, she felt unable to express her grief openly, leading to deep internal conflict.Her suffering was unique, tied to the loss of a first love, a pain often unrecognized and unexpressed. This anguish left her feeling hollow and disconnected from life's joys. Reflecting on her past, she realized her socially acceptable marriage lacked true happiness.As she sat in her room, the flat horizon mirrored her desolation. The beauty she once possessed felt burdensome, and she questioned her existence. Memories of childhood joy contrasted sharply with her current state, leaving her feeling trapped in a life that no longer resonated with her spirit.One day, the village priest, M. le Curé, visited her, sensing her sorrow. He shared a story of a father who lost all his sons in battle yet found the strength to live on. His compassion struck a chord with the Marquise, who began to see him as a potential confidant. However, her bitterness and despair quickly returned, overshadowing any glimmer of hope.In subsequent visits, the curé attempted to guide her towards faith and acceptance, but the Marquise remained skeptical. She expressed disdain for societal norms, particularly marriage, which she viewed as legalized oppression for women. Her reflections revealed deep-seated anger towards a world that had failed to provide her with the love and fulfillment she craved.Despite the curé’s efforts, the Marquise felt increasingly isolated in her grief. She longed for a love that transcended societal expectations, lamenting the loss of a connection that could have brought her true happiness. Her relationship with Helene was strained as she struggled to reconcile her feelings of motherhood with unresolved sorrow.As spring approached, the Marquise found some distraction in managing her estate, which helped alleviate her melancholy. By October, she had grown more composed, her initial grief transforming into quieter sadness. However, as she departed the chateau, she encountered the curé once more, choosing to avoid him, aware that he had seen through her facade.Ultimately, the Marquise’s journey reflects the complexities of grief, love, and societal expectations, illustrating how deeply personal pain can shape one’s existence and relationships.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Madame Firmiani hosted a ball where M. Charles de Vandenesse, a young diplomat, bid farewell to Paris before his appointment at the Congress of Laybach. Although he enjoyed the city's pleasures, he felt detached from its superficial society, prioritizing ambition over romance at thirty.At the ball, he met the enigmatic Marquise d’Aiglemont, known for her seclusion. Captivated by her beauty and depth, Vandenesse found her presence a stark contrast to the frivolity around him. Despite her delicate appearance, the Marquise revealed a life marked by sorrow and unfulfilled love.Their conversation shifted from light topics to deeper themes of love. Vandenesse expressed regret about leaving Paris, while the Marquise suggested that happiness might outweigh intellectual pursuits. He requested to visit her again, feeling a strong connection.During his visits, Vandenesse learned about the Marquise's tragic past—a lost love and an unhappy marriage. She confided about a man who had died for her honor, revealing the weight of her memories and the emptiness of her current life. This confession deepened Vandenesse's admiration for her noble spirit.Despite their growing bond, the Marquise struggled with her feelings, torn between loyalty to her deceased lover and her affection for Vandenesse. Meanwhile, Vandenesse fell deeply in love, experiencing the intensity of first passion. Ultimately, the story explores the intricate dynamics of love, loss, and the struggle for personal fulfillment within social expectations.Between the Barrière d’Italie and the Barrière de la Santé, a picturesque view of Paris captivates artists and tourists. From a higher vantage point along the boulevard, one can see a valley filled with factories among trees and streams, while the squalor of Faubourg Saint-Marceau lies on the opposite slope. The Panthéon and Val-du-Grace dominate the skyline, towering over small houses, with the observatory and the elegant lantern tower of the Invalides enhancing the architectural beauty.To the right, the canal Saint-Martin stretches out, bordered by public granaries. In the distance, the smoke-covered slopes of Belleville blend with the clouds, hinting at a vast city hidden from view. This city, caught between life and death, sends forth a muffled roar, asserting its presence.On a sunny spring morning, I admired this vibrant landscape, reflecting on the disdain some express for France while longing for its beauty. My reverie was interrupted by a scene nearby: a young woman, radiating joy, shared a tender moment with a handsome man and their charming little boy. Their happiness contrasted sharply with the sullen demeanor of a second child, a girl named Helene, who seemed burdened by thoughts beyond her years.I noted the stark differences between the siblings. Helene, with her dark hair and intense gaze, appeared older than her years, while her brother, Charles, was carefree and innocent. Their mother’s subtle favoritism was evident in their clothing, hinting at deeper family dynamics. Helene’s jealousy towards her brother culminated in a tragic moment when she pushed him into the river. Chaos ensued as the mother rushed to the water's edge, but the child was lost in the murky depths, leaving behind a haunting silence.Years later, the Marquis de Vandenesse mourned his father and hosted a notary at dinner. The notary, oblivious to the tension, monopolized the conversation, much to the Marquise de Vandenesse's frustration. She longed to join her children at the theater, but the notary continued his rambling, unaware of the couple's growing impatience.When the Marquis finally intervened, the notary remained oblivious, continuing to discuss business matters. Just as the Marquis attempted to dismiss him, the children returned unexpectedly, revealing they had left the theater due to a distressing scene. Helene had been crying, and the atmosphere shifted dramatically.The Marquise, recognizing the implications of the children’s return, felt a wave of dread. The notary, attempting to engage the children, inadvertently exacerbated the situation by asking about the play. Helene’s tears and the boy’s recounting of the play’s events revealed a deeper family tragedy, causing the Marquise’s composure to crack.In a moment of anger, the Marquis chastised the notary for his insensitivity, leading to a confrontation that left the notary bewildered. As he exited, he reflected on the evening’s events, unaware of the emotional turmoil he had stirred. At home, the notary’s wife pointed out his blunders, emphasizing the need for discretion in social situations. Despite his confusion, he resolved to seek clarification from the Marquis, unaware that the complexities of human relationships often elude even the most astute observers.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Set in spring at Versailles, the narrative follows the General, or Marquis, a former Napoleon officer who enjoys solitude at his country house near Montreuil. This retreat, originally a nobleman’s hideaway, offers seclusion while remaining close to Paris.On a winter Christmas evening, the General's family gathers at home, with servants absent due to a wedding in Versailles. The atmosphere is warm as the family engages in playful activities. The General watches his children, especially his youngest, Moïna, who playfully resists bedtime. Their older children, Gustave and Helene, are also present, each absorbed in their own pursuits.Helene, a beautiful and thoughtful girl, feels melancholic, reflecting on her life and the expectations placed upon her. The family dynamic is affectionate but hints at tensions, particularly between Helene and her strict mother, the Marquise, contrasting with Helene’s growing independence.The evening shifts when a stranger, pursued by unknown assailants, arrives seeking refuge. Despite initial hesitation, the General offers sanctuary, driven by honor. The stranger, desperate and agitated, insists on secrecy, revealing he is in grave danger.The General leads the stranger to a secluded room, locking him in for safety. Meanwhile, the Marquise and Helene remain unaware of the unfolding drama. The General wrestles with the moral implications of harboring a fugitive.Tension escalates when gendarmes arrive searching for the fugitive, who is revealed to be a murderer. The General faces a moral dilemma, caught between his promise of hospitality and the law. When questioned, he maintains composure, concealing the truth.As night deepens, Helene, driven by curiosity, ventures upstairs and encounters the fugitive, a complex character embodying both danger and allure. Their charged interaction creates a connection that transcends the immediate circumstances.Upon discovering Helene’s encounter, the General is filled with horror and rage. He confronts the stranger, demanding he leave. The fugitive challenges the General’s authority, asserting his moral justification. This confrontation reveals Helene’s feelings and the conflict between her loyalty to her family and her emerging sense of self.In a climactic moment, Helene declares her intention to leave with the fugitive, asserting her autonomy and rejecting her mother’s authority. The General, devastated by his daughter’s choice, feels a profound sense of loss. Helene’s departure marks a turning point, as she embraces a life defined by passion and danger, leaving behind her family’s safety.The narrative shifts to the aftermath of Helene’s decision, exploring the consequences of her choice. The General, haunted by the loss of his daughter, reflects on the fragility of familial bonds and the complexities of love and duty. The story concludes with a poignant reminder of the sacrifices made in the name of love, as the General grapples with the reality of his daughter’s new life and the irrevocable changes it brings to their family.Ultimately, "Two Meetings" explores themes of love, sacrifice, and the moral complexities of human relationships against a tumultuous historical backdrop, as characters navigate their desires and responsibilities, leading to a powerful exploration of defining choices.In June 1844, the Marquise d’Aiglemont, around fifty, walks through her daughter’s mansion garden in Paris. After losing her two sons, she has devoted her life and fortune to her youngest child, the Comtesse Moïna de Saint-Héreen. This intense affection for Moïna contrasts with her muted feelings for her deceased children, leading society to view her devotion as both admirable and questionable, as she sacrifices her own happiness for her daughter.Moïna, beautiful and charming, has become the Marquise's sole focus. However, the Marquise is troubled by Moïna's flirtation with Alfred de Vandenesse, whom she considers unscrupulous. Despite her concerns, she feels powerless to intervene, knowing Moïna would dismiss her warnings. Her friendship with Alfred's father complicates her ability to express disapproval.As the Marquise reflects on her life, she feels sorrow and resignation, recognizing the toll her emotional struggles have taken on her health. Tears fall as she contemplates Moïna's future and the potential consequences of her choices.When the Marquise tries to discuss Moïna's relationship with Alfred, she faces resistance and indifference. Moïna's dismissive attitude deepens her mother's anguish. After a painful exchange, the Marquise leaves, overwhelmed by the realization that her daughter no longer respects her.In despair, the Marquise collapses in the garden, and her lifeless body is carried inside. Initially self-focused, Moïna is struck by the seriousness of her mother’s condition. Witnessing her mother’s suffering, she begins to understand the depth of her love and sacrifices, ultimately mourning the mother she took for granted. The story emphasizes themes of love, regret, and complex familial relationships.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In spring 1822, M. le Baron Gaston de Nueil was sent to Lower Normandy by Parisian doctors to recover from overwork. He settled in Bayeux, welcomed by relatives who appreciated his presence.Bayeux had a distinct social hierarchy, and Gaston quickly mingled with the local elite, a mix of ancient nobility and newly wealthy families. One noble family, led by a proud patriarch, believed their daughters would secure wealth through their name alone, while a wealthier family spent winters in Paris, returning with contemporary trends. The politically savvy husband engaged in local politics, while his wife, though aware of her status, often lagged in fashion. Their son, entitled to the estate, did little, while the father focused on investments.The local bishop, a former Vicar-General, navigated tensions between these families, respected for his religious position but aware of his plebeian roots. Other local figures included retired military officers and their families, who maintained a semblance of aristocracy while discussing politics and gossiping.This social circle also included wealthy townspeople who gained entry into the aristocratic fold, often supported by older spinsters. A few witty ecclesiastics added a bourgeois element to the gatherings.Conversations among these groups were predictable, steeped in tradition and resistant to change. They critiqued Parisian society while upholding their own rigid standards. Gaston was scrutinized and accepted based on his family’s reputation and wealth, including an income of eighteen thousand livres and expectations of inheriting a chateau.Despite his promising background, Gaston’s elder brother, consumptive and likely to die soon, was conveniently omitted from discussions. Initially amused by his new acquaintances' quirks, Gaston soon grew weary of their monotonous lives, feeling trapped in a cloistered existence devoid of intellectual stimulation. Over time, he began to adapt to this stifling environment, risking complacency as he settled among these provincial fossils.One evening, Gaston found himself in a provincial drawing-room, enjoying a lavish dinner while reflecting on the simplicity of provincial life compared to the chaos of Paris. His attention was drawn to a conversation about Mme. de Beauseant, a noblewoman disgraced by a scandal involving M. d’Ajuda-Pinto. The dowager and a local nobleman discussed her isolation and societal disapproval as a separated wife.Intrigued, Gaston learned that Mme. de Beauseant had retreated to Normandy, shunned despite her noble lineage. He became fascinated by her, viewing her as a symbol of romance amidst the dullness of provincial life, igniting desires within him that contrasted sharply with his surroundings.Gaston became preoccupied with the idea of meeting Mme. de Beauseant, aware of the challenges posed by her seclusion and social status. He wandered near her residence, contemplating how to introduce himself without seeming foolish. Despite his self-doubt, he decided to enlist M. de Champignelles, a local nobleman, to facilitate an introduction.Gaston approached M. de Champignelles, claiming he had a delicate matter to discuss with the Vicomtesse and asked him to discreetly inquire if she would receive him. Flattered, the Marquis agreed to help. When he relayed the message, Mme. de Beauseant was initially hesitant but intrigued enough to reconsider.As Gaston awaited the outcome, he felt a mix of excitement and anxiety, meticulously preparing for his visit, believing his appearance would influence her perception. When he finally arrived at Courcelles, he experienced a rush of emotions, the intimacy of being alone with her starkly contrasting with the crowded gatherings he was used to. Despite his nervousness, he was determined to connect with the enigmatic Vicomtesse.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A fire crackled in the hearth, casting a warm glow over the room where the Vicomtesse de Beauseant sat gracefully in a low chair. Her fluid movements embodied the elegance of a high-born lady. As she stirred the fire and reached for a fallen glove, her delicate beauty captivated M. de Nueil, who had recently returned from a dull exile in Normandy. In her presence, he felt transported back to the vibrancy of Paris, where her fair hair, dark eyes, and noble demeanor contrasted sharply with the provincial women he had encountered.Mme. de Beauseant's beauty was tinged with sadness, hinting at a past filled with joy now overshadowed by loneliness. She was neither a wife nor a mother, but rather an outlaw of sorts, haunted by memories of a once-brilliant life. M. de Nueil admired her deeply, feeling both humbled and awed by her charm and misfortune, yet struggled to articulate his feelings.When she acknowledged him, he felt the weight of his earlier deception regarding a message he was meant to deliver. Overcome with shame, he confessed his wrongdoing, but she responded with cold disdain, summoning a servant to escort him out. As he left, he realized that departing meant losing any chance of returning to her presence, and he hesitated, contemplating the implications of his actions.In a moment of resolve, he turned back, claiming he had forgotten something. This time, he entered the salon unannounced, catching Mme. de Beauseant off guard. She invited him to sit, and he seized the opportunity, his eyes shining with hope. She, too, felt a flicker of delight at his presence, recognizing the joy he found in her company.However, she quickly reminded him of the gravity of their situation, questioning his motives and expressing disdain for any sympathy he might offer. M. de Nueil acknowledged his youthful folly and explained his longing for connection, describing the weariness of his isolation and how he had idealized her as the embodiment of his dreams. His sincerity began to soften her stance, hinting at the possibility of deeper understanding between them.An impassioned voice filled the chill solitude, bringing warmth and youthful charm. Mme. de Beauseant admired M. de Nueil's genuine expression and noble countenance, untouched by the world's cruelties. Gaston, in the bloom of youth, captivated her with his sincerity, appearing as a rare man untainted by ambition or vanity. Both harbored dangerous reflections about each other, concealing their thoughts. M. de Nueil recognized the Vicomtesse as a woman whose beauty was overshadowed by her depth of feeling, while she saw in him the ideal of a man unspoiled by societal egoism.As they explored their sentiments, Gaston’s inquiries were instinctive, while Mme. de Beauseant sought to understand his views by presenting opposing opinions. Her wit and grace made her feel at ease, prompting Gaston to exclaim, “Oh! madame, how could any man have left you?” This heartfelt remark surprised her, reviving her sense of innocence and condemning the world for her past. It offered her a chance to rise above her fall, leading her to reflect on their connection.She expressed reluctance to see him again, explaining that any suspicion of a second encounter would tarnish her reputation. Having endured a forced marriage, she sought happiness, believing it natural to pursue joy. Her past love had left her forsaken, and she accepted her blame, realizing her devotion had not been enough to keep him. Pain had taught her to guard her heart, and she acknowledged the agony of her past, which had left her feeling enslaved by love.Mme. de Beauseant, eloquent and beautiful, justified her solitude, stirring deep emotions in Gaston. He longed to express his feelings but feared rejection. Despite his internal struggle, he confessed his desire to make her forget her pain and devote his life to her happiness. However, she reminded him that they had gone too far and must remain strangers, emphasizing the pain of being unable to follow the one they love through life’s phases.As she rose to leave, she smiled, hinting at the unexpected nature of their encounter. Gaston, feeling distant from her, questioned whether her charm was merely coquetry. He returned home, consumed by conflicting emotions, recognizing that Mme. de Beauseant had become essential to his existence. He spent a restless night oscillating between despair and hope, ultimately deciding to write her a letter expressing his feelings.In his letter, he poured out his heart, acknowledging his unworthiness and the depth of his love. He expressed a willingness to leave France if she rejected him but hoped for her friendship. He sought to spend time with her, believing that the joy of her company would sustain him. He promised to respect her decision, even if it meant enduring heartache.Gaston’s anxiety grew as he imagined the Vicomtesse reading his letter, fearing she would laugh at his vulnerability. Overwhelmed by his emotions, he struggled to cope, feeling isolated without friends to distract him from his turmoil. Ultimately, Gaston’s experience encapsulated the tumultuous nature of youthful love, marked by intense emotions and the fear of rejection, illustrating the complexities of love and the challenges of navigating relationships in a world governed by societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Gaston de Nueil received a letter from the Vicomtesse de Beauseant, expressing disappointment in his youthful infatuation. While she appreciated his feelings, she could not reciprocate due to their age difference and her life experiences. She urged him to focus on his future and not sacrifice his potential for an illusion of love. Despite her kindness, she firmly stated that she could not love him and advised him to remain in his own country.In response, Gaston declared his unwavering love, vowing that only death could absolve his feelings. He sent his letter just as she was leaving for Geneva, prompting him to follow her, driven by hope that she might still care for him.Upon reaching Geneva, Gaston found the Vicomtesse settled in a small house by the lake. Their joyful reunion led to three years of blissful isolation, away from societal scrutiny. However, Gaston’s family obligations called him back to France after the deaths of his father and brother. The Vicomtesse followed, purchasing land near his estate, and they continued their life together, creating barriers against the outside world.For nine years, they lived happily, but the reality of their situation loomed. The Vicomtesse’s husband, the Marquis de Beauseant, remained alive, making their relationship socially untenable. Gaston’s strict mother would never accept her, creating potential tension.As time passed, a young heiress, Mlle. de la Rodiere, moved nearby, causing the Vicomtesse to question Gaston’s loyalty. In a heartfelt letter, she expressed fears about their age difference and the sacrifices he had made for her, worrying he might feel burdened and consider leaving for a more conventional life. Her letter revealed her deep love for Gaston and anguish over potentially harming him, imploring him to be honest about his feelings and desire for freedom. Meanwhile, Gaston felt overwhelmed by the weight of her words and the complexity of their love amid societal expectations.Gaston de Nueil faced a dilemma between his mother’s wish for him to marry Mlle. de la Rodiere, who had a substantial dowry, and his love for Mme. de Beauseant. Despite his feelings, he chose to end their relationship and pursue marriage.As he contemplated this decision, Gaston felt deep pity for Mme. de Beauseant, knowing the pain it would cause her. He hoped to soften the blow by gradually preparing her for their separation, planning to delay his marriage until she was more accepting. However, he realized that sending a letter would be a painful way to communicate his intentions, especially for someone as perceptive as she was.When Jacques delivered the letter to Mme. de Beauseant, she was filled with dread. Although it contained reassurances, she sensed the truth: Gaston was distancing himself. Overwhelmed by despair, she destroyed the letter, feeling betrayed and heartbroken.Gaston later received a note from Mme. de Beauseant, expressing her unwavering love and refusal to let him go. This letter reminded him of their past commitment and deeply affected him. Nevertheless, he married Mlle. de la Rodiere within weeks.After the marriage, Gaston appeared apathetic, leading his mother to believe he was happy. His new wife was sweet but lacked depth, and after a few months, Gaston became increasingly withdrawn. He began to secretly communicate with Mme. de Beauseant, asking Jacques to set aside game for her, hoping to maintain a connection.Eventually, Gaston attempted to reach out to Mme. de Beauseant with a letter, but it was returned unopened. In desperation, he rushed to her home, only to be met with her anguish. She threatened to throw herself from the window if he approached, and he fled in horror.Back home, Gaston wrote another letter pleading for a response, but when it was returned unopened, he succumbed to despair and took his own life. His suicide shocked those who understood the depth of his love for Mme. de Beauseant, illustrating the profound impact of lost passion and the emptiness of a loveless marriage. Mme. de Beauseant, unaware of his despair, maintained her dignity, highlighting the purity of her love. The story serves as a poignant reminder of the complexities of love, loss, and societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**La Grenadière** by Honoré de Balzac, translated by Ellen Marriage, describes a charming house on the right bank of the Loire River, near the Tours bridge. The area features picturesque cliffs, white stone houses, gardens, and vineyards, benefiting from a favorable climate for fruit cultivation.Located about a hundred paces from the village church of Saint-Cyr, La Grenadière sits halfway up a hillside, with steps leading down to an embankment that serves as a causeway. The property includes terraced gardens, trellises, and a variety of flowers, creating a vibrant environment. A Gothic archway opens into a rose-filled garden with a wooden summer-house covered in climbing plants.The rustic house has large windows, a slate roof, and yellow-washed walls with green shutters. Inside, a crooked staircase leads to a dining room with tiled floors and a sitting room adorned with saffron-colored wallpaper. The first floor has two simple bedrooms, and a modern kitchen connects to the main house, indicating that La Grenadière was once a retreat for townsfolk from Tours.The estate also includes a small vineyard and a vinedresser’s cottage, surrounded by stone walls and fruit trees. The terrace offers breathtaking views of the Loire, showcasing the beauty of Touraine. La Grenadière embodies a humble yet majestic dwelling, rich in natural beauty and tranquility, serving as a cherished retreat for lovers and dreamers.An Englishman renting La Grenadière for the summer pays a thousand francs for six months, plus extra for orchard fruit and vintage. This estate, valued since 1690, offers picturesque views of Touraine's valleys and cathedral towers, making it invaluable for health and happiness.In the spring of the Restoration, Mme. Willemsens arrived in Tours with her housekeeper and two children, seeking a home. She chose La Grenadière, likely attracted by its distance from town. The house was simply furnished, reflecting her modest lifestyle and leaving her social status ambiguous—whether upper middle or higher class. Her plain dress and reserved demeanor sparked local curiosity about her background.Tall and slender, Mme. Willemsens had delicate features and a melancholic expression. Her dark eyes hinted at hidden anguish, yet she exuded quiet strength. Dressed in mourning attire, she seemed to retreat from the world. Despite her frail health, she took evening walks with her children along the Loire, captivating Tours' residents.During her stay at La Grenadière, she ventured into Tours only twice: once to inquire about schools for her children and again to arrange lessons. Her rare appearances on the bridge piqued local interest, but little was known about her true status. The landlord referred to her as Augusta Willemsens, Countess of Brandon, suggesting a noble background, though this remained largely within business circles.Mme. Willemsens' life was a mystery, marked by her natural grace and deep bond with her children, enchanting those around her. Young men were drawn to her, but her sadness made them hesitant to approach. She discarded letters from admirers, preferring to focus on her life at La Grenadière.Her sons, Louis-Gaston and Marie-Gaston, also attracted attention. Louis-Gaston, the elder, was robust and energetic, while Marie-Gaston was more delicate. Their close relationship was evident, with Louis often caring for his younger brother. Each morning, they began their day with a prayer, maintaining a meticulous routine to present themselves well for their mother.The bond among the three was profound, characterized by mutual respect and empathy. If Mme. Willemsens was quiet, her sons mirrored her mood; Louis, perceptive and protective, often redirected their activities to ensure she was not overwhelmed. In this idyllic yet isolated life, the family thrived on shared experiences and emotional connections, illustrating the power of love and the importance of familial bonds.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Mme. Willemsens dressed beautifully for her children, striving to be a positive presence in their lives. She was available to hear their lessons from ten to three, with lunch in the summer-house. After lunch, while the children played, she relaxed on a sofa, enjoying the scenic landscape of Touraine.The children explored the orchard and garden, often seeking their mother’s guidance. During lessons, Mme. Willemsens embroidered quietly, focusing on inspiring Louis while being less concerned about Marie. Afterward, she discussed Louis’ performance with his tutors to support him effectively.Their days balanced work and play, fostering a harmonious environment. Mme. Willemsens instilled values of moderation and resilience in her children, nurturing their spirits with praise. Watching them play often brought tears of joy to her eyes, as they had never caused her distress. These moments represented her ideal of happiness, akin to a vision of Heaven.In summer evenings, she read inspiring biographies to Louis while Marie slept, using these stories to teach him about perseverance. However, her happiness was tinged with sadness, as she feared for their future without her. One evening, she confided in Louis about her illness and the inevitability of her death, expressing her deep love and concern for their well-being. She urged him to be strong and promised to guide him in caring for Marie.As summer progressed, Mme. Willemsens’ health declined, and the atmosphere at La Grenadiere grew somber. The once vibrant household became quiet, with the children adapting to their mother’s condition. Louis, feeling a strong connection to her, focused more on his studies, showing remarkable progress.Despite her illness, Mme. Willemsens cherished her time with her children, often seen walking with them in the gardens. As autumn approached, she remained devoted to them, savoring each moment. Unbeknownst to her, Louis worked diligently at night, excelling in his studies and preparing for the entrance examination to the Ecole polytechnique, encouraged by his mother to balance his studies with physical activity. Through these experiences, Louis matured, developing a sense of purpose and determination, guided by his mother’s love.In October, Mme. Willemsens rose at noon, enjoying the warm atmosphere reminiscent of the Bay of Naples. She cherished her time with her children, Louis and Marie, who adored her and brought her flowers, creating a serene yet melancholic scene amid the autumn leaves.Eventually, the doctor confined her to her room, where she remained surrounded by flowers and her children. In early November, she played the piano for the last time, reflecting on her life. When ordered to stay in bed, she asked Louis for one last view of their homeland, expressing fears about their uncertain future.As her health declined, Louis and Marie stayed by her side, witnessing her suffering. The doctor prohibited flowers, signaling that the end was near. One quiet Sunday night, Mme. Willemsens called Louis to her, revealing that she would soon die. She instructed him to retrieve important papers, including their birth certificates and a letter to her estranged husband, Lord Brandon, forgiving him.She shared her limited financial resources with Louis, urging him to keep the money close, as everything would be sealed after her death. Louis vowed to care for Marie, planning to send her to school while pursuing a career at sea. Mme. Willemsens found solace in her son’s determination, feeling a sense of peace before her passing.The next morning, she received the sacrament surrounded by her children and Annette, their caretaker. As her breathing slowed, sorrow filled the room, and she eventually passed away, leaving her children in grief. They prepared her body for burial, marking her grave with a simple cross inscribed with a poignant message about her life.After the funeral, Louis and Marie returned home for a final farewell before leaving with Annette. Louis entrusted Marie’s birth certificate and their limited funds to Annette, then took Marie to school, offering her heartfelt advice as they parted ways.A month later, Louis-Gaston, now an apprentice on a warship, left France behind. As he gazed at the receding coastline, he felt the weight of solitude and responsibility. An old sailor comforted him, reminding him of God’s presence, and Louis accepted his new life at sea, determined to honor his mother’s memory. Thus, the story concludes with Louis embracing his future, embodying the resilience and hope instilled by his mother.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In this poignant narrative, Honoré De Balzac explores the intertwining themes of fear and love among young lovers seeking solace in each other. The story combines personal experience with poetic potential.In 1819, while traveling from Paris to Moulins, the narrator finds himself outside a coach due to financial constraints. He befriends a wealthier traveler, and they bond over discussions of love, particularly for women aged thirty-five to forty. Their youthful exuberance leads them to share intimate details about their mistresses, both older than themselves.As they recount their romantic escapades, the young men express admiration for their devoted mistresses, despite their marriages. Their camaraderie deepens as they share the risks taken to be with their lovers. However, tragedy strikes when the coach overturns, resulting in the death of the narrator's companion.In his final moments, trapped beneath the coach, the young man entrusts the narrator with a solemn task: to inform his mistress of his death. He gives the narrator a key to access his home and the letters he wishes to return to her. The young man dies, leaving the narrator with the heavy burden of delivering the news.Upon reaching La Charité, the narrator fulfills his friend's last wishes, encountering grief from the household staff but focusing on the sorrow awaiting the Countess de Montpersan. With limited funds, he decides to walk the remaining distance to her chateau, driven by urgency.As he approaches, he grapples with the emotional weight of his task, realizing the cruelty of being the bearer of such devastating news. Upon arrival, he finds the chateau bustling but struggles to locate the countess. A young girl assists him, leading him through the park to find her mother.This story captures the complexities of love, loss, and the burdens of communication, illustrating how a simple journey can transform into a profound encounter with fate. The narrator's reflections emphasize the delicate balance between joy and sorrow in human relationships.I took great care to present myself well before meeting Juliette and her husband, M. le Comte and Mme. la Comtesse de Montpersan, wanting to avoid being mistaken for a local rate collector. Reflecting on that moment, I find it amusing.As I composed myself, I spotted Juliette with her husband. She seemed hurried, and upon noticing me, her expression revealed disappointment. I struggled to recall the elegant phrases I had prepared, which allowed her husband to approach. I awkwardly inquired if they were indeed the Count and Countess. Observing them closely, I noted the husband’s pompous demeanor and shabby appearance, contrasting sharply with the Countess's delicate beauty and grace. She was petite, charming, and exuded youthfulness, evoking a strong desire within me.Realizing the situation, I decided to inform the Count of my purpose. I discreetly mentioned the death of my traveling companion, which visibly affected him. He expressed concern for his wife, and I assured him I would not disclose the message I carried until he was informed. This earned me his trust, and we returned to the house where I was invited to dinner.As we approached, Juliette cast a curious glance at us, revealing her mixed feelings about my unexpected presence. I felt sympathy for her, recalling her beauty in the sunlight. I revealed that I had come on behalf of someone who called her Juliette, which startled her. When I mentioned her companion's illness, she grew anxious, fearing for his life. Her desperate plea for the truth struck me deeply, and I could only nod in response.Just then, the Count called for dinner, and I followed him inside. The meal was lavish, but the Countess was notably absent. A maid reported that she could not be found, prompting concern. The canon and I searched the grounds, calling for her, but to no avail. Eventually, we discovered her hidden in a hayloft, sobbing in despair. With the maid's help, we brought her to her room, instructing that she not be disturbed.Upon returning to the dining room, I found the Count surprisingly unfazed, having consumed most of the meal. His indifference stemmed from a severe dietary restriction due to a medical condition, explaining his lack of concern for his wife’s absence. The evening was somber, filled with unanswered questions about Juliette's distress.Later that night, I was awakened by the Countess, who appeared at my bedside, pale and shaken. She sought confirmation of the tragic news, and I recounted the accident, careful to spare her the most harrowing details. She listened intently, her grief palpable. When I revealed her companion's fears and the message he entrusted to me, her composure faltered, and she expressed regret for having burned his letters, feeling she had lost everything.In a moment of connection, I handed her a lock of his hair, a tangible reminder of their bond. Her gratitude was overwhelming, and she wished me happiness in love before retreating with her precious token.The following day felt surreal as I reflected on the previous night. I spent time with Juliette, witnessing her noble spirit and selflessness. In the evening, the Count accompanied me partway to Moulins, asking me to deliver a sum of money to a contact in Paris. I willingly accepted, unaware of the thoughtful gesture behind her request.This experience deepened my understanding of love and sacrifice, leaving me with a poignant story to share with a woman who cherished me, reminding me of the fragility of life and the bonds we form.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################In the winter of 1829-30, the Vicomtesse de Grandlieu's salon was nearly empty at one o'clock in the morning. After a young man left, the Vicomtesse approached her daughter, Camille, who was distracted by outside noises. Concerned about Camille's feelings for the Comte de Restaud, she warned her about his mother, Mlle. Goriot, whose questionable past could jeopardize Camille's future. Despite Restaud's admirable qualities, his family background raised concerns.Derville, a family friend and attorney, overheard their conversation and decided to intervene. He had previously helped the Vicomtesse regain lost properties, improving her financial situation. Though respected in high society, he remained humble and declined further career advancement.As Derville shared a story that might change Camille's view of Restaud, he noted her growing affection for him. At a recent ball, Camille expressed confidence in Restaud's potential wealth and status. Derville suggested that if Restaud were already wealthy, many girls would pursue him, hinting at Camille's feelings and suggesting a budding connection between her and Derville.Derville reflects on Gobseck, a sallow, impassive usurer living in a damp, convent-like house. Rarely interacting with others, he becomes Derville's confidant over four years. Born Jean-Esther Van Gobseck, he is wealthy yet emotionally detached.Gobseck emphasizes gold as the ultimate power, dismissing societal norms and asserting that self-interest is the only constant in human nature. His cynical view suggests that true happiness lies in wealth and manipulation.Gobseck recounts experiences collecting debts, revealing two bills: one from a beautiful Countess burdened by her extravagant lifestyle, who ultimately offers him a diamond instead of payment, and another from a struggling linen-draper named Fanny Malvaut. Derville is captivated by the Countess's beauty and the contrast between her life and Fanny's modest existence.Visiting Fanny, who embodies innocence and sincerity, Derville feels a stark contrast to the Countess's world of luxury and moral decay. He ponders their differing lives, feeling pity for both women. Gobseck's insights into human nature and societal power dynamics leave Derville disturbed about wealth and desire, as he grapples with his feelings for the Countess and admiration for Fanny.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################“Will you take a glass of _eau sucree_?” asked the Vicomtesse, interrupting Derville.“I should be glad of it,” he replied.Mme. de Grandlieu expressed skepticism about the conversation's relevance, prompting Derville to reference Sardanapalus. He recounted how Mademoiselle Camille's happiness had once depended on Daddy Gobseck, who had recently died, leaving M. de Restaud a fortune. Derville also mentioned his wife, Fanny Malvaut, affirming his love for her.After submitting his thesis, Derville became a licentiate in law and later an advocate. Gobseck, who had previously underestimated him, began consulting him on complex matters, showing newfound respect. Eventually, Derville became head-clerk and moved into his employer’s house, marking a significant career step.When he bid farewell to Gobseck, the old man showed no emotion but later sought Derville’s advice on a challenging expropriation case. Derville’s principal, a man of pleasure, fell into financial trouble and decided to sell his practice for 150,000 francs. Lacking funds, Derville approached Gobseck for a loan, determined to maintain his self-respect.Gobseck agreed to lend him the money at a rate of thirteen percent per annum, a deal Derville accepted despite the high interest. They discussed guarantees, and Gobseck insisted on receiving bills made payable to a third party. Derville also agreed to provide legal advice to Gobseck for free as part of their arrangement.Three months later, Derville became an attorney and successfully recovered estates for Mme. de Grandlieu, gaining recognition in the legal field. He married Fanny, whose inheritance helped him pay off Gobseck within five years. Their shared struggles deepened their bond, leading to a happy life together.A year after acquiring his practice, Derville attended a bachelor breakfast hosted by M. de Trailles, a well-known dandy. The event was marked by extravagant displays and chaotic revelry, where Derville found himself charmed by de Trailles, who persuaded him to introduce him to Gobseck.The next day, Derville hesitated but ultimately agreed to accompany de Trailles to meet Gobseck. Upon arrival, de Trailles appeared anxious, revealing a woman in a cab nearby, which excited him. Derville introduced de Trailles to Gobseck, who remained impassive despite de Trailles’ attempts at charm.Gobseck refused to lend money to de Trailles, citing his debts and lack of security. De Trailles, undeterred, left to fetch something he believed would satisfy Gobseck. Gobseck expressed concern about de Trailles’ intentions, revealing a rare moment of vulnerability.When de Trailles returned with a woman, Derville recognized her as the Countess, one of Goriot’s daughters. The Countess, visibly distressed, sought to pawn her diamonds for cash, hoping to retain the right to repurchase them. Derville explained the transaction terms, which relieved her but darkened de Trailles’ mood.Gobseck examined the diamonds with unsettling eagerness, revealing his obsession with wealth. His reaction was childlike, contrasting sharply with his usual stoicism. The scene underscored the complex interplay of desire, power, and desperation among the characters as they navigated the treacherous waters of social and financial relationships in Paris.The dialogue begins with a discussion about a set of diamonds, once valuable before the Revolution but now diminished in worth due to market saturation. Gobseck, a moneylender, inspects the diamonds, noting their flaws. The Count seeks a loan of one hundred thousand francs for three years, and Gobseck agrees but insists on weighing the diamonds first. The Countess appears distressed, hinting at her moral struggle.As Gobseck weighs the diamonds, the narrator observes the Countess's turmoil. When asked about ownership, she confirms the diamonds are hers, but Gobseck refuses to draft the loan agreement due to legal issues from her marriage. He offers a deal: eighty thousand francs down, with the diamonds as collateral. The Countess hesitates, while Maxime, a young man present, expresses despair, suggesting he may end his troubles soon.Ultimately, the Countess accepts Gobseck's offer, and he provides her with a check and cash. Tensions rise as Maxime insults Gobseck, who coolly threatens him. After the Countess leaves, Gobseck revels in acquiring the diamonds, showcasing his greed.Days later, the Count visits the narrator, expressing confidence in him and discussing Gobseck's character. He reveals his intention to transfer property to Gobseck, fearing for his health. The narrator warns the Count about disinheriting his other children, leading the Count to agree to include them in the counter-deed.The narrative shifts to the Countess, who is determined to secure her children's future despite her past. As her husband’s health declines, the bedridden Count forbids her from entering his room, prompting her to spy on him, believing he is hiding money and documents.As the Count nears death, he confides in his son Ernest, entrusting him with a secret letter for the narrator. The Countess, sensing her husband's intentions, tries to manipulate Ernest into revealing the secret. The Count confronts her, leading to a dramatic scene where he accuses her of treachery.After the Count's death, chaos ensues as the Countess searches for documents, revealing her desperation. The narrator discovers that the Count had hidden important papers, including the counter-deed, which the Countess inadvertently destroys in her panic. Gobseck, now the rightful owner of the property, contemplates the implications of her actions.In the aftermath, the narrator reflects on the Countess's transformation and the consequences of her greed. Gobseck, despite his avarice, shows a peculiar understanding of human nature, believing that adversity teaches valuable lessons. The story concludes with the narrator contemplating the fate of the Countess and her children, as well as Gobseck's legacy, exploring themes of greed, morality, and the complexities of human relationships.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**The Marriage Contract: Summary****Dedication**  To Rossini.**Chapter I: Pro and Con**Monsieur de Manerville, a respected Norman gentleman, married a wealthy heiress from Bordeaux and became a Gascon after selling his estate. He served as major of the Gate Guards until his death in 1813, having avoided the Revolution by going to Martinique. Upon returning, he found his estate well-managed by Mathias, a progressive notary’s clerk. After Madame de Manerville's death in 1810, Monsieur de Manerville grew increasingly miserly, depriving his only son, Paul, of resources.Paul de Manerville, returning from college in 1810, lived under his father's strict control for three years. This oppressive environment stunted his emotional growth, making him timid and unable to assert himself. He felt trapped in his father's mansion, unable to socialize with peers due to financial constraints. His father took him to royalist gatherings, where Paul felt out of place among the aging nobility. To cope, he engaged in physical activities like fencing and horseback riding, while his intellectual pursuits were limited to novels.After his father's death, Paul inherited a substantial fortune and estates but was disillusioned with Bordeaux and Lanstrac. He spent six years traveling Europe, eventually exhausting his capital and relying on his estate's revenues. Realizing he needed to settle down, Paul decided to return to Bordeaux, manage his affairs, and marry, believing that his noble title would attract a suitable partner.Now a count, Paul recognized the societal value of marriage.
