He had gained worldly knowledge through his experiences, which he believed would enhance his prospects. Despite his extravagant lifestyle, he maintained a reputation for integrity and elegance, avoiding gambling and deceit.One morning, Paul confided in his friend de Marsay about his desire to marry and live as a country gentleman. De Marsay warned him against marriage, arguing it would lead to a loss of freedom and happiness. He criticized the institution, suggesting it primarily benefits children, and questioned whether Paul truly wanted such responsibilities.De Marsay painted a bleak picture of marriage, emphasizing societal expectations and potential ridicule. He advised Paul to enjoy his bachelorhood and consider marrying later, perhaps a widow, to avoid the complications of a young bride.Paul defended his desire for a family, longing for companionship and stability. He envisioned a life where he could share joys and secrets with a wife, rejecting de Marsay's cynical view. He believed he had the courage to be a good husband and father, seeking a deep connection with a partner.De Marsay remained skeptical, arguing that Paul’s romanticized view of marriage was naive. He warned that love dynamics change post-marriage, often leading to disappointment. He cautioned Paul about the legal and emotional complexities, suggesting that a husband must exert control over his wife, which Paul might not be capable of.Despite de Marsay's warnings, Paul remained resolute in his desire for a family, dismissing the notion that he would become a mere shadow of himself in marriage. He yearned for emotional connections, rather than the superficiality he associated with bachelorhood.De Marsay continued to challenge Paul’s perspective, arguing that the realities of marriage often clash with ideals of love. He suggested that Paul’s experiences with women might lead to misconceptions about marital relationships, where initial excitement can fade into disillusionment.Ultimately, Paul’s determination to marry stemmed from a desire for genuine companionship and a stable family life, contrasting sharply with de Marsay’s cynical outlook. The conversation highlighted the tension between societal expectations and personal desires, as Paul sought to navigate the complexities of love and commitment in a changing world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In the winter of 1821, Comte de Manerville returned to Bordeaux, determined to restore his family mansion and maintain his reputation for elegance. His connections within royalist society quickly elevated him to fashionable prominence, earning him the nickname “the pink of fashion.” His charm and Parisian sophistication captivated the local elite, allowing him to navigate social interactions with ease.Paul de Manerville's success in provincial society stemmed from his attention to appearance and gentle demeanor, endearing him to both men and women. However, he soon recognized that marriage was essential for a fulfilling life in the provinces. Although too young for the mundane pursuits of provincial life, he longed for companionship, particularly with Mademoiselle Evangelista, the celebrated beauty of Bordeaux.Mademoiselle Evangelista was the daughter of a wealthy Spanish merchant who had settled in Bordeaux. After her father's death, Madame Evangelista maintained a lavish lifestyle, raising her daughter in luxury. By the time Paul returned, Natalie was a stunning young woman, yet rumors about her extravagant upbringing made potential suitors hesitant, with many believing only a prince could afford to marry her.When Paul and Natalie first met, they were immediately drawn to each other, each secretly envisioning a future together. Paul was aware of the Evangelista wealth and felt no need for inquiries that would typically deter a suitor. He dismissed warnings about the family's extravagance, confident in his ability to manage the financial implications of marrying Natalie.As Paul frequented the Evangelista household, he became increasingly enchanted by both mother and daughter. Madame Evangelista, at forty, was still strikingly beautiful and adept at navigating social circles, harboring ambitions for her daughter to rise in Parisian society. Paul, appreciating the warmth of the Evangelistas, found himself spending more time with them, leading society to speculate about his intentions toward Natalie.Despite the growing attention, neither Paul nor Natalie openly acknowledged their feelings. Natalie maintained a reserved demeanor, intriguing Paul, while Madame Evangelista subtly encouraged their connection. Paul began to view marriage as a means to secure his social standing and fulfill his ambitions, believing that wealth would ease the challenges of married life.As their relationship developed, Paul idealized Natalie, overlooking potential flaws in her character. Captivated by her beauty and charm, he failed to recognize the complexities that might arise in their future together. Societal pressures surrounding their union added to the allure, as both families seemed to endorse the match.However, beneath the surface, Natalie’s upbringing and her mother’s ambitions posed potential challenges. While Paul was drawn to the idea of a life with Natalie, he remained unaware of the deeper dynamics at play, including Madame Evangelista’s desire for power through her daughter's marriage.As Paul contemplated marriage, he grappled with the realities of commitment and potential conflict, caught between romantic ideals and the practicalities of a life intertwined with Natalie and her mother. This chapter foreshadows the complexities of their impending union, highlighting the societal expectations and personal ambitions that would shape their lives, revealing the intricate dance of desires and challenges that accompany the pursuit of love and status in a society governed by appearances.In the winter of 1822, Paul de Manerville sought to marry Mademoiselle Natalie Evangelista with the assistance of his great-aunt, Baronne de Maulincour. After consulting Madame Evangelista, the baroness informed Paul that the marriage was likely to succeed but warned him about the dowry: Natalie’s fortune was tied to her father’s patrimony, and her mother would not contribute.The baroness advised Paul to consult their notary, Maitre Mathias, to ensure the marriage contract protected his interests for future children, leaving Paul anxious about potential deceit from his future mother-in-law.Following this advice, Paul approached Madame Evangelista to discuss the marriage contract. She suggested that both families’ notaries meet to negotiate terms. Nervous, Paul agreed, relieved that the notaries would handle the details.Madame Evangelista faced pressure as she owed her daughter a significant sum from her late husband’s estate, which she could not pay. Fearing Paul might withdraw if he learned the truth, she resolved to manipulate the situation.The next day, she summoned her ambitious notary, Maitre Solonet, who assured her they could navigate negotiations favorably. Madame Evangelista aimed to maintain control over her daughter’s fortune while ensuring a good match for Natalie.As evening approached, both notaries met to discuss the marriage contract. Solonet proposed terms benefiting Madame Evangelista, while Mathias insisted on protecting Paul’s interests. Their discussions highlighted stark differences: Mathias represented traditional values, while Solonet embodied modern, opportunistic strategies.During dinner, Paul and Natalie shared tender moments, but the tension regarding the marriage contract loomed. Paul was enamored with Natalie, but Mathias warned him about the financial implications of marrying into a family that had squandered much of its wealth.As negotiations continued, Mathias expressed concern over the potential ruin of Paul’s fortune if he accepted unfavorable terms. He urged caution, but Paul, blinded by love, was eager to proceed.Ultimately, a compromise was reached where Madame Evangelista would secure her daughter’s inheritance while ensuring her own financial stability. The notaries agreed on terms that would protect Paul’s interests and provide for future generations. As the evening concluded, both families felt relief, believing they had successfully navigated the complexities of the marriage contract, though underlying tensions hinted at challenges ahead.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next day, Elie Magus, a jeweler in Bordeaux, was summoned by Madame Evangelista to discuss jewelry for the upcoming marriage of Comte Paul and Mademoiselle Natalie. Instead, she sought an appraisal of her mother’s diamonds, essential for the marriage contract. Magus valued the necklace and earrings at 250,000 francs and the “Discreto” diamond at 100,000 francs but offered only 75,000 francs for the diamond and 160,000 for the jewelry, citing depreciation.This conversation sparked rumors among the household staff and community about the marriage's stability. Speculation arose that Madame Evangelista was selling her house and that the marriage might not happen. Despite notaries denying these claims, public skepticism persisted, fueled by jealousy of the Evangelista family's wealth.As rumors spread, Paul organized a hunting party, further igniting speculation. Madame de Gyas, eager to confirm the gossip, visited Natalie and her mother, feigning concern while relaying the circulating rumors. Aware of the motivations behind the gossip, Madame Evangelista dismissed the concerns, asserting that the marriage contract was a private matter.To counter the rumors, Madame Evangelista planned an extravagant celebration called the "fete of the camellias," intending to showcase her wealth. Preparations took over a month, during which the legalities of the marriage contract were finalized, and the community buzzed with anticipation.On the day of the contract signing, Madame Evangelista had a moment of realization regarding the implications of the contract. Recalling her earlier discussions with the notaries, she feared her daughter's fortune might be compromised. Despite her initial confidence, she resolved to demand clarity on the contract's terms.Lavish preparations transformed the courtyard into a tent filled with camellias, creating an opulent atmosphere. As guests arrived, excitement heightened, but the signing of the contract loomed ominously. Paul and Natalie, blissfully unaware of the underlying tensions, engaged in playful banter while the notaries read the contract details.Madame Evangelista listened intently, and when the entailment clause was explained, she realized the potential financial ruin for her daughter. The entail would limit Natalie’s inheritance, leaving her vulnerable should Paul die first. This revelation struck Madame Evangelista deeply, leading her to conclude that her daughter was at risk of losing her fortune.Despairing, she recognized that breaking off the marriage was no longer an option; the public nature of the preparations made it impossible. She felt trapped, realizing her earlier manipulations had backfired, jeopardizing her daughter’s future.As the contract was finalized, Madame Evangelista's emotions boiled over. She confronted the notaries, expressing her anger and frustration. Solonet, one of the notaries, suggested delaying the signing, but Madame Evangelista felt that would worsen their reputation.Ultimately, she decided to proceed with the marriage, vowing to protect her daughter’s interests. In a moment of clarity, she resolved to regain control over her daughter’s fortune, planning to invest wisely for Natalie’s security.The signing of the contract marked a turning point, with Madame Evangelista determined to navigate her new reality. As the festivities began, she felt a mix of triumph and resentment, knowing her daughter’s happiness was intertwined with her own ambitions and fears. The night unfolded with dancing and celebration, but beneath the surface, tensions simmered, setting the stage for future conflicts.As Paul prepared for his marriage, he was troubled by doubts, particularly regarding his mother-in-law, Madame Evangelista. While he reassured himself about their financial arrangements and his union with Natalie, he remained cautious, mindful of Mathias's warnings.Unbeknownst to Paul, the dynamics had shifted. Madame Evangelista, once supportive, was now contemplating withdrawing her interests from their lives. Paul struggled to understand her complex Creole nature, which combined intellect and passionate volatility, now stirred by the prospect of losing her daughter.The next day, Madame Evangelista and Natalie reminisced about their harmonious life. She expressed sorrow over Natalie’s marriage, warning that love could fade if not managed carefully. She advised Natalie to establish her authority as a wife, emphasizing the importance of appearing obedient while subtly maintaining control.Initially hesitant, Natalie was influenced by her mother’s insights. Madame Evangelista cautioned against the dangers of constant proximity in marriage, suggesting social engagements to maintain allure and keep Paul interested. She stressed that a woman must never fully surrender her power, advising Natalie to cultivate mystery and reserve.As the wedding approached, Madame Evangelista revealed her plan to sell her jewelry to support Natalie, proposing an exchange that would benefit her daughter. Paul, moved by this sacrifice, agreed, unaware of the underlying tensions.When Paul arrived, he seemed troubled, leading Madame Evangelista to suspect Mathias's influence. She reiterated the importance of a woman's vanity in maintaining happiness and proposed selling her jewelry to ensure Natalie had valuable pieces. Paul accepted, and they discussed selling their property, with Natalie showing signs of understanding business matters.Determined to step back, Madame Evangelista insisted her presence might hinder their marriage. She expressed a desire to manage her own affairs at Lanstrac, believing her absence would allow Natalie and Paul to bond without her influence. Paul felt relieved by this arrangement, sensing newfound freedom.As the wedding neared, Natalie felt anxious about her new life without her mother. Despite her fears, she was determined to be a good wife. The ceremony took place at midnight, often associated with ominous omens. Separated from her mother, Natalie was filled with doubt about her future.Guests noted the unusual circumstances of the wedding, with some criticizing Paul for excluding Madame Evangelista. They speculated about the implications of this decision, suggesting it might invite trouble into their marriage. Meanwhile, Madame Evangelista, having played her part, left Natalie with a final piece of advice: to be a wife, not a mistress.After the ceremony, she feigned despair to extract promises from Paul, ensuring his commitment to Natalie. As they departed for Paris, Natalie signaled to her mother that Paul had won the initial battle, but she was already beginning to assert her influence over him.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Five years later, in November, Comte Paul de Manerville visited his old friend, Monsieur Mathias, in Bordeaux. Mathias, now retired, was absent, but his housekeeper led Paul to Madame Mathias's room. Exhausted from his journey, Paul slept until evening. When Mathias returned, he found Paul changed but calm. They shared a meal, during which Mathias expressed sorrow over the public notices of Paul's financial troubles.Mathias lamented the loss of Paul's estate, feeling personally shamed by the public seizures. Paul revealed he had left his wife, Natalie, without her knowledge, planning to sail to Calcutta to rebuild his fortune under the name Camille. Mathias was shocked by Paul's decision to enter commerce, believing it beneath his dignity.Paul explained that he had incurred massive debts due to lavish spending and the costs of his marriage. He had given Natalie expensive gifts and sold her investments to maintain their lifestyle. Mathias was incredulous at the scale of Paul's debts, which exceeded a million and a half francs. Paul insisted he could not burden Natalie with their financial troubles and preferred to separate their assets.Mathias suggested Paul could live off Natalie's income while recovering, but Paul rejected this, wanting to work for his fortune rather than rely on her. He believed women should not be involved in financial matters, while Mathias felt a wife should share both joys and burdens of marriage.The next day, Mathias received a bill of exchange for 150,000 francs from de Marsay, a friend of Paul’s. Despite his concerns about Paul's plans, Mathias ultimately accepted Paul's determination to leave for India. The ship, "Belle-Amelie," was set to sail, and Mathias accompanied Paul to the quay, where they faced curiosity and gossip about Paul's downfall.As Paul prepared to board, Mathias signaled him with a handkerchief, indicating he had important letters. However, the sailor delivering the letters did not convey their urgency. Paul, wanting to avoid being swayed by their contents, pocketed the letters unread.Once at sea, Paul reflected on his life and the choices that led him here. Overcome by seasickness, he spent days in his cabin contemplating his situation. Eventually, he opened the letters from Natalie and de Marsay.Natalie's letter expressed her anguish over Paul's departure and her desire to support him. She felt betrayed by his decision to leave without consulting her and vowed to economize to pay off his debts. She also revealed she believed she was pregnant, filling her with hope for their future.In contrast, de Marsay's letter detailed the extent of Paul's financial ruin, revealing that Natalie's mother had orchestrated much of the situation. De Marsay warned Paul of a conspiracy against him and urged him to return to Paris instead of going to India, suggesting he could regain his honor by confronting his rivals.As Paul absorbed the harsh realities of his situation, he felt anger and despair. He questioned what he had done to deserve such betrayal and turmoil. Ultimately, he resolved to confront his challenges head-on, determined to rebuild his life and restore his honor.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**A Start in Life**  **By Honoré De Balzac**  **Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley**  **DEDICATION**  To Laure.  Let the brilliant mind that inspired this scene have the honor of it.  Her brother,  De Balzac  ---**CHAPTER I. THAT WHICH WAS LACKING TO PIERROTIN’S HAPPINESS**  Railroads are set to render certain transportation industries around Paris obsolete. The Touchards, a father-son duo, monopolized travel within a 45-mile radius but faced stiff competition from local services, especially the coucous, which thrived on shorter routes. Despite their efforts, the Touchards struggled against smaller competitors who enjoyed local goodwill.One of the most contested routes was from Paris to Beaumont-sur-Oise, where the Touchards faced three rivals in 1822. The competition was fierce due to the line's profitability, serving several towns and villages. Although the Touchards extended their route to Chambly, competition remained strong.The road to Isle-Adam, known for its quarries and historical significance, was less competitive, with only two coaches operating harmoniously. Pierrotin, a well-liked coachman, and his rival coordinated their schedules to benefit customers. They operated from the same yard in Paris, the “Lion d’Argent,” where the innkeeper’s wife managed bookings and fares.On a Saturday morning in 1822, Pierrotin stood outside the inn, anxious about the lack of passengers. He had ordered a new coach to expand his business but was short on funds. Despite his ambitions, he faced financial strain, having exhausted his resources to secure the new vehicle. He hoped to attract more customers with the new coach, designed to rival the Touchards’ offerings.Pierrotin’s current vehicle, an inherited coucou, was cramped and often overloaded. He planned for a larger, more luxurious coach but needed to raise a thousand francs to complete the payment. As he contemplated his options, a footman approached, seeking a ride for his master, the Comte de Serizy, who wished to travel incognito.The footman revealed that the count had countermanded a carriage for unknown reasons, hinting at potential intrigue. Eager to please and hoping for a generous tip, Pierrotin agreed to the arrangement, despite the footman’s warning to keep the count’s identity secret from other passengers.As Pierrotin prepared for the journey, he reflected on the count’s steward, Monsieur Moreau, a man he respected and who often employed him for errands. This chapter sets the stage for a narrative exploring ambition, social dynamics, and the evolving transportation landscape in early 19th-century France, highlighting Pierrotin's struggles and aspirations as he navigates the complexities of his profession and relationships.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Monsieur Huguet de Serisy descends from the notable president Huguet, ennobled under François I. The family coat of arms features a party per pale or and sable, with the motto “i, semper melius eris,” reflecting the modesty of burgher families. The current count, a councillor of State at just twenty-two, distinguished himself during the Revolution by remaining on his estate, Serizy, where he was protected by his father's reputation. After his father's death in 1794, he was elected to the Council of the Five Hundred and later became a prominent figure under Napoleon, who appointed him to the Council of State and made him a count and senator.In 1806, he married the widow of a Republican general, significantly increasing his wealth. By 1814, due to health issues, he resigned from his posts and returned to Paris, maintaining loyalty to the Bourbons and refusing Napoleon's peerage during the Hundred Days. After Napoleon's second fall, he resumed his political duties, becoming vice-president of the Council of State and managing foreign claims without seeking personal glory. His life was marked by hard work, rising early and retiring early, earning numerous honors for his service.Despite his public success, the count suffered privately from unfulfilled love for his wife, who enjoyed considerable freedom and social engagement. He respected her autonomy, allowing her to manage her affairs while remaining devoted to her, even as his own health deteriorated. The count's dedication to work masked his emotional pain, stemming from jealousy and the complexities of their marriage.The narrative shifts to a pressing matter involving a farm encroaching upon the count's estate, Presles. The lease on this farm, held by a farmer named Leger, was expiring, and the owner, Margueron, refused to renew it. The count sought to purchase the farm to eliminate disputes. He consulted his notary and lawyer, who advised inviting Margueron to dinner to finalize the sale. The count planned to bring part of the purchase money and secure a government position for Margueron's son as part of the deal.Before this meeting, the count had initiated renovations at Presles, intending to surprise his wife. However, he decided to travel incognito to avoid drawing attention.Moreau, the steward of Presles, had a complex background. His father had saved the Serizys during the Revolution, and Moreau had been granted a position by the count after escaping a death sentence. Initially loyal and diligent, Moreau's character changed after becoming comfortable in his role, leading him to accept bribes and engage in dishonest dealings. He aimed to profit from the sale of the farm, planning to pocket a significant sum while maintaining the appearance of integrity.As the count prepared for his trip, Moreau conspired with Leger to inflate the farm's price, hoping to benefit from the transaction. Meanwhile, a retired officer, Monsieur de Reybert, and his wife, feeling wronged by Moreau, sought to expose his dishonesty to the count. Madame de Reybert approached the count, claiming that Moreau was scheming to defraud him in the farm purchase.Though initially skeptical, the count began to doubt Moreau's integrity after reading a letter from the steward that contradicted his assurances. He decided to investigate further, instructing his notary to accompany Madame de Reybert back to Presles and to delay sending his clerk as previously planned.Thus, the count's incognito journey and the warning from Madame de Reybert set the stage for a confrontation that could jeopardize Moreau's position and the count's interests.As Pierrotin left the Café de l’Echiquier, he spotted Madame Clapart and her son Oscar at the Lion d’Argent. The mother, in a worn black gown, anxiously searched for him, reflecting her devotion to Oscar, who was traveling alone for the first time. Oscar, dressed in a short olive-green coat and patched trousers, mirrored their modest means.“Is this the conductor? Ah! Pierrotin, is it you?” she exclaimed, leaving her son to speak with him privately. She asked Pierrotin to take good care of Oscar, who was heading to Monsieur Moreau’s place. Pierrotin hesitated to voice his concerns about the challenges Oscar would face.Pierrotin often transported goods for Moreau, who had a discreet arrangement with Madame Clapart, delivering baskets of food to her home, which puzzled him given their poverty. Madame Clapart lived in a modest apartment filled with old furniture but maintained a regal demeanor.Monsieur Clapart, her husband, was timid yet tyrannical, and their life was marked by hardship. Despite their struggles, Madame Clapart’s unwavering love for Oscar blinded her to his shortcomings. Raised in past splendor, Oscar now faced harsh realities, compounded by his stepfather’s disdain.Moreau, once close to Madame Clapart, had helped secure a scholarship for Oscar at a prestigious school. However, Oscar’s vanity and lack of self-awareness troubled Moreau, who wished to guide him toward a suitable career.As they prepared for the journey, Madame Clapart fussed over Oscar, reminding him to avoid discussing servants and to be respectful. Oscar, embarrassed by his mother’s attentiveness, longed for independence and felt humiliated by two better-dressed young men nearby, who mocked him for his mother’s affection.The young men, Georges and Amaury, engaged in playful banter, aggravating Oscar’s insecurities. He envied their confidence and style, feeling trapped by his circumstances. As the coach was readied, Pierrotin managed the logistics while the young men continued to tease Oscar, who tried to appear indifferent.Eventually, the coach filled with passengers, including a stout farmer named Pere Leger and the Comte de Serizy, who arrived last, disguised as a bourgeois to maintain his incognito amidst the youthful mockery.As the journey began, Georges expressed impatience with Pierrotin’s pace, while Mistigris, a young artist, made light of the situation. The count, overhearing their comments, remained composed, aware of the importance of keeping his identity hidden. The group’s dynamics shifted as they traveled, with Oscar grappling with feelings of inadequacy while others engaged in light-hearted exchanges, promising a mix of tension and camaraderie.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In public transport, passengers often begin in silence, observing one another until conversation starts, typically initiated by the most talkative. This is particularly true in French stage-coaches, known for their lively banter. Georges, a clever young man, quickly assessed his fellow travelers, feeling superior and planning to amuse himself at their expense.As the coach descended from La Chapelle, Georges contemplated various identities to entertain himself, ultimately deciding to pose as a disguised Russian prince. He engaged in playful banter with Mistigris, who was traveling with a count, spinning tales of his supposed adventures in the East, including serving under Ali, pacha of Janina.Georges claimed to have been a soldier, embellishing his past with stories of battles and decorations that intrigued the other passengers. He recounted experiences in Egypt and Greece, painting a picture of excitement. He mentioned being the grandson of Czerni-Georges, a historical figure who fought against the Ottoman Empire, and described his return to Greece, where he was welcomed by Ali Tebelen.As the conversation flowed, Georges boasted about his time in a harem, downplaying its significance while expressing a preference for French women. Upon arriving in Saint-Denis, they stopped at a tavern known for its cheese-cakes. Enjoying the attention of his fellow travelers, Georges paid for their treats and continued weaving his elaborate tales.The count, intrigued yet skeptical, suspected Georges was not who he claimed to be. He discovered a portfolio belonging to Georges, revealing him as Crottat’s second clerk, confirming his suspicions. Meanwhile, Georges continued to impress the others with his stories, even as the count remained wary.As they resumed their journey, Georges shared more about his supposed military exploits, claiming crucial roles in battles and lavish gifts from Ali Tebelen. He recounted misfortunes, including losing treasures to a Turkish admiral after being captured.The count, concerned about Georges’ reckless boasting, warned him about the consequences of speaking too freely about his past, especially regarding foreign intrigues. Undeterred, Georges entertained the group with tales of a dramatic encounter in Dalmatia involving a beautiful woman named Zena and a jealous pirate husband.Schinner, a painter traveling with the group, chimed in with his own stories of love and danger, recounting complications from his infatuation with Zena. The narrative shifted between Georges and Schinner, both vying for the attention of their fellow travelers while weaving intricate tales of their pasts.As the journey progressed, the count and others listened with a mix of skepticism and fascination, caught up in the drama of the stories. Georges and Schinner’s narratives intertwined, creating a tapestry of adventure and intrigue that captivated their audience, even as the count remained cautious about the truth behind their tales. Ultimately, the journey served as a backdrop for exploring identity, storytelling, and the allure of adventure, with each character revealing their desires through the stories they shared.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Pierrotin's coach arrived in Saint-Brice around 1:30 PM for a routine stop to rest his horses. “Ah! Here’s Pere Leger,” the innkeeper called as the coach halted. “Do you breakfast?”“Always once a day,” replied the farmer, eager to eat.“Give us a good breakfast!” Georges exclaimed, twirling his cane, impressing Oscar. However, Oscar's admiration turned to jealousy when Georges lit a cigar from a small case.“Do you smoke?” Georges asked Oscar.“Sometimes,” Oscar replied, trying to appear casual.Georges offered the case to Oscar and Schinner, who noted the cigars were cheap. Schinner declined breakfast, having eaten earlier, while Oscar claimed he had too. He envied Georges's carefree spending, calculating their trip costs.As they waited, Mistigris joked about their mismatched socks, prompting laughter from the count, who observed the young men with a mix of amusement and envy.The innkeeper and Pere Leger discussed a potential land deal involving the Comte de Serizy, hinting at a scheme to outmaneuver him. The count overheard, suspecting a conspiracy involving his steward, Moreau.Fearing indiscretion, Pierrotin promised the count to keep his identity secret in exchange for future payment. He decided not to join the others for breakfast, citing a delivery he needed to make.Pere Leger suggested harnessing a horse to the cabriolet and having breakfast before catching up with Pierrotin. The count was intrigued by the farmer's plans and potential profits.As Pierrotin drove away, Georges and the farmer discussed the landscape's beauty, while Oscar, feeling belittled, attempted to assert his future importance in diplomacy. His claims drew laughter from the others, who found his pretensions amusing.Oscar insisted he was friends with the Comte de Serizy's son, surprising Pere Leger. The count, intrigued, asked Oscar for information about the count, leading to Oscar revealing personal details about the count's life and health, shocking the count.The conversation turned to the countess, and the count interrupted, defending her honor. He then revealed his connections and left the coach at an inn, leaving the young men confused over their earlier bravado.As they approached the chateau de Presles, Pierrotin revealed he would drop off the young men at the estate. Oscar, eager to assert his status, rushed ahead, unaware of the path he took. Pere Leger pointed him in the right direction, while Georges slipped away, realizing the farmer's connection to Les Moulineaux.Pierrotin delivered the packages to the concierge, leaving the young men to contemplate their earlier claims and the reality of their situation. As Pierrotin drove away, he felt satisfied, knowing he would soon receive his payment and a new coach.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Oscar, feeling shy, hid behind trees in the courtyard as Monsieur Moreau appeared on the portico, dressed in a blue overcoat and carrying a riding whip. He greeted Oscar warmly and introduced himself to Mistigris and his master, the painters sent by architect Monsieur Grindot. Moreau instructed the concierge to show them to their rooms and invited them to dinner at five, mentioning hunting opportunities on the estate.As they walked through the park, Moreau sent his son Jacques to inform Madame Moreau of Oscar's arrival. Moreau, a stern-looking yet kind man, encouraged Oscar to learn horseback riding and hunting, despite Oscar's reluctance. He noted that Oscar's stay was limited to two weeks due to Madame Moreau's presence.The steward's house, built before the Revolution, was connected to the chateau and featured a salon, dining room, and a lavish boudoir for Madame Moreau. The Moreaus lived comfortably, relying on the estate's resources while maintaining an appearance of independence. Madame Moreau, a former maid, enjoyed her status and often dressed elegantly, seeking to be seen as a lady of the chateau rather than a steward's wife.Tensions existed between the Moreaus and the Reyberts, stemming from a past incident where Madame Reybert reminded the community of Madame Moreau's previous station. The Moreaus had cultivated relationships with local bourgeoisie, often hosting gatherings and playing cards with the rector and mayor.Madame Moreau eagerly awaited the painters, hoping to impress them and elevate her social standing. She dressed in her finest attire, but when the coach from Paris arrived, it only delivered Mistigris's packages, leaving her disappointed.When the artists finally arrived, Madame Moreau welcomed them and expressed her love for art, attempting to impress them with her knowledge. However, her past as a maid occasionally slipped through her facade. The artists, recognizing her pretensions, engaged her in conversation about art and flattery.Just as the atmosphere relaxed, a maid interrupted, announcing the count's unexpected arrival. This news shocked Madame Moreau, who worried about the implications of the count's visit. The count, having overheard Oscar's comments during the journey, was furious and confronted Moreau about the gossip, revealing his hurt feelings.Moreau, distressed, tried to explain that he had spoken of the count with affection, but the damage was done. The count decided to dismiss Moreau from his position, allowing him to keep his earnings but severing their relationship. He instructed Moreau to ensure a smooth transition to the new steward, Monsieur de Reybert.As the count prepared for dinner, the atmosphere grew tense. The arrival of various guests, including notaries and local dignitaries, added to the pressure. Mistigris, unaware of the gravity of the situation, made light-hearted comments, while clerk Georges Marest found himself in trouble for his earlier antics.Meanwhile, Moreau returned home, distraught over losing his position and its implications for his family. He confronted Oscar, blaming him for the situation and demanding an apology to the count. Overwhelmed with fear and shame, Oscar resisted, leading to a dramatic confrontation where Moreau forced him to apologize in front of the count.The count, unimpressed by Oscar's behavior, dismissed him, leaving Moreau to grapple with the consequences of the evening's events. The chapter concludes with Moreau taking Oscar home, contemplating the fallout from the count's visit and the future of their family.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################While preparing for a journey, Moreau wrote to Madame Clapart, expressing distress over Oscar's recent actions. During a trip, Oscar had carelessly disclosed sensitive information about Madame de Serizy to a high-ranking official, leading to Moreau's dismissal as steward of Presles. Moreau decided to send Oscar home immediately, feeling the weight of his family's future and his own ambitions. He lamented Oscar's behavior and questioned his intelligence, wishing he had received better guidance.That evening, Madame Clapart was knitting when her husband, Clapart, voiced concern that their friend Poiret might have visited while they were out. Clapart criticized Oscar, believing he would cause trouble at Presles. Madame Clapart defended her son, highlighting his good nature and potential, but Clapart dismissed her optimism, insisting Oscar was lazy and incapable.Suddenly, a carriage arrived, bringing Oscar home. Brochon, the stable-man, informed them that Moreau had lost his position due to Oscar's actions. Distressed, Madame Clapart fainted upon reading Moreau's letter. When she regained consciousness, she urged Oscar to go to bed, while Clapart continued to berate him.Despite the turmoil, Oscar slept soundly, unaware of the gravity of his situation. The next day, he felt hungry and surprised that life continued as normal. His mother, worried about their future without Moreau's support, explained the dire consequences of his actions. She emphasized the need for him to find a career, outlining limited options such as commerce, government work, or military service. She expressed fears about their financial instability and urged him to enlist in the army.Oscar, still naive about life's challenges, listened to his mother but struggled to grasp their reality. Her tears moved him, and she implored him to promise to be more discreet in the future. They discussed seeking help from Uncle Cardot, who had prospered in business and might assist Oscar.Uncle Cardot, a wealthy widower, lived modestly and had provided well for his children. Unaware of Madame Clapart and Oscar's struggles, he received them during their visit. She highlighted Oscar's achievements, hoping to gain his support. Cardot, while polite, was skeptical about Oscar's future and the family's needs.Madame Clapart revealed their financial struggles, explaining her sacrifices for Oscar's education. Moved by her plight, Cardot offered to help Oscar pursue a legal career, promising to cover his educational costs if he showed promise. He advised Oscar to work hard and maintain integrity, emphasizing the importance of discretion in a big city like Paris.After their visit, Madame Clapart felt hopeful for Oscar's future. Moreau later visited, having started a new venture in land speculation. He reassured her that Oscar could still have a successful career if he remained disciplined. Moreau proposed that Oscar work under a young lawyer named Desroches, who would provide the structure and guidance he needed. Madame Clapart was grateful for Moreau's continued support and urged Oscar to express his thanks. Moreau emphasized that the best way for Oscar to redeem himself was through hard work and proper conduct moving forward.Ten days later, Oscar was introduced by Monsieur Moreau to Maitre Desroches, a young solicitor with a modest office on rue de Bethisy. Desroches, who had risen from poverty, took a keen interest in Oscar, recognizing his own past struggles, though his stern demeanor intimidated him.Desroches informed his head clerk, Monsieur Godeschal, that Oscar would be living in the attic next to Godeschal’s room and would need strict supervision to excel in law school and learn the Code thoroughly. Godeschal, also from humble beginnings, was tasked with guiding Oscar, emphasizing hard work and integrity.For two years, Oscar lived a monk-like existence in the office, waking at five each morning to work alongside Godeschal. He performed errands, prepared for law school, and faced rigorous examinations from both Godeschal and Desroches. His daily routine included a simple dinner and continued work until nightfall. Oscar occasionally visited his uncle Cardot and spent Sundays with his mother, but he was often reprimanded for his attempts at elegance.Godeschal advised Oscar on maintaining a modest wardrobe and living frugally, citing Desroches as an example of success through hard work. Eighteen months into his tenure, Oscar became the third clerk, performing the duties of the second clerk without a salary but receiving room and board. By the end of his second year, he had gained significant legal knowledge and was performing well at the Palais.Despite his progress, Oscar felt trapped in a life that clashed with his desires. He envied the well-dressed young men he saw on the streets and longed for freedom and pleasure, which he suppressed under Godeschal's guidance. Godeschal, aware of Oscar's struggles, occasionally took him out for leisure to help him cope with the monotony of his life.In November 1825, after passing his exams, Oscar received funds from Moreau to upgrade his wardrobe and began working as the second clerk with a salary of eight hundred francs. His uncle Cardot promised to help him find a practice if he continued to excel.However, Oscar's internal conflict persisted. He often contemplated leaving his rigorous life, feeling more like a galley slave than a budding lawyer. Godeschal remained vigilant, ensuring Oscar was not exposed to temptations, as he often lacked money for excesses. Godeschal's occasional outings provided some relief from Oscar's otherwise dreary existence.When Godeschal returned from a trip, he reported to Moreau that Oscar was becoming vain, dressing too elegantly and seeking social adventures. He worried that Oscar's aspirations might not align with a legal career, despite his talent for conversation.Shortly after Oscar's promotion, a new clerk named Frederic Marest joined the office. Marest, a wealthy young man aspiring to become a magistrate, sought to learn the intricacies of legal practice. Godeschal announced Marest's arrival, hoping he would contribute to the office's camaraderie.The clerks engaged in a tradition of welcoming new members with a humorous register filled with fictitious cases and exaggerated accounts of past receptions. This playful spirit helped the clerks bond and relieve the monotony of their work.As Oscar settled into his role, he participated in the office's lighthearted pranks, including the ongoing joke surrounding the register. When Marest arrived, he quickly caught on to the humor, indicating he was not easily fooled.In summary, Oscar's journey through the legal world was marked by rigorous discipline, internal conflict, and camaraderie among his fellow clerks, all while navigating the challenges of ambition and societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The next day, Georges Marest, now head-clerk of notary Hannequin, visited Oscar, whom he recognized from a past incident. He invited Oscar and his colleagues to a lavish gathering at the Rocher de Cancale and later to an evening with Madame la Marquise de las Florentinas y Cabirolos, promising elegance and excitement.Oscar's superior, Desroches, instructed him to retrieve a crucial legal document, warning him about Derville, who might obstruct their efforts. Eager to prove himself as a second clerk, Oscar accepted the challenge.After Georges left, Godeschal questioned Frederic about the marquise, who continued the ruse, claiming she was a wealthy widow from Mexico. The clerks, excited by the prospect of mingling with high society, prepared for the event.The Marquise de las Florentinas y Cabirolos was actually Mademoiselle Agathe-Florentine Cabirolle, a successful danseuse at the Gaiete, supported by her benefactor, Pere Cardot. Over the years, Florentine had risen in status, enjoying a luxurious lifestyle and courting Georges, who had recently inherited a fortune.As the clerks anticipated the gathering, Oscar felt a mix of excitement and apprehension, particularly regarding Georges, who had previously caused him distress. Despite his reservations, he donned his new clothes and set out to enjoy the day.At the Rocher de Cancale, Oscar met various guests, including an old captain and a journalist. Georges charmed Oscar, downplaying past grievances. The feast was extravagant, and the clerks indulged in food and drink, leading to intoxication.Afterward, they headed to Florentine's salon, greeted by a dazzling array of women and opulence. Oscar was captivated by Florentine's transformation into a sophisticated marquise. He soon found himself drawn into a game of vingt-et-un with Fanny Beaupre, an actress, and lost significant money, including the five hundred francs entrusted to him by Desroches.Overwhelmed by his losses, Oscar contemplated despair but was reassured by Florentine, who offered him a thousand francs to recover. He accepted, hoping to regain his standing. However, as the night progressed, he lost even more, succumbing to the thrill of gambling and drink.By morning, Oscar was exhausted and fell asleep on a sofa, oblivious to the ongoing festivities. Florentine and her friends discussed the events, unaware of the trouble Oscar faced due to his reckless gambling. The night ended with Oscar still asleep, caught in the consequences of his choices.The next morning, Oscar Husson was awakened by his uncle Cardot, who scolded him for his late-night revelry and the state of the apartment, expressing disappointment that Oscar hadn’t informed him about the gathering. Florentine, a dancer, defended herself, claiming she had invited guests to please Cardot.As tensions escalated, Florentine revealed that Oscar had lost money gambling, prompting Cardot to chastise him for his recklessness. Distraught, Oscar begged Cardot for help in explaining the situation to his employer, Monsieur Desroches, fearing job loss. Florentine urged Cardot to assist Oscar, reminding him of their family ties.Reluctantly, Cardot gave Oscar five hundred francs, warning it would be the last time he would help him. Oscar left, uncertain of his next steps as he faced the consequences of his actions. Meanwhile, Mariette, concerned for Oscar, sent him a note and money to help recover his losses.Desroches, upon receiving the note, was furious with Oscar for his irresponsibility. Godeschal, Oscar's friend, defended him, suggesting that Oscar's vanity was leading him astray. Ultimately, Desroches decided to dismiss Oscar, believing he did not deserve further assistance.At home, Oscar's mother, Madame Clapart, learned of her son's troubles and was devastated. Clapart, her husband, mocked her distress, but she remained hopeful for Oscar's future. When Oscar returned home, he confronted Clapart, asserting his independence and rejecting his father's authority. Clapart's harsh words deepened Madame Clapart's sorrow.Feeling the weight of his mistakes, Oscar resolved to change his ways. He drew a military number, which determined his fate. Despite the challenges ahead, he hoped for a better future.Three days later, Oscar drew number twenty-seven, leading to his enlistment in the cavalry. His fortunes changed when he was assigned to a noble regiment, thanks to the influence of the Comte de Serizy. Overwhelmed by her son's misfortunes, Madame Clapart turned to religion for solace, believing her past sins caused her family's suffering.Oscar excelled in the military, rising through the ranks and earning honors for bravery during a battle in Africa. He saved the Vicomte de Serizy, solidifying his reputation and leading to further promotions.Years later, Oscar, now a decorated officer, traveled with his mother to Beaumont. They encountered Georges Marest, a former acquaintance whose life had taken a downward turn. Georges, unrecognizable and in poor condition, attempted to engage with Oscar, who had changed significantly since their last meeting.As they traveled, Oscar reflected on the changes in their lives. He had risen from his past mistakes, while Georges had fallen into obscurity. The journey brought back memories of their shared past, highlighting the stark contrast between their current circumstances.Oscar's military career continued to flourish, and he eventually married Georgette Pierrotin, the daughter of the coach owner. This union marked a new chapter in his life, as he learned from his past blunders and embraced a more responsible path.Through his experiences, Oscar matured into a respectable man, earning a position as a collector and later a receiver-general. His journey from a reckless youth to a responsible adult exemplified the lessons learned from his earlier mistakes, ultimately leading to a fulfilling life.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**Modeste Mignon: Summary****Dedication**  The book is dedicated to a Polish lady, celebrating her multifaceted nature as a daughter, angel, witch, and poet, enriching the narrative.**Chapter I: The Chalet**  In October 1829, Simon Babylas Latournelle, a notary, walks with his family and clerk, Jean Butscha, to the Chalet in Ingouville. Latournelle instructs his son Exupere to engage in a secretive plan involving Mademoiselle Modeste Mignon, hinting at her romantic inclinations. Madame Latournelle, proud of her lineage, is portrayed comically, while Exupere prepares to leave for Paris to study law. The Chalet, a charming cottage, is home to the Mignon family, who have fallen from wealth, contrasting sharply with Monsieur Mignon's former prosperity.**Chapter II: A Portrait from Life**  The Latournelles visit the Mignons and meet Gobenheim, a banker. Modeste, the center of attention, is embroidering beside her blind mother, Madame Mignon. Tension arises as Dumay, the cashier, prepares for a confrontation regarding Modeste's potential suitor, highlighting the dynamics of the Mignon family and their social standing.**Chapter III: Preliminaries**  Dumay's backstory reveals his loyalty to the Mignon family, having served under Colonel Mignon during the Napoleonic Wars. Their decline stems from financial ruin, leading to their life in the Chalet. Dumay's protective instincts towards Modeste are emphasized, as he vows to guard her honor against suitors.**Chapter IV: A Simple Story**  The Mignon family's downfall is detailed, including the tragic death of Bettina, Modeste's sister, which deeply affects them. The narrative explores societal gossip and the family's efforts to maintain dignity despite their reduced circumstances.**Chapter V: The Problem Still Unsolved**  Madame Mignon expresses concern about Modeste's emotional state, suspecting she may be in love. The family resolves to keep a close watch on her, leading to humorous and dramatic attempts to uncover Modeste's feelings.**Chapter VI: A Maiden’s First Romance**  Modeste's romantic fantasies reveal her desire for a love that transcends her mundane reality. She becomes enamored with the idea of a poetic, idealized love, influenced by her readings and her sister's tragic story. Her longing for a connection with a man of genius becomes a central theme.**Chapter VII: A Poet of the Angelic School**  Modeste writes to the poet Canalis, expressing her admiration. Canalis, flattered yet cynical, responds with a letter acknowledging her feelings while warning her of the dangers of pursuing a relationship with a poet. His response combines condescension and genuine concern, ultimately leading Modeste to feel disillusioned.Throughout the narrative, themes of love, societal expectations, and the contrast between reality and idealism are interwoven, showcasing Modeste's journey as she navigates her emotions and her family's complexities. The characters' interactions reflect societal norms of the time, while Modeste's inner turmoil highlights the struggles of young women seeking love and identity in a changing world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################To Monsieur de Canalis:Monsieur, you are a great poet and an honest man. After your candidness with a young girl in despair, can you answer me truthfully? Would your response to my letter have been the same if you had known that Mademoiselle O. d’Este has six million francs and seeks a fool for a husband? I admire your talent and integrity, which compel me to remain your humble servant, O. d’Este M.---Ernest de La Briere contemplated this letter as he walked the boulevards, feeling conflicted. While many might dismiss the girl as a hussy, her appeal to truth stirred his conscience. Honor, Truth, and Justice urged him to reflect on his feelings, and he recognized he would not have responded to a wealthy heiress with the same sincerity. Six days later, Modeste received a letter from him:Mademoiselle, you are not a D’Este. Your name is a disguise. Do I owe revelations to someone who is untruthful about herself? Are you of noble birth? Morality varies with social status. A daughter of Este may flaunt her wealth, but a daughter of the people would be ridiculed for doing so. Answer me sincerely, and I will respond to your question.---La Briere, shrewd yet honorable, acknowledged that had he known of her wealth, he might have acted differently. He confessed that most men equate happiness with wealth and would have pursued her for that reason. However, he valued honesty and wished to maintain his self-respect. He admitted to having followed Francoise Cochet to Ingouville, where he saw Modeste and was struck by her beauty. He resolved to continue their correspondence, despite the complications it might bring.---Modeste, buoyed by La Briere’s response, felt joy and confusion. She devised a plan to receive letters secretly, ensuring her correspondence remained hidden from her family. As she read La Briere’s letters, she felt a connection that transcended mere admiration for his poetry; she sensed a kindred spirit.---In her next letter, Modeste expressed her feelings openly, revealing her noble lineage and wealth. She implored La Briere to be truthful and to understand her heart, longing for a connection that was both poetic and profound, free from societal constraints.---Meanwhile, La Briere wrestled with his feelings, torn between admiration for Modeste and the reality of their social standings. He resolved to meet her father, the Comte de La Bastie, to confess his love and intentions.---As the story unfolds, Modeste’s family dynamics come into play. Her mother, Madame Mignon, is blind and frail, while her father, Charles Mignon, is a wealthy merchant returning from a successful venture. The arrival of the Duc d’Herouville, a potential suitor, complicates matters further, as Modeste grapples with her feelings for La Briere and societal expectations.---The tension escalates as Modeste prepares to meet her suitors, including La Briere and Canalis. Each man represents different aspects of her desires and fears, and she must navigate the complexities of love, ambition, and familial duty.---Ultimately, Modeste’s journey is one of self-discovery, as she learns to balance her romantic aspirations with the realities of her social world. The interplay of love, ambition, and societal expectations creates a rich tapestry of emotions, leading to a climax that will determine her fate and the fates of those around her. ---As the narrative progresses, the characters’ motivations and relationships deepen, revealing the intricate dance of love and ambition that defines their lives. Modeste’s struggle to assert her identity amidst the pressures of wealth and expectation becomes a central theme, culminating in a poignant exploration of what it means to love and be loved in a complex world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Balzac's works, rich in character and detail, do not depict real individuals directly. In "Beatrix," he draws on recognizable traits from notable figures of his time, such as Mademoiselle des Touches, reflecting George Sand, and characters like Beatrix and Claude Vignon, inspired by the Comtesse d’Agoult and Liszt.**I. A Breton Town and Mansion**  In Brittany, towns like Guerande remain largely untouched by modernity, preserving customs from the past. Guerande exemplifies historical essence through its well-preserved walls, moats, and medieval architecture, creating a picturesque yet melancholic atmosphere.Guerande's streets, unchanged for centuries, are now quiet, with a dwindling population. Its isolation has allowed it to maintain a unique character, where traditional Breton costumes and customs persist. The local economy relies on salt production, with Guerande salt being highly regarded. Despite its historical significance, the town is largely forgotten by the outside world, fostering tranquility and nostalgia.The du Guaisnic mansion, a symbol of the town's noble past, belongs to the ancient Guaisnic family, who trace their lineage back to feudal times. Once powerful, the family now struggles financially, receiving minimal income from their lands due to debts. Despite their diminished status, the du Guaisnics retain pride and importance within Guerande.The mansion showcases the architectural style of its time, with a grand entrance adorned with the family crest leading to a courtyard surrounded by stables and kitchens. Inside, exquisite woodwork and furnishings reflect the family's noble heritage, while tapestries and artifacts preserve the history of the du Guaisnics.The upper floors contain simple yet elegant rooms focused on functionality and tradition. The meticulously maintained garden, featuring fruit trees and a sun-dial, adds to the estate's charm. The mansion's design, influenced by Venetian architecture, highlights the artistry of the past, contrasting sharply with modern construction.Balzac emphasizes the connection between the physical environment and its inhabitants. The grandeur of the du Guaisnic mansion and Guerande serves as a backdrop for the characters' lives, illustrating how history and tradition shape identity. The narrative suggests that the values and customs of the past continue to resonate in the present, even as society evolves.In conclusion, "Beatrix" captures the essence of a fading world, where remnants of feudalism and the beauty of Breton culture endure amidst modernity's encroachment. Balzac's portrayal of Guerande and the du Guaisnic family serves as a poignant reminder of the importance of heritage and the impact of time on human lives.In May 1836, the Guenic family included Baron du Guenic, his wife Fanny O’Brien, his sister Mademoiselle Zephirine, and their son, Calyste. The baron, a veteran of the Vendee wars, had sold his property to his sister before fighting against Napoleon. After years in exile in Ireland, he returned to Guerande in 1813 and married Fanny, who gave birth to Calyste on the day Louis XVIII landed in France.At seventy-three, the baron appeared older, with deep wrinkles and a proud expression reflecting a life of hardship. Illiterate yet steadfast, he embodied the loyalty of his Breton heritage, dedicating himself to the royal cause without seeking personal gain.Mademoiselle Zephirine, blind and devoted to her brother, refused surgery to maintain her independence and avoid financial burden. Their bond exemplified selflessness, as she managed the household with care and frugality.Family discussions centered on the exiled Bourbons and their Catholic faith, with Calyste representing their hopes for the future. The baron trained him in manly pursuits, preparing him for potential military service. When the Duchesse de Berry sought to reclaim the throne, the baron took Calyste to join her cause, returning home after three months without informing his anxious family.Upon their return, the baron expressed disappointment in their failed efforts, reflecting the stoic nature of the Breton character, which avoided complaints about adversaries. His recent defeat weighed heavily on him, contributing to his increasing fatigue.Fanny, the baroness, was a striking figure at forty-two, embodying grace and beauty. Her nurturing presence and devotion to her family were evident in her care for the baron and her efforts to maintain her appearance for his sake.Despite her blindness, Mademoiselle Zephirine maintained a strong presence in the household, knitting and listening to news read by Fanny. The household was characterized by unity and shared purpose, with loyal servants Gasselin and Mariotte contributing to the family’s well-being. Overall, the Guenic family exemplified a deep commitment to their heritage, with each member playing a vital role in preserving their legacy amidst the challenges of their time.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As night fell, Gasselin approached the baron, who allowed him to leave after prayers. The baron, his sister, and Madame du Guenic knelt for prayer, followed by a visit from Monsieur Grimont, the local priest. Grimont, jovial, inquired about the baroness's health and noted Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel's tardiness. Mariotte, a servant, warned against discussing the chevalier's visits to Les Touches in her presence due to potential gossip.Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, a thin, elderly woman in a green bonnet, managed her estate frugally, earning respect for her avarice. Despite her wealth, the du Guenics refrained from courting her, maintaining pride that kept them from seeking her fortune. She aimed to marry her niece to Calyste du Guenic to reclaim lost family lands.Rector Grimont revealed that Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel had recently donated a significant sum to support the royal cause, showcasing her loyalty. She treated Calyste with entitlement, believing her intentions granted her oversight over him. While not strict about romance, she disapproved of modern morals, favoring traditional values.As the evening progressed, Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel inquired about Monsieur du Halga's attendance. The rector confirmed he would join them, prompting excitement for their game of _mouche_. The chevalier, a former naval officer now in poor health, arrived, reflecting a softer side of his once intrepid character.The trio—Grimont, du Halga, and Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel—had spent years gathering at the hotel de Guenic, where the du Guenics maintained their noble status amidst changing political tides, steeped in tradition and social hierarchy complexities in Brittany._Mouche_ is a card game where players receive five cards, with one designated as trumps. Players can choose to play or abstain, with low stakes involved. Winning a trick allows players to earn from the pot, while losers pay the full stake, increasing the pot for the next round. Players can exchange cards from the remaining deck, and the dealer can swap the trump card for one in their hand. The most powerful card is Mistigris, the knave of clubs.Despite its simplicity, _mouche_ captivates players, revealing human greed and strategic thinking. At the hotel du Guenic, players contribute twenty counters each, a significant amount for the group. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel and Mademoiselle Zephirine view the game as a financial venture. A convention from 1825 allows players to leave after losing ten sous, but clever players like du Halga find ways to keep the game going by offering counters to those who have lost.The game intensifies with the arrival of Demoiselle de Kergarouet and her aunt, who encourage higher stakes. The baroness's blind sister discreetly signals safe betting. Evenings often blend card play with discussions about local events, leading to disputes over missing counters, which the chevalier often covers, raising suspicions.As the evening progresses, conversations shift to personal matters, including the chevalier's health and the recent death of the Duc de Lenoncourt. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel shares news about the Comtesse de Kergarouet's marriage, leading to gossip about Mademoiselle des Touches, a controversial figure.Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel expresses concern over Calyste's affection for Mademoiselle des Touches, an actress with a questionable reputation. The rector warns that her influence could lead Calyste astray. The baroness, worried about her son’s future, acknowledges the potential dangers of his infatuation.The evening concludes with the baroness and rector discussing Calyste's situation. The rector reassures her, promising to investigate further, while the baroness laments her son's loss of innocence and hopes for his safe return.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################The baroness grew increasingly anxious about her son Calyste's frequent absences and his attachment to Mademoiselle des Touches, fearing that modern ideas would corrupt him. Raised with strict maternal guidance and a devout Catholic education, Calyste was expected to marry a wealthy heiress to restore the family's fortunes. However, his infatuation with Mademoiselle des Touches, whom the baroness viewed as morally questionable, jeopardized these plans.When Calyste returned home, the baroness felt joy mixed with anxiety, noticing his fatigue and sensing he was hiding something. Despite her worries, she hesitated to confront him about Mademoiselle des Touches, fearing the loss of her son to love.Calyste's sheltered upbringing emphasized traditional education, shielding him from modern literature. His mother believed he was protected from worldly influences through his Christian education. However, his encounters with Mademoiselle des Touches exposed him to new ideas, alarming his mother.The next day, Calyste overslept, indulged by his mother. During dinner preparations, the baroness voiced her concerns about his visits to Les Touches, fearing Mademoiselle des Touches would corrupt him. Calyste dismissed her worries, reminiscing about his youthful escapades.When he revealed that Mademoiselle des Touches had rejected his romantic advances, he defended her character, describing her as a generous friend and artist. The baroness remained unconvinced, believing a woman's conduct should align with societal and religious expectations. Calyste's passionate defense of Mademoiselle des Touches only deepened her fears for his future.Mademoiselle Felicite des Touches, known as Camille Maupin, was a celebrated writer whose unconventional life sparked gossip in Guerande. An orphan, her rise to literary fame was marked by personal tragedies and a rejection of traditional female roles. Raised in a convent and later by a distant relative, she developed a strong intellect and passion for literature, becoming a prominent figure in Parisian society.Camille's early life was shaped by loss and independence. After her family perished during the French Revolution, she was raised in a convent, where she cultivated her love for reading. Her unorthodox education allowed her to develop a unique perspective on life. By eighteen, she was both beautiful and intellectually formidable, yet struggled to find fulfillment in social interactions.Rejecting conventional expectations of women, including marriage and motherhood, Camille sought to carve out a space in the literary world to express her creativity without traditional constraints. Her relationships with men were complex, marked by love and betrayal, shaping her identity as a writer.Camille Maupin's literary career began with plays that challenged societal norms and showcased her talent. Her works sparked debates about gender and creativity. Despite her success, she often wrote under a male pseudonym to navigate the male-dominated literary landscape.Her Paris salon became a hub for artists and intellectuals, where she mingled with influential figures and engaged in discussions about art and society. Camille's independence and wealth allowed her to explore her passions without the limitations imposed on women. However, her desire for freedom came at a cost, as she grappled with the complexities of love and companionship.As she aged, Camille became increasingly aware of societal pressures surrounding marriage and motherhood. Despite her reluctance to conform, she found herself drawn to love, leading to a tumultuous emotional journey. Her relationship with Calyste, captivated by her intellect and beauty, further complicated her understanding of love and desire.The intertwined lives of Calyste and Camille Maupin reflect tensions between societal expectations and personal desires. The baroness's protective instincts clash with Calyste's burgeoning independence, while Camille's struggle for identity as a woman in a patriarchal society highlights the challenges faced by women artists. Their stories comment on the complexities of love, ambition, and the pursuit of happiness in a changing world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################A few hundred yards from Guerande, Brittany transitions into salt-marshes and sandy dunes, leading to Les Touches, a country house surrounded by twisted pine trees. Built from slaty stone and granite, the estate overlooks the marshes and sea, generating income despite losing its feudal rights during the Revolution. Felicite, the owner, restored the house while maintaining its desolate appearance, adding only decorative elements at the entrance.The ground floor includes a billiard room, a large salon, and a dining room, all simply yet elegantly furnished. The upper floor features Felicite's suite, decorated with luxurious Louis XV items, while the guest rooms are modestly furnished. The melancholic landscape, with salt ponds and the distant ocean, evokes a sense of sublime longing.Calyste, a young man captivated by Felicite, finds solace at Les Touches, where art, music, and literature awaken his intellect. His admiration for Felicite deepens despite her rejections. She teaches him music, and he becomes enchanted by the artistic atmosphere, contrasting sharply with his dull life in Guerande.One day, Calyste hears Felicite playing the piano and finds her in tears, waiting for Claude Vignon. Concerned, he offers to search for Claude, but Felicite insists he stay, revealing her emotional turmoil about love and relationships, lamenting her lack of deep affection.Felicite reflects on her age difference with Calyste and her past rejections of him, acknowledging her own selfishness. Despite her struggles, she hopes to inspire greatness in Claude, whom she believes longs for Paris. She suggests that Calyste should intoxicate Claude at dinner to test his feelings for her.As they converse, Felicite reveals her longing for a fulfilling relationship. Moved by her vulnerability, Calyste offers to return her fortune to her heirs if she cannot accept his love. Overwhelmed, Felicite contemplates her choices. Determined to be honest, she prepares a narghile and tea, inviting Calyste to share an intimate moment, highlighting the complexity of their relationship amid unfulfilled desires.Camille narrates the story of the Marquise de Rochefide, soon to arrive. Born Beatrix-Maximilienne-Rose de Casteran, she comes from a noble family that prioritized advantageous marriages. Beatrix, known for her originality and artistic inclinations, married the foolish Rochefide, who misinterpreted her independence as coldness. This misunderstanding led him to retreat into bachelor life, believing Beatrix would be satisfied with superficial social engagements.Rochefide's vanity and jealousy complicated their relationship, especially as Beatrix admired Camille, who hosted a prominent Parisian salon. Beatrix attempted to create her own salon, attracting superficial acquaintances but failing to build a genuine social circle. Despite her talents and intelligence, she struggled against the constraints of her marriage and societal expectations.Initially, Beatrix's marriage seemed promising but quickly soured due to Rochefide's inability to appreciate her depth. She became enamored with Gennaro Conti, a captivating composer, leading to a passionate affair complicated by his duplicitous nature. Camille reflects on her own relationship with Conti, recognizing his charm and manipulative tendencies. She warns Beatrix about the dangers of her infatuation, but Beatrix, blinded by love, chooses to follow her heart and leaves Rochefide for Conti, seeking a life filled with artistic passion despite the risks.As they travel through Italy, Beatrix writes to Camille about her struggles with societal expectations and her loss of personal freedom. She longs for solitude and the beauty of nature, feeling the weight of her choices. Despite her love for Conti, she grapples with feelings of inferiority and the sacrifices made for their relationship.The story also features Claude Vignon, a cynical critic, whose interactions with Camille and Calyste reveal the complexities of desire and ambition. Calyste, torn between his provincial upbringing and the allure of Parisian life, becomes enamored with Beatrix, struggling with familial expectations as his mother wishes him to marry a suitable Breton girl.The narrative culminates in Beatrix's impending visit to Les Touches, promising to disrupt the delicate balance of relationships among the characters. As they await her arrival, the tension between love, ambition, and societal norms unfolds, leaving the characters to navigate their desires and the consequences of their choices. Overall, "La Marquise Beatrix" explores themes of love, ambition, and women's struggles in a patriarchal society.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Calyste, a young Breton man, is eager yet anxious to visit Croisic and meet Madame de Rochefide. He leaves home early to avoid detection by his perceptive family. Upon reaching the shore, he sees boats from Croisic, one carrying luggage marked with the marquise's name, intensifying his desire and sense of connection to her, convinced he is destined to love her.While waiting, he overhears a maid and footman discussing the lack of transportation from Les Touches and learns that Madame de Rochefide is already there. He hurries to Les Touches, consumed by thoughts of the marquise, contrasting sharply with his past feelings for Camille, who had rejected him.Later, Calyste meets the Chevalier du Halga and Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, who speculate about a romance between him and Charlotte de Kergarouet, a notion that repulses him as his heart is now set on Beatrix. Returning home, his mother gives him a letter from Camille inviting him to celebrate the marquise's arrival.As Calyste walks to Les Touches, he feels excitement and anxiety. Upon arrival, he finds the company in Camille's salon, where he meets Gennaro Conti, a poet resembling Lord Byron. Calyste is captivated by Beatrix, who appears even more beautiful than he imagined, overwhelming him with attraction.During dinner, he struggles to engage, feeling overshadowed by Beatrix and Conti. The conversation about Camille's travels deepens his admiration for Beatrix while he grapples with feelings of inadequacy. Mademoiselle des Touches advises him to control his emotions, but he feels hopeless about winning Beatrix's affection.After dinner, Camille and Conti perform a duet, stirring deep emotions in Calyste, who is moved to tears. Beatrix notices his reaction, becoming aware of his feelings. As the evening progresses, Calyste retreats to a sofa, overwhelmed by his thoughts and unrequited love for the marquise.Claude Vignon enters the bedroom and takes Calyste's hand, sensing his turmoil over love and rejection. He reassures Calyste that he will soon be free to pursue love, hinting that Camille has feelings for him. Conflicted between his affections for Beatrix and Camille, Calyste struggles with his emotions, overwhelmed by the revelation of Camille's love just as he recognizes his own deep feelings for Beatrix.As midnight approaches, Calyste overhears a conversation between Claude and Camille, where Claude reveals Camille's past feelings for Calyste and her fears about love leading to despair. He criticizes her for using him as a shield against her true feelings. Camille expresses sorrow over her unrequited love and the pain of knowing she could never be truly loved in return. Claude acknowledges Camille's greatness but warns her that her strength could repel men.Calyste, still reeling from the revelations, feels a mix of pity and admiration for Camille. He kisses her hands, and she implores Claude not to abandon her. Claude reassures her that Calyste's love for Beatrix will protect her from heartbreak. As Calyste leaves, he feels a sense of loss for Camille, despite his growing feelings for Beatrix.The next day, Calyste learns that Camille's carriage has passed, prompting him to ride to Saint-Nazaire.
