Charles feels out of place in his uncle's shabby home and is warned about the ferocious dog, questioning why his father sent him there.As Charles settles in, he reflects on his visit's purpose. Meanwhile, Eugenie, Grandet's daughter, becomes aware of her feelings for Charles while preparing for the day. She admires her appearance but doubts her beauty, feeling unworthy of him.Eugenie's morning is consumed with thoughts of Charles, and she asks Nanon, the servant, to prepare a special breakfast to impress him. Grandet, however, is stingy and engages in a comical argument with Nanon over provisions. Despite his frugality, he reluctantly allows Nanon to make a galette for the young ones.Later, Grandet takes Eugenie for a walk, where they meet Cruchot, the notary. Their conversation reveals Grandet's disdain for the idea of marrying Eugenie to Charles, stating he would rather throw her into the Loire than allow such a union. This devastates Eugenie, who has begun to develop feelings for Charles.Grandet soon receives news of his brother's suicide, shocking the family. Eugenie is deeply affected, feeling compassion for Charles, who remains unaware of his father's fate. Grandet insists Eugenie should not inform Charles until he returns, revealing his cold-heartedness.Overwhelmed, Eugenie confides in her mother, who understands but urges her to respect her father's wishes. Despite the tension, Eugenie expresses her affection for Charles and desires to prepare a special breakfast for him, highlighting her growing attachment amid financial hardship and emotional turmoil.After two hours of anxious preparation, Eugenie creates an unusual breakfast for Charles, deviating from their typical meal of bread, fruit, and wine. She sets the table carefully, worried about her father's reaction if he returned to find such a spread. Madame Grandet reassures her, promising to take the blame if necessary. When Charles arrives, flamboyantly dressed, he greets them cheerfully despite the recent destruction of his family estate.As they eat, Charles expresses a desire for more extravagant food, making Eugenie wish she could provide him with a partridge. Their conversation highlights the contrast between Charles's Parisian lifestyle and Eugenie's simple country life. Charles compliments Eugenie, causing her to blush, and they share a moment of connection. However, the mood shifts when Nanon brings in coffee, prompting Charles to comment on their outdated brewing method.The atmosphere darkens with the familiar knock of Monsieur Grandet. The women panic, quickly hiding evidence of the breakfast. Grandet enters, scrutinizing the scene and chastising them for the lavish meal, unaware of its significance to Charles. As he prepares his own meager breakfast, he notices the sugar and questions its source, leading to a tense silence.Charles, oblivious to the tension, inquires about his father's health, only to learn of his father's suicide. The revelation shatters him, prompting sympathy from Eugenie and her mother, while Grandet remains detached, focusing on financial implications. Moved by Charles's grief, Eugenie feels a growing affection for him, encouraging him to think of his honor as they sew in silence, contemplating wearing mourning for her uncle, indicating her deepening connection to Charles and their shared grief.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################At around four o'clock, a sharp knock startled Madame Grandet. She felt relieved when her husband, Grandet, entered joyfully, announcing he had sold his wine to a Belgian for a good profit. However, when Eugenie suggested helping her grieving cousin Charles, Grandet's mood soured. He scolded her for even considering spending money on him, threatening to send her away if she mentioned it again.Consumed by thoughts of investments, Grandet retreated to his secret den to calculate his wealth. Meanwhile, Charles mourned in his room, refusing to eat. Grandet dismissed the idea of mourning attire, insisting true mourning was internal. The family dinner was tense, with Madame Grandet suggesting they wear mourning, but Grandet scoffed at the expense.Feeling compassion for Charles, Eugenie secretly visited him, offering comfort. She admired his handsome face, now marked by grief, awakening a desire for love she had never known.The next morning, Grandet's demeanor shifted; he appeared more thoughtful and less harsh. He planned to manipulate the situation to his advantage, intending to help Charles while safeguarding his financial interests. He devised a scheme to impress the townspeople without spending his own money.Eugenie busied herself caring for Charles, bringing him breakfast and attending to his needs. Their bond deepened as they shared tender moments amidst their sorrows. Grateful for her kindness, Charles began to see Eugenie in a new light, appreciating her innocence and compassion.As the day progressed, Grandet prepared to host a dinner for the Cruchots, signaling a rare moment of generosity. The household buzzed with activity, and Charles, though still grieving, joined them with quiet dignity. The atmosphere was charged with unspoken emotions, as Eugenie and Charles exchanged glances filled with understanding and hope.At this moment, the town of Saumur was more focused on Grandet's dinner for the Cruchots than on the previous night's controversial wine sale. Grandet, a shrewd miser, disregarded public opinion, viewing himself as superior to the community he often deceived.The des Grassins family learned of Guillaume Grandet's death and decided to visit that evening to express condolences and understand his motives for inviting the Cruchots. The dinner atmosphere was somber, with Grandet grave, Charles silent, and Eugenie and Madame Grandet saying little. After dinner, Charles excused himself to write letters, allowing Grandet to speak privately with the Cruchots.Grandet, known for his cunning, used his stutter and feigned deafness to manipulate the conversation, avoiding responsibility. He discussed bankruptcy with Monsieur C. de Bonfons, who tried to guide him through insolvency complexities. While pretending to be confused, Grandet assessed how to navigate his brother's financial troubles without damaging his own reputation.The Cruchots suggested that Grandet could liquidate his brother's affairs to preserve family honor. However, Grandet expressed reluctance to engage in matters outside his expertise, insisting he was merely a wine-grower focused on his vines. The conversation shifted to the possibility of buying his brother's debts at a reduced rate, which intrigued Grandet but seemed complicated.As the discussion progressed, the notary cautioned against compromising integrity. Just then, the des Grassins arrived, offering condolences for the Grandet family's misfortunes. The notary seized the moment to assert that Grandet intended to liquidate his brother's debts honorably, surprising the des Grassins, who had expected selfishness.Impressed by Grandet's supposed nobility, the des Grassins offered to help manage the financial situation, but Grandet preferred to work with them rather than the Cruchots. He confided in the banker about his interest in investing in government bonds, indicating a desire to profit while maintaining a façade of familial duty.After the des Grassins left, Grandet instructed his servant Nanon to prepare for a secretive journey to sell his gold in Angers, taking advantage of rising prices due to military preparations. His meticulousness ensured that no one suspected his true intentions as he departed under the cover of night. Grandet's dinner highlighted his manipulation of those around him, reflecting a complex interplay of greed, deception, and the desire to uphold a facade of honor amidst personal gain.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Eugenie stood at the top of the stairs, reflecting on her father’s departure. The house was silent until she heard a cry from her cousin Charles's room. Concerned, she rushed upstairs to find him asleep in an armchair, exhausted from writing letters. She noticed addresses of various businesses, realizing he was preparing to leave France. Her heart sank upon spotting a letter addressed to "My dear Annette," igniting feelings of jealousy and despair.Despite her resolve not to read it, curiosity overwhelmed her. The letter revealed Charles's tragic situation: his father had committed suicide, leaving him destitute. He expressed his determination to seek fortune in the Indies, lamenting that he could not return to Paris, where he would face scorn as the son of a bankrupt. Eugenie felt compassion for him and vowed to help.Continuing to read, she discovered that Charles had no money for his journey or even a proper outfit. He contemplated sailing as a common sailor if necessary. The letter also revealed his deep love for Annette but his decision to part ways for her sake, believing it would be better for both. This filled Eugenie with joy, interpreting it as a sign of his love for her.Eugenie then found another letter addressed to Alphonse, where Charles detailed his financial ruin and requested assistance in settling his affairs. Moved by his situation, Eugenie decided to offer him her savings, nearly six thousand francs. She approached Charles, who was initially hesitant to accept her gift.Eugenie insisted, kneeling before him and imploring him to take the money. Overcome with emotion, Charles eventually agreed, recognizing their deep bond. He then revealed a family heirloom, a dressing case containing portraits of his parents, asking her to keep it safe. Their exchange solidified their connection, transcending mere familial ties.As they shared this intimate moment, both felt a shift in their relationship. Charles began to see hope in their bond, while Eugenie vowed to support him. The next day, they spent time together, their mutual affection growing stronger amidst family struggles.Eugenie's father, Grandet, returned home with news of his financial dealings, having profited from the sale of gold. His jovial demeanor contrasted sharply with Charles's somber reality, highlighting disparities in their lives. As the day unfolded, tension between the characters became palpable. Charles grappled with his newfound responsibilities, while Eugenie remained steadfast in her support. Their relationship blossomed against societal expectations, setting the stage for future challenges. Ultimately, both found solace in each other, their bond deepening as they navigated love, loss, and harsh realities.The next day, the Grandet family gathered for breakfast, creating a scene of domestic intimacy. Grief united Madame Grandet, Eugenie, and Charles, while the old man, focused on his avarice, remained indifferent. He busied himself with his land and vineyards, allowing the young couple to express their feelings under Madame Grandet's watchful eye.Eugenie experienced the awakening of love, having shared a secret with Charles that deepened their bond. Their relationship blossomed amidst mourning, as they found joy in each other's company, exchanging tender glances and words. Charles, who had known only tumultuous love, now embraced a pure affection for Eugenie and even the old house, which he had once found ridiculous.After breakfast, while Grandet worked, Charles enjoyed the simple pleasure of being with Eugenie and her mother, appreciating their beauty. Eugenie became his ideal, reminiscent of Goethe's Margaret before her fall. Their innocent love was tinged with the awareness of impending separation.Three days after des Grassins' departure, Grandet took Charles to the Civil courts to renounce his rights to his father's estate, a painful act for Charles. He also arranged for a passport and sold his belongings, which pleased Grandet. Dressed in mourning clothes, Charles appeared more mature, and the family attended a Requiem for Guillaume Grandet.During breakfast, Charles received letters from Paris, prompting Eugenie to inquire about his affairs. Grandet, irritated by the question, insisted on privacy. Later, in the garden, Charles revealed that he was preparing to leave for the Indies and expressed concern for Eugenie's future, suggesting she might find happiness elsewhere.Eugenie declared her love for him, and they shared a passionate kiss, interrupted by Nanon. As Charles prepared to leave, Grandet showed unexpected generosity, taking an interest in his nephew's departure and arranging for his belongings to be sent to Nantes.On the day of Charles's departure, sadness filled the air. Nanon, usually cheerful, shed tears for him. The family escorted Charles to the diligence, where Grandet offered parting words of encouragement. As the coach departed, Eugenie wished for divine power to keep Charles close.Meanwhile, Grandet engaged in financial dealings in Paris, acquiring a significant fortune through investments. His cunning allowed him to manage his brother's debts while maintaining his reputation. Over time, creditors grew impatient, but Grandet skillfully delayed payments, leveraging their changing moods to his advantage.As months passed, many creditors forgot their claims, and Grandet continued to manipulate the situation, ultimately selling his investments for a substantial profit. Des Grassins, now living in Paris, faced personal troubles, while Grandet distanced himself from any obligation to him.In the aftermath of Charles's departure, Eugenie faced profound loneliness. She kept Charles's room as it was, hoping for his return. Her beauty transformed, reflecting the depth of her love. She followed his journey on a world map and mourned his absence while maintaining a facade of cheerfulness around others.Eugenie's love became a constant presence in her life, overshadowing her daily activities. Nanon, sympathetic to her plight, expressed frustrations about love, highlighting the sacrifices women make. Despite the pain, Eugenie's devotion to Charles remained unwavering as she navigated her emotions in a world that felt increasingly empty without him.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Two months passed, and the once monotonous life of Eugenie and her mother, Madame Grandet, was now filled with anxiety over a shared secret involving Charles, Eugenie's cousin. Eugenie often gazed at her aunt's portrait, searching for traces of Charles in her features. One Sunday, her mother discovered Eugenie's secret exchange of gold for Charles's affection, leading to panic about how they would face Grandet on New Year’s Day when he would expect to see Eugenie's gold.As the new year approached, dread consumed the women. Madame Grandet, feeling the weight of her husband's anger and their precarious situation, suggested they avoid confronting him directly. On January 1, 1820, the cold winter morning prompted Madame Grandet to request a fire in her room, hoping to delay their visit to Grandet. Surprisingly, he agreed, displaying an unusual good humor that bewildered both women.Grandet's joy stemmed from a successful financial speculation, having received a substantial sum from Monsieur des Grassins. He reveled in his newfound wealth, oblivious to the turmoil within his family. As he prepared for breakfast, he expressed a desire to see Eugenie's gold, heightening the tension. Eugenie, feeling the weight of her secret, suggested they eat first to postpone the inevitable confrontation.After breakfast, Grandet pressed Eugenie for her gold, insisting she return it. When she claimed she no longer had it, he erupted in anger, accusing her of deceit and theft. Desperate to protect her mother from Grandet's wrath, Eugenie fell at her mother's feet, pleading for mercy. Madame Grandet, weakened by the confrontation, was taken to bed, leaving Eugenie to face her father alone.Grandet's fury escalated as he demanded to know what Eugenie had done with her gold. She maintained her silence, asserting her independence. Grandet, unable to comprehend her defiance, threatened to confine her to her room until she submitted to his authority. Heartbroken, Eugenie retreated to her mother, who tried to comfort her despite her own suffering.The confrontation reached a boiling point, with Grandet cursing Eugenie and accusing her of giving her fortune to Charles. He refused to forgive her, insisting she confess where her gold had gone. Caught between her husband and daughter, Madame Grandet implored him to show compassion, but Grandet remained resolute in his anger.As the evening wore on, Grandet dined alone for the first time in years, while Nanon, the servant, tried to comfort Eugenie. The tension in the household was palpable, with Madame Grandet's health deteriorating under the strain of the conflict. The arrival of guests for New Year’s festivities only highlighted the absence of the women, raising suspicions about the family's troubles.In the quiet of the night, Nanon brought Eugenie a special treat, a hare pâté, determined that she would not suffer on dry bread alone. This small act of kindness amidst the turmoil provided a glimmer of hope for Eugenie, who felt the weight of her choices and the love she held for Charles. The family dynamics, marked by love, fear, and the struggle for power, set the stage for a tragic unfolding of events in their lives.For months, the old wine-grower, Grandet, visited his wife’s room without mentioning their daughter, Eugenie, who was kept under harsh conditions. As Madame Grandet's health declined, townspeople speculated about the family's situation, eventually learning that Eugenie was being fed only bread and water by her father's orders. Despite the town's condemnation, Eugenie remained unaware of the gossip, focused on her love for Charles and her ailing mother.As her death approached, Madame Grandet urged reconciliation between father and daughter, but Grandet remained indifferent. She confided in the Cruchots about Eugenie's treatment, prompting them to consider legal action against Grandet. However, Eugenie insisted on loyalty to her father, asking them to remain silent to protect family honor.With spring nearing, Madame Grandet's condition worsened, and she sought help from the notary, Cruchot, who warned Grandet that if his wife died, Eugenie would inherit her mother’s estate, potentially forcing him to sell his property. This revelation shocked Grandet, who had never considered the legal implications of his daughter's inheritance. Cruchot advised him to treat Eugenie kindly to avoid conflict over the estate.Realizing the gravity of the situation, Grandet decided to reconcile with Eugenie. However, upon entering their home, he found Eugenie and her mother admiring a dressing-case filled with gold given to Eugenie by Charles. Driven by greed, Grandet attempted to seize the gold, leading to a confrontation where Eugenie threatened self-harm if he did not relent. In a moment of hesitation, Grandet backed down, allowing Eugenie to keep the dressing-case. He then attempted to show affection towards both his wife and daughter, scattering coins on the bed and promising to care for them. Despite his efforts, Madame Grandet passed away peacefully, leaving Eugenie to navigate a world filled with selfishness and sorrow. In her final moments, Madame Grandet imparted a poignant message about happiness, emphasizing that it lies in heaven.The day after her mother’s death, Eugenie felt a renewed attachment to her childhood home, despite its painful memories. Her father, M. Grandet, showed unexpected tenderness, which soon revealed itself as a façade for his self-serving agenda.During a family dinner with notary Maitre Cruchot, M. Grandet informed Eugenie that she was now her mother’s heiress. He pressured her to renounce her rights to the estate, claiming it would spare him taxes and the burden of an inventory. Confused, Eugenie agreed, believing it would ease her father’s worries. M. Grandet promised her a monthly allowance of a hundred francs, which he never fulfilled, instead giving her a few jewels.In the following years, M. Grandet trained Eugenie in managing the household and his finances, instilling his miserly habits in her. Their lives became monotonous, marked by daily routines devoid of joy. Eugenie’s only social interactions were with the Cruchot family and their friends, who played whist with her father. As M. Grandet aged and became frail, Eugenie devoted herself to caring for him after he suffered a stroke.M. Grandet’s death was uneventful, overshadowed by his obsession with hidden wealth. In his final moments, he expressed concern for his treasures rather than for Eugenie. After his passing, she learned from Maitre Cruchot that she inherited a vast fortune, including three hundred thousand francs in income and millions in investments and gold. Yet, her thoughts were consumed by her missing cousin, Charles.Eugenie mourned her father and lost love in solitude, confiding only in her loyal servant, Nanon. Nanon’s marriage brought some joy, but Eugenie remained isolated in her grief. Despite her wealth, she felt no happiness, overshadowed by her mother’s suffering and her unfulfilled love for Charles.As time passed, the Cruchots and other suitors surrounded Eugenie, eager to win her favor and wealth. They flattered her, but she remained indifferent, still longing for Charles, who had not contacted her in seven years, deepening her emotional desolation despite her wealth.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################While events unfolded in Saumur, Charles Grandet was amassing wealth in the Indies. His initial success of six thousand dollars revealed the profitability of the slave trade. He ventured to Africa, purchasing enslaved individuals and engaging in exploitative practices, including usury and smuggling. Initially inspired by his cousin Eugenie, he became hardened and lost sight of right and wrong, ultimately severing ties with his family.In the Indies, he adopted the alias "Shepherd" to protect his identity while pursuing wealth through unscrupulous means. By 1827, he returned to Bordeaux aboard the "Marie Caroline," bringing gold-dust worth nearly two million francs. On the ship, he met Monsieur d’Aubrion, whose financial struggles stemmed from marrying into a fashionable family. The d’Aubrions were desperate for a wealthy son-in-law, and Madame d’Aubrion saw potential in Charles.Upon arriving in Paris, Charles became close to the d’Aubrion family, who sought to marry him to their unattractive daughter. Madame d’Aubrion promised to help him gain a noble title and a lucrative court position, exciting Charles and overshadowing thoughts of Eugenie.Meanwhile, Eugenie remained in Saumur, reflecting on her past with Charles. One day, she received a letter from him, which she hesitated to open. The letter revealed that Charles had moved on from their youthful love, expressing that he was free and engaged to Mademoiselle d’Aubrion, prioritizing ambition over their childhood promises. Devastated, Eugenie grappled with feelings of loss, recalling her mother’s prophetic words about suffering and resigning herself to a life of quiet anguish.Eugenie returned from the garden, deliberately avoiding the corridor filled with memories of her cousin. The day was significant due to the parish priest's visit, who urged her to consider marriage as a means to fulfill her societal obligations, especially to the poor. Overwhelmed by her unhappiness, Eugenie expressed a desire to retreat from the world and devote herself to God.Madame des Grassins interrupted with shocking news: Eugenie's cousin, Charles Grandet, had returned from the Indies and was engaged to Mademoiselle d’Aubrion. Despite her calm exterior, Eugenie felt turmoil inside. She read a letter from Madame des Grassins detailing Charles's financial troubles and the potential bankruptcy of his father, feeling betrayed as she returned the letter coldly.Later, Eugenie asked the priest if it would be a sin to remain a virgin after marriage, revealing her conflicted feelings about the institution. After he left, she spent the day alone, preparing for the evening gathering at her home. The news of Charles's return had spread, and guests were curious about her reaction. Despite their scrutiny, Eugenie maintained her composure, hiding her pain behind a polite facade.As the evening progressed, Eugenie made a surprising declaration to Monsieur de Bonfons, the president, asking him to stay. Guests interpreted this as a promise of marriage, solidifying her social position. Alone with the president, she stipulated that he must leave her free and not claim marital rights. In exchange for her hand, she asked him to pay off her uncle's creditors, offering a substantial sum for this.The president agreed, recognizing the bitterness behind her request, and hurried to Paris to settle her debts. Upon his return, he presented Eugenie with the receipts, and she expressed gratitude, acknowledging the complexities of their relationship.Meanwhile, Charles learned of Eugenie's actions and received a letter from her severing ties due to his engagement. She wished him happiness but emphasized that their love could not thrive within societal conventions. The president, now aware of the dynamics at play, felt a mix of triumph and concern.Eugenie and the president's marriage was announced, and he quickly rose in social standing, becoming a judge and later a deputy. However, his ambitions were cut short when he died shortly after his election, leaving Eugenie a widow. Despite her wealth, she lived modestly, dedicating her life to charitable works and maintaining a simple lifestyle reminiscent of her past.Eugenie's life became a testament to her resilience and compassion as she continued to support the less fortunate while grappling with emotional scars. Though pursued by suitors, including the Marquis de Froidfond, she remained steadfast in her independence, embodying a spirit that transcended societal expectations. Her story reflects the complexities of love, duty, and the pursuit of personal fulfillment amidst the constraints of her world.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################Honoré De Balzac dedicates "Pierrette" to Mademoiselle Anna Hanska, emphasizing the story's melancholy and the need to confront sorrows for someone cherished like Anna.In October 1827, sixteen-year-old Jacques Brigaut stands in a small square in Lower Provins, admiring Pierrette Lorrain's renovated house. He notices a calico curtain fluttering in an attic window and sings a Breton wedding song. When Pierrette appears, they greet each other, and Brigaut, back from Paris, expresses his desire to settle nearby. However, Pierrette urges him to flee when a noise alarms her.Alone, Pierrette reflects on her life, marked by three years of sorrow. Memories of her childhood starkly contrast with her current misery. After her father, Major Lorrain, died, her mother faced financial struggles, inheriting little and marrying a physician, which led to further ruin. Pierrette's grandparents, unable to support her, sent her to live with the Rogrons in Provins, who became her guardians after receiving a letter from them. This unexpected turn shaped her future, making her dependent on her new guardians.The narrative explores themes of social class, familial duty, and the impact of financial struggles on relationships. Pierrette's delicate health and emotional turmoil reflect broader societal issues, particularly for women in precarious situations. The story foreshadows the challenges she will face with the self-interested Rogrons, poignantly examining the intersection of personal aspirations and societal constraints in her life.Pere Rogron, an unattractive and selfish innkeeper in Provins, lives with his equally unappealing wife on an annual income of about two thousand francs from their land and inn. After selling the inn, they move into a dilapidated house inherited from Pierrette’s grandfather, old Auffray. The Rogrons have two poorly raised children, leading to their physical and emotional decline, as Rogron provides minimal support, expecting them to fend for themselves.Their daughter, Sylvie, becomes a forewoman in Paris, while her brother, Jerome-Denis, follows a similar path. Together, they save enough to buy a retail establishment called the “Family Sister.” Despite their success, both siblings are unattractive and socially inept, earning a reputation for being miserly.Sylvie effectively manages the business, while Jerome struggles with broader trade aspects. Their lives revolve around the shop, leaving them oblivious to the outside world. They dream of returning to Provins to improve their inherited property, but their aspirations are tinged with nostalgia.When the Lorrains seek to place their orphaned cousin Pierrette in their care, the siblings debate the implications. Viewing Pierrette as a threat to their inheritance, they ultimately refuse the request, prioritizing their own interests. Four years later, the Rogrons return to Provins, where their actions will significantly impact Pierrette’s life, highlighting themes of selfishness and familial duty.A provincial shopkeeper returning from Paris brings new ideas but soon succumbs to provincial life, transitioning from shopkeeper to bourgeois. This shift feels like an illness as they struggle to adapt to a quieter lifestyle. With their savings, they indulge in long-held desires through travel, politics, or hobbies like hunting and fishing. The Rogrons exemplify this as they remodel their old house into a fashionable one, aiming to emulate the wealthier bourgeois of Provins.Their renovations reflect their aspirations, with the contractor consulting them on every detail, emphasizing items once owned by local notables. The Rogrons' first year back in Provins is consumed by discussions about their home and attempts to integrate into local society, which they had previously avoided. They reconnect with former acquaintances, including the Julliards, Guepins, and Guenees, who dominate the social scene.Madame Tiphaine, the town's belle, is determined to elevate her husband, Monsieur Tiphaine, to a deputy position, using her charm to gain influence. She hosts gatherings that attract the local bourgeoisie, while the Rogrons, eager to fit in, struggle with their lack of social grace. Sylvie's attempts at card games reveal her poor manners, and her brother, Rogron, is socially awkward, leading to their exclusion from elite circles.Despite their efforts, the Rogrons are perceived as vulgar and unrefined, resulting in social ostracism. Their lavish dinners, intended to showcase their wealth, are met with indifference, leaving them isolated. Sylvie's attempts to maintain their social standing fail, prompting her to abandon their extravagant lifestyle in favor of frugality.As time passes, the Rogrons grow increasingly lonely, reminiscing about their past business and feeling the weight of isolation. They engage in mundane routines, with Rogron obsessively checking his barometer and seeking gossip from townsfolk. Their social ambitions crumble, leaving them in ennui, longing for the excitement of their former lives.In a bid to revitalize their household, Sylvie invites her cousin Pierrette Lorrain to live with them, hoping to regain social standing. However, their plans are complicated by Pierrette's unfortunate circumstances and the Rogrons' past misdeeds regarding family property. Their attempts to ingratiate themselves with the upper class backfire, leading to further alienation. The Rogrons' growing resentment towards the local elite culminates in their alignment with marginalized figures like Colonel Gouraud and lawyer Vinet, who seek to exploit the Rogrons' wealth and ignorance for their own political ambitions.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################After her grandparents were hospitalized, Pierrette Lorrain felt burdened by charity until she discovered wealthy relatives. Her childhood friend Brigaut, an apprentice cabinet-maker, offered her money for her journey to Paris, which she accepted, highlighting their deep bond.In October 1824, at eleven years old, Pierrette left Nantes, entrusted to a diligence conductor who took her to her cousins, Mademoiselle Sylvie and Rogron. Upon arrival, she was met with indifference, treated more like a package than a family member. Despite her modest attire, she radiated innocence and charm, but her cousins were more concerned with the costs of her arrival than her well-being.Sylvie and Rogron's initial surprise at Pierrette's presence quickly turned to criticism of her clothing, as they planned an expensive wardrobe for her. Having known love and freedom in Brittany, Pierrette felt a stark contrast in her new home, where her cousins' harshness stifled her spirit.As she adjusted to her new life, Pierrette faced constant scrutiny from her cousins, who prioritized their financial investment in her clothing over her happiness. Her innocent nature clashed with their coldness, leading to feelings of isolation.Despite attempts to dress her like a little lady, Pierrette's playful spirit often led to wear and tear on her clothes, resulting in further criticism. The Rogrons' harshness transformed their relationship from curiosity to tyranny, imposing rigid expectations.The arrival of Vinet and Colonel Gouraud, who sought to manipulate the Rogrons, complicated Pierrette's situation, deepening their resentment and neglect towards her. As her life in Provins progressed, she became increasingly confined and controlled, losing the joy and freedom she once enjoyed, trapped in a cycle of fear and repression.The narrative centers on Pierrette, a young girl living with her wealthy cousins, the Rogrons. During a family gathering, she is coldly dismissed by her cousin Sylvie, which deeply wounds her. Pierrette longs for the affection of her late grandmother, contrasting it with the indifference of her new guardians. Sylvie and her husband, Vinet, trivialize Pierrette's feelings, asserting that while her grandmother took her money, they will leave her theirs.As Pierrette adjusts to her new life, she faces harsh treatment from the Rogrons, who see her as a burden. They impose strict educational expectations, leading to feelings of inadequacy and despair. Her efforts to please them are met with criticism, resulting in increasing isolation and suffering, both physically and emotionally.Despite having a writing master, Pierrette struggles academically and is often punished for her perceived failures. The Rogrons' lack of kindness exacerbates her difficulties, making her feel like a servant in their home. The only warmth comes from Adele, the servant, but this comfort is stripped away when Sylvie insists that Pierrette take on household duties herself.Over time, Pierrette's health declines under the oppressive atmosphere of the Rogron household. The relentless criticism and absence of affection contribute to her deterioration. The narrative starkly contrasts Pierrette's innocent nature with her relatives' coldness, highlighting the harsh realities faced by those dependent on wealthier family members.The story also delves into the political dynamics of the time, as the Rogrons become involved in local politics through their connections with Vinet and Colonel Gouraud, leading to the establishment of a local newspaper.As Pierrette approaches her first communion, she finds solace in her faith, which offers hope amid her suffering. However, the Rogrons remain indifferent to her spiritual growth, viewing her as an obstacle to their ambitions. The arrival of Bathilde de Chargeboeuf complicates matters, as she becomes a rival for Rogron's affections and a source of jealousy for Sylvie. Ultimately, the narrative presents a bleak picture of Pierrette's life, marked by neglect and cruelty from her relatives, who prioritize their ambitions over her well-being.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################To understand the turmoil in the Rogron family after Jacques Brigaut's arrival, we must first consider his journey to Provins. Troubled by Pierrette's distress, Brigaut fled her home, shaken by her transformation from a lively child to a sickly shadow. He climbed to a vantage point to observe her house, consumed with worry and realizing his deep feelings for her.At sixteen, Brigaut was determined to improve himself for Pierrette's sake. After a brief stint in Paris, he returned to Provins and became a journeyman with Monsieur Frappier, the town's best cabinet-maker. His dedication to his trade and desire to protect Pierrette drove him to work hard, earning a modest living while keeping a watchful eye on her.As Brigaut settled into his new life, he learned about the Rogrons' dubious acquisition of Pierrette's grandfather's property and the oppressive nature of her living situation. He often saw Pierrette in the market, where she appeared burdened and unhappy. Despite his longing to approach her, he sensed her distress and chose to keep his distance.Meanwhile, Pierrette faced her cousin Sylvie's jealousy, which intensified after Brigaut's arrival. Sylvie, an old maid fixated on marrying Colonel Gouraud, grew envious of Pierrette, perceiving a potential romantic connection between her and the colonel. Sylvie's insecurities about aging led her to consult a doctor regarding marriage risks, complicating her feelings further.The dynamics within the Rogron household were tense. Sylvie's jealousy manifested in her treatment of Pierrette, whom she accused of having a secret admirer. Pierrette, innocent and unaware of the implications, remained silent, fearing repercussions. Sylvie's relentless scrutiny exacerbated Pierrette's fragile health.As the story unfolds, Sylvie's jealousy becomes increasingly irrational, leading her to plot against Pierrette, convinced that her cousin threatens her chances with the colonel. The tension culminates in a breakfast scene where accusations and distress reach a breaking point, leaving Pierrette feeling isolated and ill.Ultimately, the narrative explores themes of jealousy, innocence, and complex familial relationships, contrasting Brigaut's love for Pierrette with Sylvie's bitterness, highlighting the destructive nature of envy and its impact on those caught in its web.Colonel Gouraud ceased delivering the “Constitutionnel” to the Rogrons for a month, opting for brief visits instead. Sylvie, eager for his attention, dressed attractively and awaited his arrival with her reluctant brother, Rogron. Upon entering, Gouraud complimented Sylvie, but she feigned concern for her cousin's illness to avoid going out.Gouraud felt uneasy about his partnership with Vinet, who seemed to dominate their political efforts, sidelining him. He was also aware of Rogron's growing infatuation with Bathilde de Chargeboeuf, which made him wary of his own standing. To gauge Rogron's feelings, Gouraud mentioned his interest in Bathilde, provoking jealousy and hostility from Rogron.As Gouraud conversed with Sylvie, he suspected her intentions were influenced by Vinet, who had suggested he marry Pierrette instead. Sylvie aimed to control Pierrette and was suspicious of any connection between her cousin and the colonel. Despite Gouraud's attempts at affection, Sylvie remained calculating, wanting to test his loyalty.Sylvie's jealousy intensified as she noticed Pierrette's growing beauty and began plotting against her cousin. She sent Pierrette to dress nicely for dinner, suspecting the colonel's interest in her. Meanwhile, Bathilde and her mother arrived, with Bathilde intent on winning Rogron's affection, encouraged by Vinet to view marriage as a means to gain power.Tensions rose during the evening's card game, with Sylvie's jealousy flaring when she believed Pierrette had helped Gouraud win. After a confrontation, Pierrette fled, injuring her head. Sylvie, indifferent to her injury, focused on her frustrations with the colonel. The next day, Sylvie cruelly attributed Pierrette's injury to divine punishment for disobedience, revealing her harshness and control over her cousin. The dynamics of power, jealousy, and manipulation highlighted the domestic tyranny Sylvie exerted over Pierrette.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Pierrette endured a week of torment from her cousin Sylvie, suffering both physically and emotionally while remaining silent about Brigaut's arrival for fear of his safety. Despite her pain, thoughts of Brigaut provided solace, akin to an exile longing for home.Brigaut, aware of Pierrette's struggles, devised a plan to communicate by having her lower a letter from her window at midnight. Their mutual longing allowed them to connect despite their separation. One day in the market, Brigaut slipped a note to Pierrette, expressing his love and urging her to escape her oppressive situation and return to Brittany.Moved by Brigaut's words, Pierrette wrote back, revealing her suffering and desire for freedom. After exchanging letters, she felt renewed hope, believing Brigaut would help her escape her misery.However, Sylvie grew suspicious of Pierrette's newfound happiness and began to monitor her closely. Despite her illness, Pierrette clung to the hope of Brigaut's nightly visits. When she fainted during a gathering, the colonel, who had feelings for her, expressed concern, but Sylvie dismissed it.As tensions escalated, Sylvie discovered Pierrette's secret correspondence and confronted her. In a violent struggle, Sylvie attempted to seize Pierrette's letter, leading to a brutal confrontation that left Pierrette injured. Just as Sylvie's cruelty peaked, Pierrette's grandmother arrived, alerted by Brigaut. The old woman intervened, taking Pierrette away from the oppressive environment.Filled with anguish upon seeing her granddaughter's condition, Pierrette's grandmother vowed to seek medical help and take action against the Rogrons. Meanwhile, Brigaut sought a doctor for Pierrette's recovery. As she recuperated, her grandmother provided comfort and support, promising a better life for them both, underscoring themes of love, resilience, and the quest for freedom from oppression.Monsieur Martener visits Monsieur Tiphaine to address Pierrette's mistreatment by the Rogrons, which has caused her severe illness. The feud between the Vinet and Tiphaine factions in Provins intensifies as the Rogrons spread damaging rumors about the Tiphaine family. Tiphaine views Pierrette's situation as a chance to politically undermine the Rogrons.Tiphaine consults notary Auffray and Martener to strategize on legally removing Pierrette from the Rogrons' guardianship. They plan a Family Council to appoint Auffray as her guardian, aiming for her release. News of Pierrette's distress incites public outrage in Provins.As tensions escalate, Vinet, the Rogrons' lawyer, learns of potential criminal charges against them. He manipulates the Rogrons into agreeing to a marriage contract with Mademoiselle de Chargeboeuf to gain public favor and distract from the allegations. Court proceedings commence, with the Tiphaines and their supporters rallying around Pierrette, while the Rogrons defend their actions.Pierrette is moved to a safer environment under Auffray's care, where doctors confirm her injuries result from the Rogrons' abuse. A court ruling prevents her return to their home, and despite Vinet's attempts to discredit her, the Family Council decides to remove Rogron as her guardian.As the legal battle continues, Pierrette's health deteriorates. She expresses love for her grandmother and friend Brigaut, forgiving her cousins. Tragically, she passes away, leading to a public funeral that the Rogrons avoid due to backlash.Brigaut, devastated, vows to seek justice. The legal proceedings against the Rogrons collapse with Pierrette's death, while Vinet exploits the situation for political gain, critiquing societal indifference and moral decay. In the aftermath, Vinet gains political power, and Brigaut becomes a soldier, haunted by memories of Pierrette.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**The Vicar of Tours: Summary****Dedication**  De Balzac dedicates "The Vicar of Tours" to sculptor David, emphasizing the enduring nature of David's work compared to the uncertain permanence of his own literary efforts.**I**  In autumn 1826, Abbe Birotteau, a short, corpulent priest nearing sixty, is caught in the rain while returning home from a friend's house. Despite his discomfort, he feels uplifted by the prospect of being nominated as canon at the cathedral of Saint-Gatien, a long-held ambition that promises a comfortable life filled with good food and clean clothes.Birotteau lives with Mademoiselle Gamard, an old maid who boards priests. He has long envied the apartment of his deceased friend, Abbe Chapeloud, who created a beautiful living space filled with books. After Chapeloud's death, Birotteau inherits his library and furniture, which brings him joy but also guilt.As Birotteau settles into his new life, he enjoys Mademoiselle Gamard's meticulous care. However, he soon notices a shift in her treatment. Small signs of neglect, like a lack of fire in his room, lead him to suspect that she harbors ill will against him.Despite his good nature, Birotteau struggles to understand her hostility. He reflects on their relationship, realizing he has taken her attentions for granted. Mademoiselle Gamard, feeling slighted by his social engagements, withdraws her kindness, creating tension between them.Birotteau's longing for the canonry intensifies, blinding him to the conflict with Mademoiselle Gamard. His naivety prevents him from recognizing her resentment, stemming from his social aspirations and failure to appreciate her efforts. The narrative explores themes of envy, ambition, and the fragility of happiness, illustrating how misunderstandings can lead to profound personal conflicts.The following morning, Birotteau awoke thinking of his potential canonry, momentarily forgetting the previous night's ominous signs. He rang for his servant, Marianne, but when she did not come, Abbe Troubert entered after knocking. Troubert, a tall, serious man, contrasted sharply with the jovial Birotteau. Their monthly visits were routine, and Troubert expressed concern over Birotteau's health and prospects for the canonry, unaware of Birotteau's naivety regarding Troubert's ambitions.The two men were opposites: Troubert's somber nature clashed with Birotteau's cheerfulness. Troubert, respected for his austere presence, was expected to be promoted to vicar-general, while Birotteau's robust health made him a less appealing candidate for advancement.Abbe Chapeloud quietly opposed Troubert's rise but maintained a facade of respect towards him. After Mademoiselle Gamard declared war on Birotteau, Troubert visited him to borrow a book. Birotteau, pleased to help, felt grateful for Troubert's kindness, especially since it meant he would have a fire to dress by.As they descended for breakfast, Mademoiselle Gamard scolded Birotteau for bringing books into the dining room, souring the atmosphere. Birotteau struggled to engage in conversation, ultimately falling silent as his attempts at small talk were met with disdain from Gamard, leaving him feeling isolated.Gamard embodied the archetype of an old maid, marked by bitterness and unfulfilled desires. Despite her harshness, Birotteau remained oblivious to her malice, caught in a cycle of trying to please her. After breakfast, he sought solace in the church, but upon returning home late for dinner, he discovered that Gamard had manipulated the clock to ensure he missed the meal, exemplifying her vindictive nature. Birotteau's life was increasingly overshadowed by Gamard's petty tyranny.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Eight days into the story, Abbe Birotteau discovers a plot against him by Mademoiselle Gamard, who has harbored resentment for months. Initially oblivious to her malicious intentions, Birotteau becomes tormented as unsettling incidents reveal he is under her scrutiny. His once peaceful life spirals into despair as he perceives Gamard's vindictiveness as a personal attack.Feeling isolated and ashamed to confide in friends, Birotteau seeks help from Abbe Troubert, who dismisses his concerns as trivial, attributing Gamard's behavior to health issues, which deepens Birotteau's helplessness.To escape, Birotteau plans a short stay at Madame de Listomere's country house, unaware this plays into Gamard's hands. At the Alouette, he is confronted by Monsieur Caron, Gamard's lawyer, who warns that his absence could lead to legal complications regarding his living arrangements. Confused and distressed, Birotteau returns to his friends, who urge him to stand firm against Gamard.Madame de Listomere and others analyze the situation, suspecting Troubert covets Birotteau's apartment. They encourage him to resist Gamard's influence, but Birotteau's naivety leads him to sign a document relinquishing his rights to his belongings in Gamard's house, believing it to be a mere formality. This act seals his fate, as he later learns Gamard intends to keep his possessions as compensation for the difference in board prices.Upon returning to confront Gamard, Birotteau is met with her triumphant demeanor and realizes the extent of his loss. Troubert has taken over his former apartment, and Gamard claims his furniture as part of their agreement. Devastated, Birotteau seeks solace at Madame de Listomere's, where friends promise to help him reclaim his dignity and possessions.Mademoiselle Salomon, a devoted friend, takes Birotteau back to the Alouette, where he struggles to comprehend the betrayal. His friends, including Monsieur de Bourbonne, strategize a legal response against Gamard, believing they can expose her deceit. However, the local lawyer advises against pursuing the case, suggesting Birotteau's actions may be seen as ingratitude.Despite this, Birotteau's friends remain committed to supporting him, recognizing the social implications of the conflict against Gamard and Troubert. As the legal battle unfolds, they gather evidence of the value of Birotteau's possessions, which far exceed what he owed Gamard, illustrating the complexities of human relationships and motivations.A few days after hostilities began, Baron de Listomere received concerning news about his potential retirement from the Navy. Seeking clarification, he approached the minister, who dismissed his fears. However, further inquiries revealed a document confirming his retirement was pending signature. The baron sought help from his uncle, a deputy, who warned that his political views had made him unpopular, particularly with the influential Abbe Troubert. He advised the baron to reconcile with Troubert to secure his career.Three days later, the baron returned home, anxious about the family's support for Birotteau, whom he considered a liability. He consulted Monsieur de Bourbonne, who suggested distancing themselves from Birotteau and appeasing Troubert. The baroness then asked Birotteau to withdraw his lawsuit against Mademoiselle Gamard and leave her house for family interests. Distressed, Birotteau reluctantly agreed but insisted on retrieving a portrait of Abbe Chapeloud.Madame de Listomere promised to negotiate with Mademoiselle Gamard for the portrait. Shortly after, Gamard fell ill and died, leaving her fortune to Troubert, which led to gossip blaming Birotteau for her death. Madame de Listomere visited Troubert to negotiate peace, presenting Birotteau's relinquishment of claims. Troubert, feigning indifference, refused to part with the portrait, claiming it was a cherished memento.Gamard's death further isolated Birotteau, who was appointed curate of Saint-Symphorien, effectively exiling him. Meanwhile, Troubert ascended to Bishop of Troyes, consolidating his power. After Madame de Listomere's death, Troubert contested Birotteau's annuity, threatening the baron's career. Ultimately, Birotteau's life deteriorated amid illness and despair, while Troubert's influence grew unchecked, highlighting the consequences of ecclesiastical politics in provincial France.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. ###################################################################################################################### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########################################################**The Two Brothers**  *By Honoré De Balzac*  *Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley*  **Dedication**  To Monsieur Charles Nodier, a member of the French Academy. This book explores the hidden injustices of domestic life, where divine chance often replaces human justice. It reflects on the decline of paternal authority and the consequences of feminine weakness, urging society to return to the moral foundations of marriage and family.---**Chapter I**  In 1792, the townspeople of Issoudun viewed Dr. Rouget as malicious, believing he made his beautiful wife, Madame Rouget, unhappy. Their son, Jean-Jacques, was born first, followed by their daughter Agathe a decade later, whose unexpected arrival strained family dynamics. The Rougets were connected to the wealthy Descoings family, who profited from the wool trade. Descoings’ brother sought fortune in Paris as a grocer but ultimately failed.In 1792, Descoings was imprisoned due to political missteps during the Revolution, leading to his execution, which shocked the town and overshadowed the death of poet André Chenier. After Descoings’ demise, Madame Rouget’s health deteriorated, worsened by her husband’s neglect and their son’s indifference. Agathe was sent to Paris to live with her uncle, but her arrival coincided with Descoings’ imprisonment, leaving him unable to care for her.Fearing she would never see Agathe again, Madame Rouget fell ill. Meanwhile, Agathe’s beauty attracted Bridau, a secretary in the ministry, who married her with Dr. Rouget’s approval. After inheriting the Descoings’ wealth, Dr. Rouget led a debauched life following his wife’s death in 1799, leaving Jean-Jacques unprepared for adulthood.Agathe, resembling her mother, was a devoted wife and mother, but her ignorance of life’s complexities led to misfortunes. Bridau, loyal to Napoleon, worked tirelessly in the ministry, earning a substantial salary. Their life in Paris was marked by domestic bliss until Bridau’s overwork led to his death in 1808, just before a significant promotion.After Bridau’s death, Agathe received a pension and scholarships for her sons but remained estranged from her family in Issoudun. Despite warnings from her godmother, Madame Hochon, Agathe did not contest her father’s will, which deprived her of her rightful inheritance. Instead, she and Madame Descoings pooled their resources, living modestly on a combined income.Madame Descoings, now sixty-five, maintained her youthful appearance and sought to cheer Agathe, often taking her to the theater. However, she harbored a secret about her grandson, Bixiou, whom she raised in secrecy. Agathe, devoted to her children, declined to remarry, but her lack of authority as a mother led to significant challenges.Madame Descoings’ gambling addiction worsened their financial situation, as she used household funds to support her lottery habit. As debts mounted, Agathe, unaware of the extent of the problem, continued to trust her aunt. Eventually, Madame Descoings confessed her financial troubles to Agathe, who, without reproach, took drastic measures to pay off the debts, selling possessions and cutting expenses.This chapter sets the stage for the unfolding drama of familial relationships, financial struggles, and the consequences of choices made within societal expectations and personal ambitions, highlighting the complexities of love, loyalty, and the often-hidden burdens within families.The rue Mazarin, particularly between rue Guenegard and rue de Seine, is one of Paris's dreariest areas, dominated by Cardinal Mazarin's college and library. Here, Madame Bridau, a widow, lives on the third floor of a dimly lit building. Opposite her, the Institute trains aspiring artists, known as "rapins," who undergo rigorous examinations for prestigious prizes, though this artistic hub has since moved to the Palais des Beaux-Arts.Madame Bridau's modest apartment, furnished with only essentials, features small gardens despite police regulations. She chose this location for its affordability (400 francs a year), proximity to her sons' school, and familiarity. The interior reflects her frugal lifestyle, with a sparsely decorated dining room and salon, save for a portrait of her late husband and a vibrant painting of Napoleon.Since her husband's death, Madame Bridau has become increasingly disheveled and consumed by grief, neglecting her appearance and home. Her devotion to her late husband is evident in her preservation of his belongings, including a pen labeled as his last. With financial struggles looming, she worries about her children, Philippe and Joseph, despite receiving some support from the Emperor.Nearby, her aunt Madame Descoings also faces financial difficulties but provides assistance to Madame Bridau. The two widows share a close bond, often playing cards with friends from the ministry. Madame Descoings dotes on Philippe and Joseph, indulging them with treats and small gifts, while Madame Bridau restricts her spending to save for her children's future.Philippe, the elder son, resembles his mother and exhibits a bold demeanor, preferring physical activities over academics. In contrast, Joseph is more introverted and artistic, struggling with cleanliness and often scolded by his mother. Despite their differences, both boys are loved by their mother, though she tends to favor Philippe.Joseph's interest in art is ignited when he sees a student drawing a caricature at the Institute. He becomes enamored with the artistic environment and begins visiting sculptor Chaudet's studio, who recognizes his potential. However, Madame Bridau is distressed by her son's aspirations, fearing he will face hardship as an artist.Despite her objections, Joseph pursues art, encouraged by Madame Descoings. Meanwhile, Philippe, inspired by military grandeur, secretly petitions the Emperor to join the army. He is accepted into the Imperial Lyceum and quickly rises through the ranks, earning accolades for his bravery.As the political landscape shifts following Napoleon's return from exile, both brothers face challenges. Joseph's scholarship is revoked, and he is determined to make a living as an artist, while Philippe, now a captain, returns home to find his family in financial ruin. Their contrasting paths highlight their differing personalities and the complexities of their mother's affections.Madame Bridau's preference for Philippe grows as he embodies the heroic ideals she admires, while Joseph's artistic pursuits cause her anxiety. Despite this, she remains a loving mother, struggling to understand her younger son’s aspirations. The family dynamics reflect broader themes of ambition, sacrifice, and the pursuit of identity amidst societal expectations.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################In 1816, Joseph Bridau transformed his attic into an art studio with support from his mother, Agathe, and financial backing from Madame Descoings. Both widows viewed painting as a legitimate profession. Joseph decorated the studio and continued his studies under Gros and Schinner. Meanwhile, political unrest in France led to conspiracies and riots near the Chamber of Deputies. Philippe, Joseph's brother, became involved in these conspiracies, was arrested, and later released, but his military career was jeopardized when he was placed back on active duty.Agathe worried for Philippe's safety as he frequented disreputable cafes, while the Grecian sages, regular visitors to her home, advised sending him abroad. Despite their concerns, Philippe was determined to serve France and eventually joined General Lallemand in the United States, leading to the disastrous Champ d’Asile venture. Agathe invested ten thousand francs to support him, but upon his return in 1819, Philippe was a changed man, hardened by his experiences and struggling with alcoholism.Eager to support her son, Agathe bought him new clothes and indulged his drinking habits, despite her suspicions about his character. Philippe's tales of woe masked his selfishness and brutality, traits that had developed during his time away. Agathe remained blind to his flaws, believing he was a victim of circumstance.Back in Paris, Philippe's behavior alarmed Agathe and Madame Descoings. They faced financial struggles, and Agathe suggested selling their furniture to support him. Philippe assured them he would find work, but he continued gambling and drinking, worsening their financial strain.Philippe's social life revolved around old army comrades and gambling, becoming increasingly reckless. He formed a bond with Giroudeau, a former dragoon, who introduced him to Paris nightlife. Philippe's ambitions shifted towards pursuing a relationship with Mariette, a young dancer, while still dreaming of political influence.Despite his failures, Philippe sought to leverage his past for personal gain, planning to demand accountability for the funds raised for the Champ d’Asile. His mother and Madame Descoings, desperate for stability, agreed to secure him a cashier position at a new newspaper, believing it would alleviate their financial woes. As Philippe's situation improved, he promised to contribute to the household, renewing Agathe's faith in him. However, Joseph, feeling overshadowed by Philippe's return, resolved to support himself independently, highlighting the contrasting paths of the two brothers amidst their family's struggles.Three months later, Colonel Philippe lived extravagantly without supporting his mother and Madame Descoings, who hesitated to remind him of his obligations. His mother believed he was content. Meanwhile, Mariette, a ballet dancer at the Porte-Saint-Martin, gained fame through Philippe and his friends, who were captivated by her.Bixiou informed Agathe and Madame Descoings of Philippe's infatuation with Mariette, shocking them as they viewed stage women as morally corrupt. Joseph dismissed the idea that Philippe would waste money on her. Bixiou warned that Mariette might attract wealthier suitors, including an ambassador.Driven by greed, Philippe proposed marriage to Mariette, but she declined, seeking more lucrative prospects at the Grand Opera. As Philippe distanced himself from his family, he indulged in a hedonistic lifestyle, mingling with performers and enjoying Parisian nightlife. However, when Mariette found success, Philippe's finances worsened, leading him to embezzle funds from his job.After Mariette left for London, Philippe returned home, grappling with despair over his inability to maintain his lifestyle. He confided in Joseph about his suicidal thoughts stemming from his embezzlement and impending disgrace. Alarmed, Joseph urged him to reconsider, suggesting that repayment could avert dishonor.Philippe fled, leaving his family distressed. Agathe and Madame Descoings were devastated upon learning of his actions, fearing for his life. Despite their anguish, Philippe concealed his theft by claiming he had kept the money for safekeeping.He then visited a gambling house, initially winning but ultimately losing everything. Returning home, he feigned distress, and his family welcomed him back with relief. Agathe, still shaken, begged him to promise not to harm himself, and Philippe exploited their concern.In the following days, Agathe visibly aged from worry, realizing their financial situation was dire. Madame Descoings suggested consulting Desroches, a lawyer, to manage the fallout from Philippe's actions. Desroches devised a plan to protect Philippe’s reputation, framing his theft as a result of misplaced trust.Philippe resumed a life of leisure, gambling, and drinking, while Joseph focused on his art, gaining recognition. Agathe remained preoccupied with Philippe, oscillating between hope and fear regarding his future. Desroches assured them that Philippe would soon be reinstated in the army, but warned that his gambling habits could jeopardize this opportunity.As Philippe continued his reckless lifestyle, Joseph noticed discrepancies in his finances, suspecting Philippe was stealing from him. Madame Descoings confirmed similar suspicions. They resolved to catch Philippe in the act, believing he needed to be confronted about his behavior. Despite their efforts, Philippe's charm allowed him to evade accountability, deepening their struggles. Joseph worried about the impact on their mother and their future, closing the chapter with foreboding about Philippe's potential downfall.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################Despite Philippe's cautious gambling, he sometimes found himself "cleaned out." Desperate for his nightly stake of ten francs, he resorted to stealing from his family, including his mother Agathe and Madame Descoings. Agathe, heartbroken, recognized that his thefts indicated a deeper moral failing. After a troubling dream where Philippe robbed her, she urged him to ask for money instead of stealing. However, within two weeks, Agathe was left with nothing and considered finding work to support herself.As Agathe's health deteriorated from worry, Philippe continued his reckless lifestyle, neglecting his appearance and becoming increasingly debauched. Madame Descoings urged him to buy new clothes, but Philippe dismissed her concerns, claiming he had no money. He had even mortgaged his military pension to pay off gambling debts.On Christmas Eve, Philippe planned to steal from Madame Descoings, who had saved money for lottery tickets. He broke into her apartment while she was out, found twenty napoleons hidden in her mattress, and left without raising suspicion. With this money, he gambled and experienced a lucky streak, winning substantial amounts at the gaming house. However, his greed led him to ignore his own gambling rules, and he ultimately lost everything.Meanwhile, Agathe and Madame Descoings awaited Philippe's return for Christmas dinner. When he finally arrived, he was drunk, and tensions escalated as Agathe confronted him about his behavior. Philippe, in a fit of rage, dismissed their concerns, even as Madame Descoings suffered from the shock of losing her savings.The next day, Madame Descoings learned that her lottery ticket had won three million francs, but she was devastated to discover that Philippe had stolen her money before she could pay for her stake. This revelation caused her to suffer a stroke, leaving her incapacitated. Agathe and Joseph were left to deal with the fallout of Philippe's actions, which had brought ruin upon their family.As Madame Descoings lay ill, Philippe returned home, oblivious to the chaos he had caused. Agathe confronted him about his theft and the pain inflicted on their family. Philippe, however, remained indifferent, believing he was justified. The confrontation escalated into violence, leading to Philippe being restrained due to his delirium.In the aftermath, Madame Descoings passed away, leaving Agathe with a meager annuity. Philippe, now estranged from his family, continued his reckless lifestyle, showing no remorse. Agathe, heartbroken and defeated, prayed for her son, realizing that her motherhood had become a source of deep sorrow.By February 1822, Madame Bridau had moved into a modest attic room above her former apartment, where her son Joseph aimed to make her comfortable after Philippe's departure. Joseph decorated the room and added a double door for elegance. To manage expenses, he took Agathe to dine daily at a table-d’hote frequented by respectable patrons, costing nearly a hundred francs a month. Concerned about costs, Agathe secured a job as an assistant in a lottery office, earning seven hundred francs a year, which required long hours.Despite her new routine, Agathe found solace in its stability. She prepared breakfast for Joseph and kept his studio tidy, though she struggled with the artistic discussions around her. Their bond deepened through small acts of care, even as they remained distant regarding art. One day, Agathe expressed concern for Philippe, prompting Joseph to visit him. Philippe, now a cashier for a newspaper, invited Joseph to dinner, but Joseph declined, prioritizing his mother.As Philippe posed for a portrait, Agathe secretly observed, filled with hope for their family. However, Philippe's behavior raised Joseph's suspicions, particularly regarding his finances. After a lavish dinner, Agathe's joy turned to worry when Philippe's reckless spending became evident. During another sitting, Philippe boasted of his ambitions but was evasive about his finances.One evening, Philippe stole a valuable painting from Joseph's studio, believing it to be a copy. When Joseph discovered the theft, he was devastated, and Agathe was heartbroken, realizing the extent of Philippe's betrayal. Her despair deepened upon learning of Philippe's deteriorating condition and need for medical care. Despite Giroudeau's pleas for financial assistance, Agathe refused to help Philippe, feeling betrayed and exhausted.Months later, Agathe encountered Philippe on the street, now a shadow of his former self, recently released from a hospital. Overcome with maternal instinct, she gave him money, despite knowing it would likely be squandered. Her health declined as she grappled with the pain of her son's choices.When news broke of Philippe's involvement in a conspiracy, Agathe fainted, fearing for his life. Joseph sought legal advice from Desroches, who advised them to secure their family's inheritance from Philippe's influence over their wealthy uncle, Jean-Jacques Rouget. Agathe wrote to her godmother, Madame Hochon, seeking help, but learned that Rouget was unlikely to assist. Desroches urged Agathe to travel to Issoudun to protect her family's interests, prompting her to reflect on the changes in Issoudun and her estrangement from her brother.Issoudun, one of France's oldest cities, has a rich history and significant commerce, highlighted by archaeological finds like a fifth-century basilica on a Roman temple site. Its name, derived from "Dun," suggests Celtic origins and a long-standing settlement. Historically a wool trade center, Issoudun had a population of around 16,000 in the fourteenth century, but this has declined to about 12,000 today, including vine-growers from surrounding areas.This decline is largely due to residents' resistance to change, particularly in viticulture, where outdated practices hinder quality wine production. The local bourgeoisie, clinging to medieval traditions, resisted military presence that could have revitalized the economy, reflecting broader regional trends seen in towns like Bourges.Issoudun's topography features a canal and fertile meadows, but the social fabric has deteriorated, lacking the class distinctions that once enriched community life. The absence of nobility and military presence has led to a homogenous society, with a local government of familiar faces becoming ineffective, further entrenching the town's inertia.In the early 19th century, young men in Issoudun formed the "Knights of Idleness," led by Maxence Gilet, a charismatic figure with a tumultuous past. The group engaged in mischievous pranks that terrorized the town, with Max, rumored to be the illegitimate son of a local official, endearing himself to the common folk despite bourgeois contempt.Under Max's leadership, the Knights executed elaborate pranks, often involving townspeople in humorous chaos, leading to the resignation of the sub-prefect due to relentless harassment. This chapter illustrates the interplay between social stagnation, youthful rebellion, and the decline of a once-thriving community, highlighting how the past influences Issoudun's present dynamics.############################################################# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############################################################As Joseph and Madame Bridau traveled from Orleans to Issoudun, the Knights of Idleness played a prank on an old Spaniard, Pere Fario, by hoisting his cart onto the embankment. They later returned to Mere Cognette’s for food and laughter, eager to see Fario's reaction.The Knights enjoyed each other's company through hunting and leisure, though not every night involved mischief. Max was particularly admired by Madame Hochon's grandsons, Francois and Baruch, who viewed him as a cousin and relied on him for financial support and social experiences.During a gathering at Mere Cognette’s, Baruch informed Max that Madame Bridau and her son were coming to Issoudun, which could threaten Max's interests in the property of Jean-Jacques Rouget, an old bachelor connected to Flore Brazier, Rouget's servant-mistress. Although Max initially dismissed the concern, he felt unsettled, knowing Rouget's wealth was tied to Flore, and the arrival of the legitimate heirs could jeopardize his plans.Francois and another friend speculated that if Rouget revoked his will in favor of Flore, it could impact Max's future. However, Max expressed confidence in his relationship with Flore and his ability to manage the situation. He proposed that the Knights assist him in dealing with the Bridaus, clarifying that he did not intend to harm them but wanted to secure his position.The Knights rallied around Max, pledging their support and loyalty. They toasted to Flore and dismissed the Bridaus, reflecting their relaxed morals. Max's leadership among the Knights was strategic; he aimed to cultivate their loyalty for future support in reclaiming his status.As the night ended, Max returned home, contemplating the implications of the Bridaus' arrival. He realized Flore had not informed him about her family, which could threaten their financial future. His affection for Flore was intertwined with her potential inheritance from Rouget, and he felt an urgency to act before the Bridaus could secure their claim.The narrative then shifts to Flore's backstory. Discovered by Doctor Rouget as a child, she was taken in due to her beauty and innocence, receiving a better life than she would have had with her uncle. Under Rouget's care, Flore enjoyed education and luxuries that distinguished her from other girls in Issoudun.Despite gossip surrounding their relationship, Flore thrived, though townspeople often misinterpreted Rouget's intentions. Her life changed dramatically when Rouget, nearing death, refused to leave her any inheritance, believing her beauty would ensure her future wealth.This chapter highlights the intertwined lives of Max, Flore, and the Rouget family, setting the stage for the conflicts and dynamics that will unfold as they navigate their relationships and ambitions in Issoudun.Jean-Jacques Rouget, at thirty-seven, was timid and socially awkward, particularly around women, and did not mourn his father as Flore Brazier did, having gained materially from him. His father had hoped to marry him off but hesitated, fearing a wife's influence. Instead, he instilled avarice in Jean-Jacques, who secretly loved Flore, the only woman in his life.Flore began to see Jean-Jacques differently after noticing his silence. Over three weeks, their relationship evolved, culminating in a conversation where he expressed his desire for her to stay. Intrigued by his affection, Flore found him less unattractive. Their dynamic shifted as Jean-Jacques, emboldened by his feelings, offered her a place in his life, leading to mutual understanding.As time passed, Flore took charge of the household, surpassing Jean-Jacques in management and influence. She learned to cook and manage finances, becoming indispensable. Their lives settled into a routine, with Jean-Jacques growing happier under her care. However, Flore's beauty attracted attention, and she eventually fell for Maxence Gilet, a penniless officer, straining her relationship with Jean-Jacques.Flore's passion for Max led to tension, as she expressed frustrations with Jean-Jacques's perceived stinginess and argued for Max to move in. Despite his reluctance, Jean-Jacques agreed, hoping to maintain her affection. Once Max moved in, dynamics shifted again. Flore adopted a more refined lifestyle, complicating the interplay of love, power, and dependency among the three. Jean-Jacques found companionship in Max, while Flore reveled in her newfound status and influence, illustrating the evolution of relationships shaped by love and social expectations in a provincial setting.When Madame Bridau returned to Issoudun to safeguard her inheritance, Jean-Jacques Rouget had lapsed into a semi-vegetative state, motivated solely by his affection for Flore, which led him to comply with her every demand.Max, Rouget's companion, had adorned his apartment with provincial luxuries. One morning, Flore found Max asleep after a late night and learned that Rouget's family was coming to claim their inheritance. Concerned, Max advised Flore to act as if she hadn’t heard the news.A commotion erupted when Fario, a local Spaniard, reported his cart missing. Max feigned indifference but soon gathered a crowd, humorously suggesting the cart had magically ascended the tower, which led to laughter at Fario's expense. Eventually, Max and his friends destroyed the cart, further enraging Fario and complicating Max's situation with Flore.As tensions rose, Francois informed Max of the Bridaus' imminent arrival. Max opted for manipulation rather than open opposition. Meanwhile, Flore, upset by the news, took her frustration out on Rouget, withholding affection and leaving him confused.Flore's harsh treatment stemmed from her fear of losing her position. She berated Rouget about his sister's visit, claiming it would disrupt their lives. Rouget, bewildered, insisted he knew nothing of their arrival, blaming Madame Hochon for the news.Max advised Rouget to welcome his family to avoid scandal, emphasizing the importance of appearances.
